THE RED PAGE.
FROM PHILISTIA.
( With apologies to a host of perjervid appreciatovs.)
I have read what I could in my short life—
Run wild, and had plenty of rope :
I have tasted impossible court life
With ingenious Anthony Hope';
With old Verne I have fathomed old ocean,
Been turned into goose-flesh by Poe;
And (with singular zest and devotion)
Been faithless with Manon Lrscaut.
With writers inspired—and pedantic—
I have wandered ’mid all sorts of scenes . . .
Now (the problem is driving me frantic),
Can you tell me what Meredith means ?
I have sampled sweet savours with Herrick
In meads where no moralists are;
And in bed (with no mate but enteric)
I have done the wrong thing with Faublas.
I have walked in the purple with Dickens,
And in grey with the bore Adam Bede ;
And by Kant, after tentative pickings,
Been quite unaccountably treed.
But I’ve struggled devoutly with Beauchamp
From the days of my earliest teens :
Ah ! pity poor sduls, ye who teach ’em,
And tell us what Meredith means !
With Crebillon (a regular bad ’un)
I’ve wooed, with my tongue in my cheek ;
I have looked on bare bosoms with Braddon,
With Petronius I’ve hugged the antique.
I have hobnobbed with Kipling’s wild creatures,
And quipped with his highly-strung dames ;
I’m acquaint with the sins and the features
Of the heroes of G. P. R. James.
I have stood with young eyes all a-goggle
While Grammonthas thrown down the screens;
But say, Gods of Blurr and of Boggle,
Can you tell me what Meredith means ?
I have snuggled to comfort with Maupin
While the minutes have passed like a song,
Gently lulled by rich music of Chopin
Played by Mabel, who thought the book
“wrong.”
I have hunted the Latest Sensations
With decadents of various schools ;
I have yawned through the dull lucubrations
Of a hundred variety of fools.
If I’ve sinned —Lord ! the penalty’s horrid
As no non-Meredithian weens . . .
Ho ! wise Imps of the Nether-gloom Torrid ! —•
Can you tell me what Meredith means?
Gath, Aug. 16.
E.M
The Forest.
IN the clearest-c.ut type and on the whitest
of papers *“ chosen ” words are still to the
mind blurred and stained with the associa-
tions of the past:
“ not in utter nakedness,
But trailing clouds of glory'do they come.”
And of these “picked” ones the word “forest ”is
to me especially rich in glories ; shadow and airy
colour ; savage spell and delicate romance ; lost
love and sacred parting. And only from a race of
tree-lovers could have sprung my passion for the
breath and murmur a blowing forest land.
Through, what of the aead survives within I seem
able to trace my way back to a far-off time wiien I
dwelt with the oak and the beech and knew their
secrets. In my earliest dreams my ideal pia.ve of
refuge was a tiny hu‘t hidden in the f hi aged
depths of a mighty tree. There, in the daytime,
I watched the animals playing in the hot sun-
shine, and in the night trembled with delighted
fear at the winds and the noises that haunt the
wild heart of an immense woodland. From this
leafy hermitage my tales of adventure excited the
admiration and the envy of my less fortunate
companions. A shallow sandy cave, a hollow
hedge at twilight, a firelit tower of loose bricks,
were the favorite crouching places of this small
narrator of forest happenings. How small and
distant he is—this narrator: it is a farewell, a
detachment, to look back at him !
Some German fairy tales, read by the winter
fireside, greatly influenced the growth of his
enchanted .forest. The murmurs of the haunted
Rhine filled its lonely roads ; black robber castles
grew from blacker rocks ; fearful gnomes guarded
.unearthly treasures beneath torn and fantastic
and'from afar came the rush and the cry of
the demon hunt. To this solitary boy, listening
intently to the wind’s efforts to force the glowing
room, these homely folk-tales were full of charm
and sweetness.
Later, his mind opened to the wonder and
terror of ,the blood-stained woods of the Ohio. He
was the noble Indian, a statue of living bronze,
uttering the melancholy cry of the whipp-o-will to
summon the pale-face maiden to the forest tryst.
How delicate, how reticent, was the courtship—
just a clashing of the hands and a bending of the
proud, eagle-plumed head to the lovely face look-
ing up to him in the moonlight. Or he was the
lone scout and Indian fighter tracking the wily
red-skin with marvellous insight; rescuing whites
from the burning stake, or smoking a quiet pipe
before a fire hidden in a cave. Ah, the romance
of the dark and bloody‘ground of old Kentucky !
Where are ye now —Delaware, Pawnee Loup,
Iroquois, Wyandotte, Mohican—with whom Eng-
lish youths were wont to do desperate battle m
the days of long ago, when Zenda was not and
Mars unknown !
Pierce Eagan’s Robin Hood—a book breathing
the forest air—brought the boy back to England
and the oak. Th’is was life: in the pure air of
morning, under the broad summer leaves, to feel
the blood stir with the sparkle of steel through the
green, to chase king’s deer through thorn and
thicket, to close round the evening blaze at the
trysting tree ! And still in poignant dreams the
man’s heart becomes a boy’s again, and echoes
witfi the long-drawn sweetness of Robin’s bugle-
call. Love walked under the roof of forest boughs.
'How did Maid Marion die, and where was she
buried ? Keats, with a tang 'of saßness, 1 tells us
tflat Robin and Marion are sleeping in the under-
wood. Surely something of my being lies under
the stone whose place was marked by the wavering
arrow shot from the fatal shade of the fair
Kirkleys.
With Evangelineithe boy passed away to “the
forest primeval of murmuring pines and hem-
locks.” The writer counts it a glad thing that he
read the tale in youth. At that poetic time pure
beauty easily slips from the encumbering form and
becomes a possession for ever. Many were the
voyages taken with the gentle Evangeline on the
moonlit forest flood.
* * * *
As time went on „the youth suddenly found the
dream-forest a reality. There is, is England, a
certain river called the Ribble, sweet and musical
as becomes its name, and on a long slope of it
flourished for a brief season the green bowers of
love. For the first time to be alone with the
beloved in the shade of old Sympathetic trees, with
shafts of golden light piercing the leafy roof, and
long blue shadows lying on the keen sward—the
joy of these can make paradise grove out of the
barest of straggling woodlands ! Again the forest
became a dream. It was steeped in Celtic twi-
light. On the grey uplands, were tombs of for-
gotten kings. Immemorial ruins—the heart was
touched with a great regret. Armour-clad knights
rode through its shadowy ways. When colour
slanted between the mighty boles it was wet,
vivid, jewelled, but for the most part the light
was grey and forlorn, yet full of a pensive’ charm.
One lonely knight wandered and lingered in the
long green glades ; at times, to his dreaming eyes,
the forest flowers were a drift of fays—exquisite,
white-limbed ; his lance, keen and strong, flung
his foes to the dust ; and his talk at the castle fire-
side was of Arthur and Merlin, Launcelot and
Guinevere.
But Rosalind’s merry laugh dispelled all this
wan light and sadness. The sunshine poured
down on the broad-spreading oaks ; the white
morning vapours vanished in the golden quivering
light : and down the forest alleys strolled those
loving lords of loyal service fleeting the careless
time.
Orlando,_ Rosalind, Celia—beautiful names,
beautiful lives—joyous creatures playing with
lowly occupations ; forester, shepherd, shep-
herdess, marring trees with love-songs on their
bark, hanging odes upon hawthorns, and elegies
upon brambles. The whole forest philosophy is in
Amiens’ little song, “Under the greenwood tree.”
The courtiers passed to the court; but the youth,
with that love of solitude and regret so character-
istic of this somewhat sickly spiritual time, sought
out with Jaques their abandoned cave, not with-
out a sigh for the silken life gone from the forest
bounds.
* * * *
The huge peace of antique woodlands has often
been desired by the anguished ones of the earth.
Even Christ fled the full light of .the passover
moon and suffered in the dim shadows of the
olives of Gethsemane. From his mattress-grave
Heine yearned for the “ melodious tomb of the
enchanter Merlin, which is in the forest of Broce-
liand, in Brittany (of the Dream), under high
oaks whose tops shine like green flames to
heaven.” We are sick for tne Forest of the Dream,
for the. blue twilight of morning, the broad, silent
sunshine of noon, the slanting red rays of evening,
the ineffable odours of the earth-born. We seek
the old leaning boles ; we dream of long glades
and brown pools and glittering streams —the
towers of fairyland above the billowy heights ; we
desire with a great desire the shadow of the leaves
of healing peace.
Alas, we have lost the Master ! Louis, the
well-beloved, could in a few words give us a sense,
direct and penetrating, of the heart and mystery
of a great woodland—witness that wonderful
vision of the lost princess in the dawning forest,
stepping fearlessly, “ splendidly arrayed and piti-
fully tattered ; the diamond ear-drops still glit-
tering in her ears ; and with the movement of her
coming, one small breast showing and hiding
among the ragged covert of the laces.”
Have we a living writer who can bring to our ears
the infinite murmurs of leagues of waving .green,
and give us beautiful adventures down never-
ending alleys, under arching boughs? Yes, we
have Maurice Hewlett. When Prosper le Gai
sticks a sprig of green holly in his cap, puts on his
armour, takes horse and sword, and sets off for the
wilds, our hearts beat with expectation—this is
the true way of beginning. When we learn about
the fresh wet air abroad, the thin blue of the sky,
the dew and the gossamer heavy in the hedges, we
laugh with Prosper. But when we hear of Mor-
graunt, Morgraunt the Forest, deep, dark,
“haunted with the waving of perpetual woods,”
infested with outlaws and wolves, we know that
we have struck “upon the right vein of savage
life.”
Under a spell we ride through Morgraunt with
Isoult La Desirous—black-haired Isoult, with
white shape shining through jher rags, slim, with
a pale face, and two grey eyes a-burning—fight
with Dom Galors, sleep in the forest, and finally
learn why Isoult was called La Desirous. It is in
that sojourn, that sleep under the shadow of the
beech, vyith the strange loveliness and complexity
of the situation, and the shadowy background of
gloom and death, that Maurice Hewlett “touches
upon forest glamour.
* * * *
How has this dim but effective sympathy be-
tween us and the trees arisen ? Is it not a sur-
vival ? As the dog .turns round and round before
settling down, keeping a memory of an ancestral
abode in the tall jungle grass, and as in other
animals primitive habits are ineradicably fixed, so
in man this sigh of relief on entering the forest,
may be the gratitude of the spirit of the old tree-
dweller in the core of our being for ancient salva-
tion, refuge, and shelter.
Or, it may be a trace of that primitive awe
which associated with dark and waving groves the
intimate presence of Deity. Or again, it may be
a sense of relationship, of descent from one remote
common origin, so that in the huge wistful pre-
sence of our Brother the Tree we find an escapal
from the pressure of things, and live for a moment
a fresher, simpler life.
_ As I ride through the bush by night there are
times when I seem about to surprise the great
secret. The vast dumb souls pass their bounds
and watch and over-gloom the wanderer. While
we have night and the forest we are still sure of
some lingering relics of romantic dreams.
When we have received the wound from which
sooner or later we must die, it is a wise habit in
the disenchantment, the revision of values, that
ensues, to spend much time in the woods. To
watch in quiet the sunset glow fade up the leafy
wall, leaving one in green twilight while the dis-
tant hills are still a delicate purple, is to loosen the
bands that tighten on the heart.
* * * *
How feeble are the words used by an unprac-
tised writer ! By their aid I have endeavoured to
show something of the beauty of a dream that has
haunted me through life, and lo ! the words are
grey and cold, and there is no hint of the tranquil
joy of the vision, no cadence of its singing birds,
no touch of its infinite brooding mystery. Yet
within, gathered from things seen and lit by the
indwelling spirit, lies Broceliand, Morgraunt,
Arden, Sherwood, what you will, a forest of oak,
beech and elm, not of slender stems like those
beloved of Puvis de ChaVannes, but squat, gnarled,
moss-grown, hoary.
It is no slight to the land of my adoption that its
trees grow not there—the trees we climbed in
childhood are the trees of remembrance. It is
very beautiful here in Tasmania as I write:
Autumn now, and instead of dying colour
and. melancholy rustling leaves, a feel of
spring in the air, the violet .blowing afrdsh, the
open gold of the drocus, the heath hanging its green
spire with crimson bells, the wild rose flowering
beside its full-filled fruit, and a light of p ea f
blossom against an old stone wall; but. . . but
Like a wave of the sea that floods and breaks in
failin'),
My heart flows to thee, O England of my dream, '
Floods and breaks with a cry when I hear thy voices
calling,
And see in the spring light thy wet woodlands
gleam.
Here in this pleasant isle I rest from my sad roam-
ing,
Soft is the wind, and green and sweet the earth;
But it is not the land where I would fain be homing,
Whose dust and whose passion came forth at mu
birth.
Tasmania.
J.H.
Balzac and His Coffee.
“I have discovered,” wrote Balzac, “ a horrible
and cruel system, which I recommend only to men
of exceeding vigor, with heavy black hair, a com-
plexion of ochre and vermilion, solid hands and
legs like the balusters of the Place Louis XV.
I speak of the use of coffee, ground, distilled,
cold, anhydrous (a chemical term signifying with
little or no water), taken fasting. This coffee
drops into the stomach, which, as you know from
Brillat-Savarin, is a bag of velvety interior, up-
. with papillae and capillaries ; finding
nothing,, it attacks this delicate and voluptuous
lining; it becomes a sort of nourishment demand-
mg its juices; it wrings, it compels them like a
pythoness adjuring her god; it maltreats those soft
walls as a waggoner abuses his young horses. The
flexuses inflame, they blaze and send their sparks up
to the brain. Then, all at once, everything stirs.
Ideas move like the battalions of an army on the
eve of battle, and the battle begins. Memories
charge with banners displayed ; the light horse of
comparisons deploys in a superb gallop ; logic
brings up its ■ artillery with cartridge-boxes and
tram; witticisms come as sharpshooters; figures
of rhetoric take form ; the paper is covered with
ink, for the watch begins and ends in torrents of
black liquid as does the battle in its black ammu-
nition.
“I recommended this beverage taken in this
way to one of my friends who wished to accom-
plish a task to which he was pledged for the
morrow. He believed himself poisoned, went
back to bed, and stayed there like a married
woman. He was tall, fair, with thin hair, and a
stomach as frail as papier mache. I had certainly
shown great lack of discernment !
“In certain conditions, coffee taken fasting pro-
duces a sort of nervous excitement resembling
anger. The voice rises, the gestures express un-
natural impatience. All things must succumb to
the passing idea. You are fierce, quarrelsome
about trifles ; you assume that variable poetic
temperament so much condemned by grocers ;
you attribute to others your own clearness of
vision. A wise man should then avoid showing
himself or permitting himself to be approached. I
discovered this singular condition by chancing to
lose, without working, the state of exaltation that
I had produced. Some friends with whom I was
staying in the country saw me surly, irritable and
unreasonable in discussion. The next day I ack-
nowledged my fault, and together we sought for
its cause. My friends were scientific, and we
soon found it. The coffee would have its prey ! ”
... “All things have their price,” said Na-
poleon, and Balzac was about to pay the price of
his three hundred thousand cups of coffee, his
forty volumes written in fevered haste, his thirty
years’ breathless struggle for genius and love. At
Ylerzohovnia, where he had taken refuge near
Mme. Hanska, hdffelt the urgency of his danger.
In vain did this embodiment of energy struggle
against death, in vain he cried :
“Lbelong to the Opposition, and it is called
Life ! ” •
He married Mme. Hanska, his debts were paid.
“Les Parents Pauvres, ” received with enthusiasm,
proved that his genius was younger, fresher, more
fruitful than ever. It mattered nothing ! 'He died
at fifty-one years of age. Had he not written in
“Albert Sawarus,” with a presentiment startling
in its truth :
“To reach the goal dying like the runner of
old ! To see fortune and death cross the threshold
together; to obtain the beloved one at the
moment that love is extinguished ! To have no
longer the faculty to enjoy when the right to be
happy has been won ! Oh !of how many men
has this been the destiny ! ”
The Gum-Tree Poet.
Possibly the solitude of bush life has much to do
with this poetry (Davitt declares that “portry ” is
the chief gaol product), but as far as my New
England experience goes, the smooth bark of the
trees and the bushman’s custom of carrying a
sharp knife account for some poetry, and a good
deal of publication. The clean, soft bark of the
white or red gum is a great source of inspira-
tion. Our camp cook, a most practical man, was
troubled by the mealtime arrival of non-paying
guests, and when some of them threatened to be
regular visitors, we noticed the following lines cut
into the tree that supported the back of the
sage :
Again, at a point in the New England Range,
looally known as “ the Lighthouse,” whence can
be had a glimpse of the sea, this almost Tenny-
sonian verse is carved on a great white gum :
Gharactoii'istic of the boundary-rider and his rows
with the boss are lines scratched on trees along
boundary-fences, such as
Such sentiments, more or less unprintably ex-
pressed, may be seen along any boundary-fence.
Nor is it the stockman alone who indulges in
rhymes Near the head of the Peel, there is much
evidence of deep alluvial prospecting, also the re-
mains of an old house and garden, which probably
date from an earlier time. In the garden is a fine
old aloe, on a leaf of which is scratched
are specimens of bush poetry from, the hands of
bushmen of different types.
TWIG.
He who eats and goes away
Might be asked another day—
But he who eats and wants to stay
Will be lucky if he get away.
Certain lines most characteristic of the typical
bushman I found cut in the door of a deserted
hut—
goodby me lads with and an art
all uses takes jack mullers part.
A sail, a sail.
The shipwrecked mariner beholds
With beating heart and hope, half fear
Lest t were but the issue of an anxious eye and bralo,
And so he looks away and looks again.
So even I, with object all unlike,.
But instinct just the same—
I look away—and find it all were vain.
Old Hogan’s nose is long,
Old Hogan’s nose is strong,
’T were no disgrace to Hogan’s face
If half his nose were gone.
Or
I’m goipg away,
Hip, hip, hurray.
I’m sure I can’t do worse ;
But I hope when I’m gone
It won’t be long
Before he takes a ride in a hearse.
They are graves that you see down yonder,
A man’s hopes are buried there—
He dug them, seeking his fortune,
But he only unearthed despair.
These lines, with a coffimshaped border—
Curse the man who sent me astray—
Curse him from now to his dying day.
and
Me and my dog
have tramped together
In cold weather
and hot.
Me and my dog
don’t care whether
We get any w< rk:
or not.
Reprints.
THE VAMPIRE.
A fool there was and he made his prayer
(Even as you and I!)
To a rag and a bone and a hank of hair
We called her the woman who did not care,
But the fool he called her his lady fair—
(Even as you and I!)
Oh, the years we waste and the tears we waste
And the work of our head and hand
Belong to the woman who did not know
(And now we know she never could know)
And did not understand !
A fool there was and his goods he spent
(Even as you and I!)
Honour and faith and a sure intent —
And it was n’t the least what the lady meant,
But the fool must follow his natural bent
(Even as you and I!)
Oh, the toil we lost and the spoil we lost
And the excellent things we planned
Belong to the woman who did n’t know why
(And now we know that she never knew why)
And did not understand !
The fool was stripped to his foolish hide
(Even as you and I !)
Which she might have seen when she threw him
aside—
But it is n’t on record the lady tried,
So some of him lived but the most of him died—
(Even as you and I!)
And it isn’t the shame and it isn’t the blame
That stings like a white hot brand —
It’s coming to know that she never knew why
(Seeing at last she could never know why)
And never could understand.
Rudyard Kipling.
THE VAMPIRE.
A woman there was who heard a prayer
(Even as you and I !)
From flesh and bones and a lock of hair —
He called her the woman beyond compare;.
But he only used her to lighten his care
(Even as you and I!)
Oh, the walks we had and the talks we had,.
And the best of bur heart and hand,
Were sought by the man who pretended to care:
He didn’t —-but why he pretended to care
We cannot understand.
A woman received the flowers,he sent,
(Even as you and I!')
Honour and faith she thought his intent,
But God only knows what the gentleman meant,
Yet a man must follow his natural bent
(Even as you and I !')
Oh, the vows we spoke and,the vows we broke;
And the various things we planned,
Belong to the man who said he was true;
(But now we know that he never was true):
And we cannot understand.
One favour she asked —but it was denied
(Even as you and I !) '
In some way or other he might have replied,.
(But it is n’t on record the gentleman tried,—
Her faith in him faltered and finally died,
(Even as you and I !)
And it is n’t the shame and it is n’t the blame,
That stings like a white hot brand,—
It’s coming to know he would n “ver say why,
Seeing at last she could never know why,,
And never could understand.
Mary C. Low.

WHAT THE DOCTORS SAY s—

Doveton-street, Ballarat,
My dear Mr. Malyon,— August 21st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record progress
with regard to my old enemy, “ Eczema Capitis,” from
which I suffered, notwithstanding constant treatment for
some years under the prescription of Professor M‘Call
Anderson and other eminent dermatologists, with only
partial and temporary relief. When I met you I com-
menced somewhat sceptically your ointment Ligmolliene,
and after about two months’ treatment I may honestly say
my enemy is defeated, and I am perfectly well. I will only
add that since I commenced the treatment of myself I have
prescribed it to others, and am still doing so with brilliant
results. Please accept my hearty thanks, and remain yours
sincerely, THOS. LE GAY HOLTHOUSE, M.R.C.S., L.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Sandgroper :Re steam boilers. I hay©
been for weeks working here (W.A.) close to a
boiler (photo, thereof enclosed) which has its
safety-valve tied down with a 2in. rope made fast
to the clamp on the man-hole door and over the
valve-lever, and twitched tight with a fin. bolt.
By a peculiar paradox certain far Northern
M.’sL.A. at Cairns and other towns advocate
Federation because it will “save the North from
the dominion of Brisbane,’’ while the Rockhamp-
ton legislators curse Federation, saying that xt
will place Rockhampton more than ever “under
the heel of Brisbane.” The truth is that the
Northern men feel that union is a big, broad move-
ment that appeals to big, broad men, while the
small Rockhampton soul quakes in deadly fear at
the mention of any scheme beyond the renovation
of the town-pump or the inauguration of a new
sanitary system.
Sinking.
I had often faced a seeming
Certain death without a shiver,
And I boasted of my valor
In the pride that courts a fall —
Boasted vainly, little dreaming
That the Death-fear soon should quiver
Thro’ each nerve, and stamp the pallor
Of a quaking heart o’er all. . . •
Lightning flashing, thunder growling,
Queensland rain in torrents pouring,
As the midnight shift makes ready
For the eight hours’ work below ;
Fierce wind, thro’ the whim-drum howling,.
Shrieks thro’ poppet-heads, and roaring
’Cross the shaft’s mouth, drowns my “ Steady I
Right, old man ; now let her go ! ”
Standing upright in the bucket,
Legs astride its mounted handle,
With my right hand tightly gripping
The old rope Jack feared to trust
With our joint weight, by bad luck it
Chanced a drip put out my candle,
And past slimy slabs I’m slipping
Down in darkness and disgust.
Full three-hundred feet beneath me,
Like a star, I catch the glimmer
Of Jack’s light, and hear him singing ;
But the powder-clouds that hang
In the shaft, and now enwreath me,
Make the distant light seem dimmer—
When my bucket, in its swinging,
Strikes a slab-ledge with a bang !
And the rope between my fingers
Turns from taut to slack instanter,
And I know my weight is resting
On a quarter-inch of pine ;
And each second that it lingers,
With the whim-horse at a canter,
Brings a coil of slack, suggesting
That it’s time to free the chine.
“ Steady ! Heave-up ! Ho ! on top, there ! ”
But my voice is lost in thunder,
And the slack comes coiling round me,
Reaching knee, and thigh, and hip :
And I curse, and scream out “ Stop, there !
Heave-up 1 Jack, lad, stand from under ! ”
For the stranded coils surround me,
And I feel the bucket slip.
As the end draws near and nearer,
All my bones seem turned to marrow,
And with fear and rage I’m choking ;
For the drop means death, I know.
But one piercing shriek of terror,
Shooting upwards like an arrow,
Finds the braeeman calmly smoking ;
And he drawls, “ What’s wrong below ? ”
“Heave-up ! May ten-thousand cancers
Rot your leprous ears for ever! ”
And my brain is fairly boiling,
For I hear the splinter crack.
“ Lower? Right you are! ”he answers,
And I rave as one in fever
As the slack comes coiling, coiling
Round my armpits— tons of slack !
As the bucket disengages,
Head and hands alike are busy;
Still I shriek a malediction
As I gasp, and drop through space !
And the seconds seem as ages
In that downward rush and dizzy,
And I feel the fiery friction
Of the air against my face.
Then a sudden jerk that almost
Tears each arm from out its socket,
(But not death itself could sunder
Such a death-grip) brings relief.
Now ’tis Jack must fear the fall most,
For I hear the unhooked bucket
Crashing down below like thunder,
While I’m trussed up like a sheaf.
Yes, the sturdy hempen strands that
Stood the fearful strain so stoutly
Still encircle me and save me.
After God, I thank the slack ;
And no doubt He understands that
I would thank Him more devoutly
For the lease of life He gave me,
If He'd steered the cask off Jack /

“The application of one Clark, a dental
surgeon,” writes The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, “ for a judicial separation from his wife
on account of her persistent cruelty to him augurs
well for the future of the male race. Thank
goodness, there is. one man with sufficient pluck
to go into court and swear how brutally his wife
treats him. Certainly Mrs. Clark had done well.
In the honeymoon she dug her fingers viciously
into his chest, wearing a fiendish expression ;
she presented him with three black eyes ; she
kicked him until he had to wear Wellingtons to
protect his shins;'she would pretend to kiss him
and kick him saying, ‘I do like to see you
squirm! ’ As his right leg became a mass of
bruises, he squirmed all right, it is supposed. This
was plaintiff’s story; but a large-eyed, auburn-
haired beauty entered the box as defendant and
denied everything. Yes, she did pull his whiskers,
and locked him in the bedroom, because he wanted
to go home to his mother, but she didn’t manu-
facture all the black eyes ; he ran against her
elbow once and that caused one, and so on, &c.,
till the jury said * Not guilty ! ’ and Mr. Clark
fled for safety into the wilderness.”

During the prolonged absence of a
southern dame hubby beguiled the tedium of his
grass-widowerhood by entertaining a select assort-
ment of friends—male and female ; and when an
acquaintance drove up in a hansom one night
with two plump and pleasing damsels the fizz was
brought out and the girls told to “ look in any
time they were passing.” They used to come
pretty often, and the fun would be fast and
furious. But after the lawful mistress of the
establishment had been home about a week the
servant opened the door one day to two stylishly-
dressed young ladies whom she ushered into the
drawing-room. They knew their way about well
enough. One brought the tantalus from the next
room, and by the time the lady of the house
appeared to receive her visitors, they had their
ankles on the mantelpiece and brandy-and seltzer
in their hands. Not having expected to see a
woman, they were a trifle surprised at first. Then
a light broke in upon one of them. *“ Ah ! ” she
cried, “you’re dear old Charley’s latest mash, I
suppose. Well, never mind, we ain’t jealous—are
we, Tottie ?—though Charley’s a real duck.” The
yarn breaks off right here.
In a Hibernian country district of Victoria
there is trouble over a horse-deal. A local Pro-
testant bought an amiable carriage-horse from a
local Catholic, who had reared him in his own
faith. A guarantee of good conduct, docility, and
all the virtues went with the horse, and he was
found to be just full of good humor until be was
harnessed into the trap, and set to haul the family
to church on the blessed Sabbath. Then his one
fault was discovered. He would not pass the
Catholic church (to which he had regularly
drawn his former owner) for cajolery, threats, or
brute-force, and, when hard pushed, simply lay
down in the mud, and defied argument and com-
pulsion. He did this on three consecutive Sun-
days, and the family never got to church. As
there is no approach to the Protestant house of
worship for this family, excepting via the R.C.
establishment, the pony is useless, and litigation
is resorted to. It will be interesting to find
whether or not the law holds that religious bigotry
in a horse renders a bargain null and void.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buver of all other Australian gems.*

SATURDAY, AUGUST 26, 1899.
Treasurer Carruthers’ Balance-Sheet.
Treasurer Carruthers, of N.S.W., delivered
his first financial statement last week, and the
worst that can be said about it is that it was
very like some other balance-sheets of the Reid
Government. Carruthers himself has just
taken over the bag from the previous Iscariot
(t hat historic character is the earliest Treasurer
this paper remembers), and he isn’t in any
sense directly responsible for the unclean finan-
cial doings of his predecessor. These doings
were very unclean indeed. George Reid
proved himself, in his five years custody of the
cash, probably the worst and most shufflesome
and most unscrupulous Treasurer N.S.W. has
ever possessed. His whole scheme of finance
was that it mattered nothing how bad things
were so long as they hoofed reasonably well, and
he had nothing but unutterable scorn and
abuse for his predecessors who tried to put
things right in reality, and didn’t trouble so
very much about mere appearances. The com-
parative straightness of these predecessors
filled G. Reid with loathing and derision. But
the new Treasurer, Carruthers, makes him-
self responsible for these past doings by brag-
ging about how his Government has reduced
the public debt, and cut down the expenditure
of loan money, and paid its way, and provided
surpluses, and put the finances on an honest
footing, and otherwise done everything that it
hasn’t done.
* * * *
Apropos, in the first place, of the Reid
Government’s claim that it has introduced
economy into the expenditure of loan moneys,
and done something to alleviate the borrowing
curse in N.S.W. : The following is a statement
of the loan expenditure during the lifetime of
the present and the last Governments, the sin
being charged in each case to the man who
supplied the estimates and planned the expen-
diture for the year :
Dibbs. Reid.
1892 ... £3,014,680 Jof 1895 ... £659,125
1893 ... 1,929,580 1895-96 ... 1,279,098
1894 ... 1,330,046 1896-97 ... 1,477,318
1897- ... 1,644,769
1898- ... 2,102,192
The Dibbs Government inherited a vast mass
of unfinished public works from its predecessor
Parkes. Parkes went in for public works on
such a gigantic scale that in his last year of
office, 1891, he spent £4,750,167 of loan moneys,
and he left some 300 miles of railway for the
next man to complete ; therefore his successor
had to spend a lot of loan money in his first year
—or else abandon these partly-executed under-
takings, and let the cash already spent on them
be wasted. But Dibbs systematically cut
down loan expenditure, and when he went out
of office he left it almost at the lowest ebb,
and bequeathed practically no unfinished
contracts to his successors. And Reid
systematically increased loan expenditure again
until, in the last year of his Treasurership, it
was £772,000 more than in Dibbs’s last year.
Yet Treasurer Carruthers has the unfathom-
able cheek to allege :
Whilst this Government has been in office the expen-
diture of loan-money has been DIMINISHING at the rate
of £1,100,000 per annum.
There has been a steady increase every year,
and the figures showing that increase glare at
the public for everybody to read on page 946
of Coghlan’s “ Wealth and Progress of
N.S.W. for 1897-98.” And George Reid’s
Government of bluff and bounce has the
effrontery to call its persistent undoing of
Dibbs’s good work a diminution of loan
expenditure.
* • * *
On the same page of the same volume it is
set forth that, at the end of 1894, when Dibbs’s
reign came to an end, the fixed public debt of
N.S.W. was £55,058,846; and that, at 30th
June, 1898 (only 3J years later), Reid had in-
creased the fixed debt to £60,119,156. Between
June, 1898, and June, 1899, there has been a
further increase of about £2,000,000. And
Treasurer Carruthers said, blandly, in his
financial statement of last week :
We have not only met the current expenditure, but
have paid our own debts, and paid off nearly a million of
old debts.
* * * *
The Reid Government has not only increased
the loan expenditure very steadily ; it has also
steadily demoralised in the manner of its expen-
diture. In the early days of Australian borrow-
ing it was understood that loan moneys were
to be applied to works which were permanent
and directly reproductive—not to be used for
purposes of ordinary expenditure in order to
lessen the apparent outgoings and enable an un-
scrupulous public financier to say he had a sur-
plus when he hadn’t one. This virtuous resolve
was departed from almost as soon as made ;
yet in spite of all departures and extravagances,
the loan-money dealings of N.S.W. were
moderately honest till the leprosy and dry-rot
of the Reid regime set in. No previous
Treasurer carried his honesty to the extent of
making it a disease, but none of them aban-
doned financial decency altogether—till Reid
arrived. The following are some interesting
figures on this point:—
Loan money spent on
Loan money unproductive works
spent on re- which should have
productive been paid out of
works. revenue.
State of affairs at
31st Oct., 1894 £48,668,917 ... £6,377,100
Reid.
1895- 711,346 ... 559,552
1896- 985,447 ... 562,658
1897- ... .. 992,353 ... 650,514
1898- 1,318,969 ... 783,223
The first set of figures is taken from Coghlan’s
“Statistical Survey of N.S.W.” for 1893-4
(page 314). The figures for 1895-98 are taken
from the same official’s annual “Statistics of
the Seven Colonies” and though the totals don’t
quite agree with the previous set, taken from
Coghlan’s “ Wealth and Progress,” they come
near enough to it. The figures for 1899 are
based on Premier Reid’s loan estimates.
• * » •
The lesson they teach is this. Reid’s prede-
cessors in the Treasurership were moderately
honest, though their probity didn’t amount to
an incurable complaint ; and, while professing
to borrow for reproductive works they spent
11-4 per cent, of the loan moneys on works
which were not reproductive, and which should
have been paid out of revenue. They did this,
presumably, to swindle a trusting people into
the belief that the public accounts were square
when they weren’t. And George Reid, whose
want of political probity amounts almost to a
distemper, misapplied nearly 40 per cent, of
the loan money in like fashion. 'ln 1895-96,
under the Reid Ministry, N.S.W. spent more
borrowed money than any other two provinces
of Australia, and it also spent more borrowed
money on unproductive works, which should
have been charged to revenue, than any other
two provinces. In 1896-97 it spent more bor-
rowed money than any other province, bar
Westralia; and it spent more money on un-
productive works than any other Australian
province, bar Westralia. In 1897-98 it again
spent more loan money than an}' other pro-
vince, bar Westralia, and it spent more money
on unproductive works than any other Aus-
tralian province. The Reid Government has
put up some very fine borrowing records.
* * * *
It has been one of the loudest and shrillest
boasts of the Reid Government that it would
never—never—never have a deficit like its
wicked and shiftless predecessors, but would
walk in the ways of strict and almost frantic
economy all the days of its life. The following
table, from pages 24 and 25, Part 1., of Cogh-
lan’s “ Statistical Register ” for 1898, shows
how the last two Governments walked in this
respect up to 12 months ago ; the remainder of
the statistics being from Treasurer Carruthers’
statement of last week :
Dibbs Ministry.
Revenue. Expenditure. Surplus.

According to the Auditor-General’s report the
famous deficit reached its maximum under the
Reid regime. At all events the expenditure
is rising very materially, and this is the second
year in succession when the Reid Ministry has
asked for extra taxation. And even with the
extra taxation it expects to go £126,728 to the
bad in the year ending 30th June, 1900.
* * * *
In one respect, however, it must be admitted,
the Reid Ministry has practised economy. It
has been as moderate as possible in paying for
public works out of revenue (and has charged
them to loans instead, thereby making the
accounts look better on the outside). In the
three years, 1892-3-4, Premier Dibbs paid
.£3,251,321 of the public works expenditure out
of revenue—an average of £1,084,000 per
annum. (His predecessor, Parkes, by the
way, paid even more.) And in the 3J years
from 31st December, ’94, to 30th June, 1898,
Premier Reid paid ,£2,899,617 of the public
works expenditure out of revenue—an average
of <£829,000 a year. In the matter of saving
£255,000 a year by paying things out of loans
instead of out of revenue, George Reid has
always been an economist of the highest order.
These figures are to be found by anyone who
wants to look at them on page 31, part I, of
Coghean’s “ Statistical Register ” for 1898.
* * * *
And in addition to all these other sins and
dodges and political frauds, the Reid Govern-
ment invented the great Gash Basis. In for-
mer times not only the actual cash outgoings of
the year, but also the unpaid liabilities existing
when the financial year closed, were charged
against the year’s revenue. Premier Reid
saved himself about .£700,000 on paper at one
fell swoop by enacting that the unpaid liabilities
at the end of the financial year should not be
reckoned as liabilities at all—that nothing
should be said on the unpleasant subject. The
principle is cut so fine that a sum of nearly
£200,000 due for interest on part of the public
debt for the half-year from Ist January to 30th
June is not reckoned a liability for the financial
year which ends on 30th June, because the
money isn’t actually paid over to the public
creditors till next day, Ist July ! The Treasurer
has paid it away before the financial year closes
to the Bank of England on behalf of the public
creditors. So far as the Treasury is concerned
the money is both due and paid. But because
the Bank of England hasn’t parted with it till
24 hours later the Reid Government reckons
this money, which it owes and has paid away,
as money that it doesn’t owe and which is
actually on hand. Another huge mass of
interest falls due in October. But though half
of this is owing at the close of the financial year
it isn’t treated as a debt. Premier Reid is the
inventor of this great device for making things
look better than they are, and he ought to be
proud of it.
* * * *
N.S.VY.’s new Treasurer also says that “ the
railways have been paying their way for some
years past.” What they have really done for
the year just closed is as yet uncertain, but,
according to the Auditor-General’s reports,
things “for some years past” have been as
follows :
June, 1895 year) ... Loss £186,135
„ 1896 (year) ... „ 321,690
„ 1897 ( „ ) ... „ 38,545
„ 1898 ( ~ ) ... „ 57,129
The same painful public officer further reports
that the accumulated N.S. W. railway losses to
30th June, 1899, are £7,773,758 !
* * * «
And, in addition to all these things, N.S.W.
Treasurer Carruthers has this specific griev-
ance about the great accumulated deficit:—
The same state of affairs existed in Victoria, where they
have an old deficit, and have issued bonds to redeem that
deficit. The same state of affairs exists in New Zealand.
Nearly all the other colonies have accumulated deficits,
and yet we do not hear of their public accounts being
constantly ripped up as if these deficits were new.
Apart from Treasurer Carruthers’ remark-
able ignorance of the fact that Maoriland has
no accumulated shortage, these remarks are
quite true. And as to why the deficits of the
other provinces are not perpetually ripped up
—well, Kingston and Braddon and Turner
and Nelson were more or less gentlemen in
their manners. They didn’t yelp day and
night with sickening iteration about the
“Downer deficit,” or the “Dobson de-
ficit,” or the “Patterson deficit,” or
the “MTlwraith deficit.” They didn’t
brag constantly about.the great things they
were going to do in the way of putting the
finances straight, nor assume an attitude of
“ me and God” ; nor did they issue mean and
shabby slanders in which they tried to repre-
sent their predecessors as vastly worse than
they were. They didn’t proceed to go from
bad to worse themselves, and meanly try to
saddle their own shortages on their predeces-
sors. They never invented a Cash Basis, nor
did they do strange juggles with the accounts
while boasting with horrible persistence of
their absolute straightness, and throwing jibes
at their much-more-honest predecessors. They
didn’t aim mud for three years at a stretch. In
fact, being gentlemen, more or less, they
thought their mission was just to put the
finances straight, while the amazing George
Reid’s mission was to be a cocky political gut-
tersnipe running after his predecessor and ask-
ing him where he got that hat. None of the other
Treasurers ever placed before the Assembly a
garbled document which they called “a piece of
cold iron for their opponents to chew,” and had
to humbly crawl down with the document after-
wards. Nor did any of them offer to Parliament
a crooked statement about their predecessors’
Civil Service appointments and have to back
down and withdraw the slander. The main
reason why the N.S.W deficit has become an
open sore has been Premier Reid’s unspeakable
want of political decency. If Treasurer Car-
ruthers can’t understand why the N.S.W.
shortage is a ghost which is perpetually rising
amid streaks of lurid language, a perusal of his
chief’s speeches in some bygone Hansards and
elsewhere may enlighten him. And if he
wants to know why it is likely to keep on being
a ghost, the answer to that is also easy. A
Government which for three solid years did
nothing but shriek “ Dibbs deficit!” and which
rises in tearful complaint when its own short-
ages are mentioned and asks the enemy it
jeered at so long not to hit it, doesn’t inspire
much respect. It looks so mean that people
hit it as a matter of course.
A True-Born Briton.
Stand forward, John Norton ! formerly
Heron, never Newton ; editor and proprietor
of Truth newspaper ; transit commissioner and
alderman of the city of Sydney; member of the
Parliament of New South Wales ; influential
pressman and politician ; linguist and master of
bad language ; denouncer of “ Tattersall,”
“ Tooth,” and “ Toohey,” but never (on your
Oath) blackmailer; hero of squalid amours,
deserter of the heroines, but never (on your
Honour) anything worse ; drunkard aforetime,
but for eighteen months (on your Thirst)
as sober as any of the Judges who have
acquitted you ; exposer (0 Sacred Boomerang !)
of concupiscent politicians and lecherous
Governors (Holy Platypus ! aid us and defend
us !); champion of public virtue, guardian of
the public morals, jealous pillar of the Church
of England, typical true-born Briton—stand
forward !
* * • *
Yes, typical Briton ! though you may exag-
gerate the type. You have valiantly lusted,
canted, and fought; and Lust, Cant, and
Eight are the glorious birthrights of that great
race which is devouring the earth and populat-
ing the heavens and the hells. Humanity’s
unparalleled hybrid of Teuton, Scandinavian,
and Celt, wherever he goes the true-born
Briton begets children,. erects churches, and
sheds blood. You, the individual, sum all the
aboriginal qualities of your dominating tribe.
You fought on board ship, you fought your
landlord, and you have fought at intervals all
through your lurid career—sometimes because
you had to, but often because you wanted to;
and, if you carry a revolver now, it is because
your pace has been fast enough to kill any
man’s nerve. You have enjoyed to the full the
Briton’s splendid privilege of uniting moral
status to immoral contract; you have laid your
offerings, like any British deacon, on the altar
of religion and the altar of the servant-girl;
you have schooled archbishops while you were
tutoring mistresses ; and expounded at the same
time the blessings of the Thirty-nine Articles
and other articles which could not be men-
tioned in the same breath, if the Briton did
not worship them in the same respectable
home.
» * * *
And the true-born Briton casts you off,
renounces and despises you ! —though in the
sixteenth century his soldiers were robbers, his
sailors pirates, preying on friend and foe, and
in the nineteenth his instinct to fight and
plunder is merely veneered with a “civilisation”
which does not prevent a British general from
securing, like a Dyak, the head of his enemy
for a trophy—though the Dyak does not dese-
crate graves. The true-born Briton is ashamed
of you, is shocked at you, while his missions
depopulate the South Sea Islands and his
syphilitic Indian army yells for the reintroduc-
tion of its overthrown system of licensed and
State-regulated vice 1 Wonderful past all
whooping ! —and not wonderful in the least;
because so beautifully characteristic, so
eminently just. The Briton hoists you with
your own petard. How would you froth and
declaim if J ohn Norton were only John Smith !
What an “ Open Letter ” you would write
John Smith ! under what mountains of alli-
terative indignation you would smother him !
how you would expose John Smith to the
scorn of men and moral Britons ! You are
soused in your own sauce, John Norton ; you
are frizzled in your own fat; you are served in
your own canting and hypocritical gravy.
Truly, the gods are very just.
* * * *
You have done much good and evil in your
time, John Norton ; you have fought against
public abuses, even if you nourished private
abuses of your own ; and it is the contrast
between your paraded principles and your per-
sonal practice which makes the gravamen of
your sin to-day. Lusting and fighting are
natural; if our ancestors had not lusted and
fought they would have left no posterity to
extol the ascetic virtues ; the true-born Briton
is none the worse man because he is the better
animal. Throughout the scheme of Nature
stronger devours weaker; and rose-water
ethics avail nothing against the savage
moral of Life: “Eat, or he eaten!”
It is the cant of brutality denying
brutality which is brutality’s damnation.
A good brute, Briton or other, fulfils
the law of his nature, and his brutish-
ness may be borne with, even countenanced,
occasionally admired; but the brute who
masquerades as saint is an object for all the
Universe to detest.
* * * *
Stand back, John Norton ! your strength is
becoming a little too strong for us ; you exag-
gerate a little too much the qualities of your
British type. The best Briton is good to his
enemy, when he has thoroughly beaten his
enemy; he is true to his women and pro-
tects his children; his brutality is an
honourable kind of brutality, and he does
not cant more than his blood and tradition
make inevitable. Take him all round, the best
Briton is as good a man as humanity has bred.
But the inferior Briton is an abomination, and
there is no reason why we should endure him
longer than Australian apathy compels us.
Stand back, John Norton, in the shadow of
your past ; of —since none of us can cast stones
—stand forward, if you will, and live your past
down.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Woe of Kidston, M.L.A.
Up in Rockhampton (Central Queensland) one
Kidston, M.L.A., has struck a grievance
against the Commonwealth Bill which is all his
own. It is quite an original grievance. His
trouble is that the proposed Federation covers
all Australia —at least he thinks it does—and


his complaint is this :
The area of Australia is 2,946,000 square miles. Under
the constitution the 123rd and 124th clauses will cover
the whole of this area, if all the States adopt the
Commonwealth Bill, and consequently there will not be
a single acre left under the Federal Parliament for sub.
division in the future into self-governing States as settle-
ment increases and the needs of the people demand.
The area of the United States is 3,490,000 square miles,
which is divided at present into 45 States and five
territories. A provision similar to the 123rd and
124th clauses of the proposed Australian constitution was
included in the constitution of the United States, but it
covered only the thirteen original States, whose area is
about one-tenth of the whole. The remaining portion
was placed under the Federal Legislature, and in the
course of the last hundred years thirty-two districts have
been carved out of it and erected into self-governing
States, while a portion nearly as large as New South
Wales and Queensland combined still remains under the
Federal Government in the form of territories, to be
divided into States in the future as occasion arises.
The exact nature of the grievance is none too
clear, but Kidston, M.L.A., is in deadly
earnest about it, and illustrates it with big dia-
grams in the papers. Yet Kidston seems a
trifle upside-down in his grief. The Aus-
tralian Commonwealth will almost certainly
start with a very large piece of Federal coun-
try—the Northern Territory, which is purely
British property now, though S.A. administers
it in a temporary sort of way : S.A. is simply
caretaker of the Territory, and makes no secret
of its willingness to hand it over—in fact, if the
Federal Government won’t take it, Britain will
probably have it thrown back on her hands
before long. And the U.S. started with prac-
tically no spare Federal territory, and no pros-
pect of any. Its little scrap of ground was
shut in by the sea on the east, Canada on the
north, Spanish Florida and French Louisiana on
the south, and Louisiana on the west —in those
days, Louisiana extended from the Gulf of
Mexico to Canada. And, behind Louisiana,
the territories of the Viceroy of Mexico
filled two - thirds of the west, and the
allegedly British province of Oregon occupied
the rest. The United States bought or con-
quered much territory afterwards, just as
the Australian Commonwealth may absorb
Papua, Polynesia, Java, Celebes and other
places, if it feels hungry, and has the chance,
and wants more soil and more niggers and
responsibilities. But if there is any special
advantage in a Commonwealth starting with a
bit of spare country to make new States out of,
Australia makes a better beginning than the
U.S. did.
4
Victoria’s Old Age Dole.
The Victorian Old-Age Pensions Bill pro-
vides, in a small way, something after
the M.L. fashion, for persons over 65, or
younger people who are infirm through
following unhealthy occupations. The appli-
cant must be of good character and have
lived in Victoria 20 years out of the last 25 ;
he must also have put in five years continuous
residence before applying for his pension, unless
he is a sailor, in which case special exemptions
are allowed. Chinese and Asiatics are shut
out. Maximum pension is only .£lB a year—
it ought to be £25, but there are hopes
that it may increase later on. If the
applicant has no private means, or his private
means don’t exceed £lB a year, he gets full pen-
sion ; for every £1 of income above £lB a year,
he is to receive £1 less pension. The residence
qualifications are pretty hard, but it is difficult
to see that much better can be done. So long as
only one or two provinces grant Old-Age Pen-
sions they must have a stiff residence qualifica-
tion, else they will have to pension the aged
poor of all the neighboring provinces as well as
their own. When the Federal Government
takes the matter over and applies the system
to all Australia, then so many years’ residence'
anywhere in Australia and Tasmania will be
sufficient title, and the nomads who have
drifted from province to province till they have
lost all chance of qualifying in any one province
can be provided for. But while the matter is
attended to by the States separately no one
State can take the risk of having the venerable
wanderers of all Australia loaded on its
Treasury. Federation is the only thing that
can put the Old-Age Pension business on a
satisfactory footing. Till it comes along the
itinerant one must apparently fall between two
or more stools, and come down heavily on the
cold, hard soil of impecuniosity. By way of
postscript, it may be mentioned in the mean-
time that in one respect Turner’s Bill is utterly
and incurably bad. It provides that nobody
who has put in six months’ gaol sentences dur-
ing the five years before he reached the quali-
fied age shall be eligible for a pension, no
matter what the sentence was for. And
Victoria, which is the most utterly bowelless
province in Australasia in its treatment of the
poor, is full of decent, sober, absolutely respect-
able old fellows who have done six and twelve
months and more for being found swagging in
the country in search of work ; and of old
people who were gaoled for long terms because
they were friendless and sick, and the J.P.
didn’t know what to do with them. Turner’s
Old-Age Pension Bill absolutely shuts out
the class which most wants a pension
—the decent, aged person who is friendless and
has no assets at all. The respectable old Vic-
torian man or woman who is past work, and
has no friends or assets, is almost dead sure to
have done at least six months in gaol inside
the last five years. The disgraceful Victorian
J.P. has attended to that.

Big 1 John, the Apostate.
“Cornstalk” writes from the land of the
camel and the wild cat:
Dear Bulletin. —You are right in saying (29/7/’99)
that the local Federationists are badly in want of a
leader, yet this is scarcely our difficulty. It is as if you
said that the Outlanders were badly in want of a leader.
That is, if Kruger or some other prominent Boer were to
lead them, they would speedily get the franchise and all
that they wanted. So, if Forrest, or, better still, if
Hackett, M.L.C., editor of the West Australian— who
is the David Symb of Westralia, only more so—were to
declare for remitting the Commonwealth Bill to the
people, there would be an end of the local federal diffi-
culty, for three-fourths of the electors favor union in
terms of the Bill. The position is peculiar. Forrest at
heart wishes W.A. to enter the union at once, as an
Original State, principally because he is becoming tired of
his long lease of power in Westralia, and considers that
he is fitted for the larger atmosphere of Australasian
politics. In May, 1898, after returning from the Conven-
tion, he addressed (for the first time in his life) a public
meeting in Perth, on Federation, and posed as an ardent
Federalist. “In my opinion,” he said, “the Bill is a
thoroughly good one, and quite as good as we are likely
to get if we had to go over the work again. There are
many parts of the Bill that I do not approve of, and at
the Convention I voted accordingly, but, taking the Bill
altogether, as I have said, I am now fairly content.
Then he explained several minor objections to the Bill.
The chief reason which had been given why the Bill
should not be adopted was that the time was not oppor-
tune. “My own opinion,” said Forrest, “is that this
is a matter for the people themselves to judge of.
I would ask those persons (who say that the time is not
opportune) to carefully examine their own minds and
ask themselves whether they are in earnest in their pro-
fessions in favor of Federation. In my opinion the people
who say that the present time is not opportune for
Federation will have to wait a very long time indeed
before they will ever be convinced that the time is oppor-
tune. . . . Now that the Bill has been decided upon,
and we have done all that we could to obtain the fairest
terms possible, I see no reason to be other than satisfied
with the result that has been obtained.” He dismissed
the objection that W.A. should not enter till a rail-
way to S.A. had been built, because—“ in a very
short time we ourselves could construct a line from
the end of our present railway system to the S.A.
border, a distance of only about 400 miles. . . . Com-
paratively, that would be an insignificant work.” And,
finally, he said (it is in the speech which he was so proud
of that he had it specially printed and circulated): “I
tried to get the fairest terms I could for the colony, and,
that being the case, I can’t make myself believe I should
be acting properly or honorably if, after we had finished
our labors —after I had taken part in the discussions and
influenced decisions—if I were to come back and say to
the people of this colony that t he Bill we had passed, and
to which I had given my acquiescence, was such a bad
Bill that I would not even allow it to be submitted to the
people of this colony. . . . My duty is to try' to obtain
the verdict of the people upon it calmly and delibe-
rately, and when there is no other excitement.”
Now Forrest thoroughly believed all this, and was
sincere in his intention to submit the Bill to a referendum.
But Hackett came on the scene, and decided that our
“infant industries” and the local cabbage production
would suffer by interprovincial freetrade. His plea was
that although we have five years of fiscal grace under the
special sliding scale, yet the Bill provides merely that we
may re-impose the duties if we want to take advantage of
the sliding scale, and not that they remain in any event,
and that the duties are so obnoxious that Parliament
could never be trusted to re-impose them so as to take
advantage of the concession, but woul t be content to see
them disappear right off. Well, Forrest was given the
choice of fighting Hackett, losing the support of the
West Australian, and having the old hands stirred up
against him, or giving in to the anti-federalists, and
countenancing their log-rolling. He did the latter—he
had to. He had to move as he was told; to refer
Federation to a select committee, which is to find reasons
why Westralia cannot join the Union under present con-
ditions. He had to declare that he considered we
could not join without some amendment of the Bill ; and
he had to support the doctrine that if Parliament did not
approve of the Bill it should not let it go to the people.
This was to intimate that if the Lower House passed a
Referendum Bill in spite of him, the Upper House, where
Hackett is, might with impunity throw it out. He had,
in fact, to swallow his speech of May, 1898. Two mem-
bers of the Government who had declared for the submis-
sion of the Bill to the people and in favour of federation
have returned to the anti-federal camp —Hackett
whipped ’em in like stray hounds. As to the future, the
will of three-fourths of the population—and those
three-fourths mostly people who are not backward
in expressing their opinions, and making a noise when
they are thwarted—cannot be flouted for long. Their sheer
weight must tell. We have leaders, but their value is a
cypher. One of them is the Government’s tame-cat
Opposition-leader Leake ; another is the awfully-con-
ceited Little-boy James, M.L.A. ; another, boomster
Matiiieson, who is trying to graduate for a Commons
seat. The erratic Voster of the Long Hair has seceded to
the anti-Federalists. No, the want of a leader is not the
particular trouble just now, unless it were a leader who
would talk fight, and perhaps scare the old Gropers a
little, as they were scared at the time of the alluvial
trouble —just enough to show them that the will of
three-fourths of the community cannot with impunity be
made subservient to that of the other fourth. A redistri-
bution of seats would do good though—or Hackett's
funeral.
MTntyre’s Mistake.
Vic. Full Court nearly took Melbourne’s breath
away last week by upholding the conviction of
lawyer MTntyre, although Chief-Justice Mad-
den, who “ sat ” at the long-warded-off trial of
Mac., had evidently thought him certain to
wriggle out of danger. Judge Madden’s read-
ing of the Law took a turn for the better under
the guidance of brothers Williams and
a’Beckett, and he gave forth the judgment of
the Full Court as though it were exactly w hat
he had expected all along. On behalf of the
learned trinity, he stated that lawyer M‘ln-
tyre’s criminality lay in his having presented
himself with certain property contrary to his
client’s instructions. There was no need for
this fraudulent transfer under the circum-
stances, and as a stroke of policy it was a dis-
grace to the legal profession. “The document
he had prepared was simply a means by which
he might appropriate Cocidon’s property,” said
Full-Courtecl Madden. Now, simple appro-
priation of a client’s property is the biggest
mistake a lawyer can be guilty of. The proper
professional way of robbing one’s victim is to
hold his property in trust and stick to it.
There was a suspicion of contempt in his
Honor’s accents when he said, “If MTntyre
had pursued the instructions given him, and
prepared a settlement creating himself a trustee,
his position would be very different in respect
of the transaction. But he had not done that,
for instead of drawing up a mere settlement, he
had made an absolute transfer of the property
to himself, without being created a trustee, or
assuming any of the duties, obligations, and
rights of a trustee.” Judge Madden, in his in-
teresting deliverance, did not suggest that
MTntyre’s transfer of the property to himself
by fraudulent deed, instead of appointing him-
self sole trustee of the same property under the
deed he was instructed to draw up, could have
made any real difference to his client, who
stood to be equally well robbed either way.
But it made all the difference to M‘lntyre by
putting him in a position to be convicted.
“ Their Honors (the Full Court) considered that
MTntyre had prevented himself, by his own
wrongful act, from being a trustee.” Hence
his liability to imprisonment. MTntyre had a
fair chance offered him for getting possession
of the property as a trustee, but he “ had
feloniously made himself the apparent owner of
the property,” and the lawyer who chooses to
be a felon when he might just as well appoint
himself a trustee is not entitled to any protec-
tion from the Law. So M'lntyre, who was
due to receive sentence on Tuesday of this
week, will have to repent his tactical error in
a dungeon cell, the Bulletin supposes.

Melbourne’s Sweating 1 Tramway
Monopoly—Once More.
On Sunday, 6th Aug., 200 Melb. Tramway
employes met in the local Temperance Hall
and unanimously agreed to support M.L.A.
Bromley in his effort to obtain for them the
working hours agreed upo.i in the Tramways
Act. Several letters from employes were
read, showing that the men generally had been
warned by the co.’s officials not to attend under
pain of being “ marked men.” In view of the
unspeakable co.’s well-known methods of secret
observation and underhand coercion this was to
be expected, but it is important to note
that the co. which so forcibly objects to the
men putting their case before the public has
bombarded Melbourne royally with observa-
tions about the iniquity of the new Bill and
with raucous cries about “ repudiation.” Hav-
ing circularised every local municipality and
petitioned Parliament, it finally rounded up its
shareholders to protest in strenuous fashion —
against the co. being compelled to keep itsagree-
ment. In its circular the co. states in an airy,
off hand manner, without going into particulars,
that the effect of the proposed Amendment Act
will be to increase its wages bill by <£27,000 per
annum. It is difficult to see how these figures
are arrived at, as the increased annual cost of
employing 480 men at 425. per week (the pre-
sent average wage) for 48 hours instead of 60,
would only be ,£13,000. The above-men-
tioned circular further goes on to talk largely
and vaguely about this awful co.’s responsi-
bilities. Just here is might be well to remark
that its responsibilities don’t amount, literally,
to a brass farthing. It has a paid-up capital of
£lBo,ooo—a good part of it not subscribed by
the shareholders, but accumulated profits. The
lines were built with money (£1.650,000) bor-
rowed by the municipalities on their guarantee,
for which they—not the co.—are responsible,
and should the co. default in its payments to
the interest and sinking-funds, not one farthing
of its uncalled capital can be called up to
make good the deficiency ! The circular also
reiterates the old lie about the paltry 5 per
cent, dividend, though the shareholders, work-
inglines built by the people’s money, received
dividends of 12 and 16 per cent., besides
bonuses of 20 and 60 per cent. (! !) in the good
times of ’B9 and ’9O, and all through the de-
pression of late years they never received less
than 9 per cent, on their original capital, or 5
per cent, on their capital and accumulated pro-
fits. Furthermore, the co. competes against the
State railways, which pay their employes a liv-
ing wage and work them 48 hours. The Tramway
Co. asks for special concessions in the shape of an
extended lease and a lot of other things, on con-
dition that it leaves off breaking the law by
which it is pledged to give its_ employes an
eight-hour day—which is much the same as
granting a burglar a bonus to leave off burg-
ling, instead of giving him seven years for the
burgling he has already done. Also, it wants
special concessions before it will establish
penny sections on its lines, which is also pure
bluff. According to its agreement, the State
has power to revise the fares after 1898 with-
out asking the co.’s permission or granting
it any concessions at all. Suburban councillors
lately held a conference on the whole “ fares”
question, and requested Premier Turner to
take advantage of the power he possesses, but,
though the demand for penny sections is loud
and general, Turner replied that “ the Govt,
did not see its way to revise the fares as re-
quested,” whereupon the conference carefully
dissolved itself and left matters in statu quo.
On the whole, there is a great deal too much
statu quo about the business. It is nearly a
year now since the Tramway Commission
reported on the matter, and the Turner Govt,
has carefully declined to move ever since, and
the councillors and their Tramway Trust refuse
to take the concern into their own hands.
Wherefore it is quite time Parliament stepped
in and forced the company to keep its original
agreement, or else shut down on it for breach
of agreement, and let the Trust run the city’s
trams itself.

One Destiny, and One Shilling.
The A.N.A., which was started in Victoria as
a modest benefit society and small-talk associa-
tion, and gradually expanded into a valuable
Federation agency, is just now trying to make
up its mind on the matter of its annual shilling
fete. Said fete is, as everybody knows, chiefly
a bicycle meeting, and now that public interest
in Bill Martin has got down to low ebb,
there wouldn’t be much f6te left but for the
lottery associated therewith. Thousands of
people pay a shilling each for tickets represent-
ing a penn’orth or so of chance to win a £lOOO
gold vase, but comparatively few people use
them as tickets of admission to the fete. In
short, the A.N.A., like the Ancient Druids,
and the Eight Hours Society, invites the
general public to partake of a gamble from
which the promoters reap a sinfully large
profit after paying fat prizes to sundry bicycle-
riders, and meeting a lot of necessary and
unnecessary expenses. It is not a respectable
gamble in either its speculative or political
aspects, but it seems to be tolerated by the
same Leg. Assembly that prohibits the respect-
able Tote on alleged high moral grounds. The
question now before the A.N.A. is—shall the
Lottery be cast aside as an unclean thing, or
shall a high-sounding Association continue to
figure in the character of Ikey Mo ? There
still remains some good work for the A.N.A. to
encourage before it dies a natural death, but if
the voice of Young Australia is to ring impres-
sively on any future occasion the annual
Demonstration lottery will have to be discarded
in the meantime. The “bob” swindle—for
practically it is a fraud on the average investor
who doesn’t realise how little gambling value
he gets for his money—has acquired an
unpleasant notoriety. The arguments advanced
in its favor are quite despicable. They breathe
a national aspiration for boodle—very paltry
boodle at that. Also, the arguments urged by
A.N.A. opponents of the lottery are mere snuf-
flesome echoes of church cant, and, there is no
health in them. As a matter of regrettable
fact, the young men who whooped so elo-
quently for the great Australian cause, now
seem incapable of criticising their “ art-union ”
from a political standpoint. The essential per-
niciousness of their financial device is what they
seem not to understand. It is left for the more-
or-less-unfriendly newspapers to state the case
properly, and whilst the A.N.A. committee
wrangles about its illegal shilling flutter, the
Age, which is jealous of the Association’s in-
fluence, and the Argus, which dislikes all
young, un-English creeds in a general way, are
making preliminary efforts to laugh the Asso-
ciation into contempt.

The dreadful Hogan, M.P., who poses as
member for Australia in the British House of
. Commons, has written to London Times pro-
posing that Australia should, do as Canada did—
let the Queen select the Federal capital. Surely
even sucli a dull dog as Hogan ought to know
better. The greatest difficulty in Australia is
to select a reasonably central site where there
is water—water enough to let the people of the
future great city have a bath in the morning;
water to mix with their whisky; water
to make their daily tea with, and to boil
the potatoes in, and a little over to wash
the family dog with, and some for irriga-
tion, and to supply electric-light and power
with if possible. Canada, like England, is a
,splend : dly-aWered country, and when the
Queen se.e-.e.d a central-looking place on a
river she could hardly go wrong. In Aus-
tralia, if the Queen selected a nice, central-
looking spot on a river it would probably hap-
pen to be a map-river only—one of those
streaks in the sand where there is a trickle of
water for a mouth in every two years, and a
raging torrent for a day and a half every ten
years. If Hogan, M.P., had suggested that '
the Queen should come out here personally,
with boring apparatus, and select a site, the
idea would have something in it. Otherwise
The Bulletin would suggest as an amend-
ment that, if there is a deadlock over the
Federal capital, the Khalifa be made referee.
He knows something about deserts. Victoria
was brought up in a country where they have
the delusion that a river is a place with water
in it.

Toowoomba (Q.) Chronicle publishes this
among a long list of reasons why Queenslanders
should vote against the Bill:
Because it will ultimately mean a land-tax which will
fall heavier on Queensland farmers than on those in the
southern colonies who have no Divisional-Board taxation.
The Chronicle evidently never heard that
Victoria, Tasmania and S.A. all have local
government systems, and that their farmers
pay local taxes just as the Queensland ones do.
Groom’s paper, it would seem, really believes
that Queensland is the only province which has
a local government system. The Queensland
farmer pays one land-tax—to his Divisional
Board. The N.S.W. farmer pays one—to the
State. The Victorian, S.A., and Tasmanian
farmers pay a tax to the local authority, and all
except the very small ones also pay a second
land-tax to the State treasury. So if the people
of these provinces vote for a Federal land-tax
as well, that will leave Queensland and N.S. W.
with two land-taxes, and Victoria, S.A., and
Tasmania with three each. And if Groom’s
silly paper at Toowoomba thinks that the
people of these provinces are going to put all
these taxes on themselves if they can help it,
then Groom’s silly paper ought to be sent to
an asylum for weak-minded publications.

Despite the plain, emphatic statement in clauses
8 and 30 of the Commonwealth Bill that at
elections for both Federal Houses “ each
elector shall vote only once,” Brisbane Tele-
graph rises up every now and then and tells
the public that “ plural voting is not abolished
by the Federal Constitution,” and the present
plural voter will be able to vote a dozen times
just as he does now. There is no ingenuity
and no thrill in this kind of mendacity. Now
and then, in a bush pub., you meet a man who
tells a really interesting dog-lie, and you feel
that it is Art. And then the coarse, unimagina-
tive person, who envies that dog-lie but hasn’t
the gift to invent one of his own, rises up and
says, in a thick brown voice, that he once
knew a blanky dog that was eight feet high
and two yards round the middle. And he only
excites scorn and disgust, and the audience
throws the heel-taps of its beer at him, because
his lie isn’t Art. That is just what is the
matter with the paper run by three ex-sin-
shifters at Brisbane. It has all the lightness
and grace of the domestic cow dancing, but it
isn’t Art. It is only a mixture of prejudice
and ignorance and dull venom and foolish old
woman.
“ Old Stoney ” on an hitherto neglected sub-
ject :
Considering how town employers are forced to attend
to the sanitary arrangements of their premises, why
should the squatter—the largest employer of labor for a
portion of the year—be allowed to utterly neglect the
health and comfort of his employes ? Properly to ap-
preciate the aroma of a shearer’s hut a visit should be
made to a certain N.S.W. station, the property of a rich
and old-established company, about four weeks after the
start of the season, by which time the hut, killing-yards,
and piggery, all within 50yds. of each other, will have
their respective stenches in full blast; or to , where
the drainage from the huts runs into an open tank used
for drinking purposes. As exemplifications of space-
economy, many of the huts are worth inspection—
four tiers of bunks in some of them ; the top-tier resting
on the tie-beams, immediately over the dining-table.
Spare food is stowed promiscuously under the lower
bunks, and the cook usually camps with the bread and
cooked beef 1 The whole hut, about big enough for six
men, has often 30 stowed into it. The reason this
abominable state of affairs has not been more noticed is
that shearers, being long-suffering and healthy animals,
after getting knocked over at one shed, soon recover and
cut in again at the first opportunity. Any complaint to
the squatter is, as a matter of course, promptly met with
the “ sack.” But the very best argument in favor of the
extension of the Factories Act to stations, lies in the fact
that to a man the squatters oppose such extension.

Legislative Councils claim to be revisers of
proposed legislation ; yet, though special atten-
tion was given by the N.S.W. body to the Coal
Mines Regulation Bill, on the ground that it
was sent through the Assembly while most of
that House was asleep, this clause was passed :
“No person not now employed as a coal-getter
shall be allowed to work alone in the face of the
workings until he has had two years’ experi-
ence of feuch work under the supervision of
skilled workmen.” The intention undoubtedly
was to keep incompetent new hands from being
entrusted with dangerous work without due
supervision—to make it impossible for un-
skilled workmen to be put in the faces without
skilled men beside them. But it is now pointed
out that, though an unskilled man may: not ,be
eniployed alone, there is apparently nothing to
prevent two or more unskilled workmen being
employed together 1
