The above remarks are intended to indirectly con-
vey the fact that this is the last week of the
“ King's Musketeer ” at Her Majesty’s (Sydney).
“ Secret Service ” follows on Saturday. This play
is new to Australia, but certain encouraging
rumors have got here ahead of it, and there is a
general impression that Sydney will give it a great
reception on spec.
*.'#*■*
The pretty and clever, and altogether
pleasant comedy of “Lady Ursula "’ is revived,
for one week only, at Sydney Royal. Persons
who wish to see the lady who put on her brother’s
pants and coat from the purest and noblest
motives, and went out into the wicked world in
these compromising garments, and was slapped
on the back by coarse, ungodly men, are reminded
that to-night (Thursday) and to-morrow night are
their last opportunities. In addition to these
other inducements it may be worth while to
remind the public that the lady is complimented
on her legs, and urged to bury her adorable little
nose in the wine-cup, and cordially invited to
light up the long clay pipe of friendship. Every
well-brought-up female who wants to know how a
perfectly respectable girl should conduct herself
under trying circumstances ought to see “Lady
Ursula ” and derive a lesson therefrom. Some
years ago a very dreary paper started a series
of articles on what a lady should do in em-
barrassing circumstances—say if she unwittingly
offered ham to a Jew, or if an unexpected visitor
came in just when she was falling downstairs with
the coal-scuttle in her hand, or if while she was
smiling on a favored guest in front the dog bit
her from behind. But it never rose to explaining
what a lady should do when circumstances that
have got beyond her control compel her to put on
her brother’s clothes, and drink with four reckless
bachelors late at night in their own bachelor
apartments. “ Lady Ursula ”is a valuable lesson
on feminine deportment in a tight place, and there
is a lesson hidden in it somewhere.
* *
“ The Gay Lord Quex,” by Pinero, makes
its first Australian appearance at Sydney Royal
on Saturday. British rumour speaks well of
“Lord Quex ” as a play wherein a woman comes
so very near to losing her character that the hair
of the audience stands up in pleased and horrified
anticipation. And yet the lady doesn’t lose her
character after all, consequently the audience can
lay its hair down again with the knowledge that it
has had a- beautiful thrill without lending its
countenance to anything in the least degree
immoral.
** * *
“The Shaughraun ”is to'be staged at
Sydney Lyceum on Saturday for one week only.
This sixpenny paper hasn’t been disturbed on its
midnight couch of late by any loud or unusual
demand for Boueicault’s old Irish drama, or if it
was, it mistook the same for the male fowl next
door; and it passes no opinion upon the advisa-
bility or otherwise of the proceeding.
*# * *
The present is Cinquevalli’s very last week
in Sydney, unless he has two or three farewells
later on. The marvellous Paul has had a long run
at the Tivoli, and even at the burnt and ashy fag-
end pf his season business is so good that he may
possibly feel called upon to farewell a little more
before leaving Australia. The very last week in
this country of Bellman and Moore is also an-
nounced, and the community which has lovingly
watched the somersault of the little brown-haired
lady for many months will deeply regret
her departure. Australia in general is pre-
pared to swear that no other brown-haired
lady who ever came this way has done a somer-
sault so gracefully and modestly and promptly as
Lottie Moore, and the spot where her curly head
used to rest on the floor while she went over is
sacred for evermore. The next lady somersaulter
is requested to put her head on some other part
of the floor while her feet are in the air.. That
particular part has become enshrined in the
tenderest feelings of this great country, and to
see any other lady’s head on it would be
sacrilege.

“ The Kelly Gang ” is in its sixth week at
Sydney Criterion, and to all appearance this crude
and boshful horror may run for some time longer.

Alfred Dampier, who has been laid up by
a severe illness for many weeks, is sufficiently well
again to get into harness, a fact which will
gladden the hearts of innumerable friends. Next
week he leaves Sydney on a tour Northwards with
“Robbery tinder Arms,” “Three Musketeers,”
“ Richard III.,” &c, The Dampier co. is due at
Brisbane by 16th September, when it will open
with good old M’Ginnis and O’Hara at the Opera
House.
** * *
At St. George’s Hall the Human Frog
goes on his way contorting, the cake-walkers still
perambulate in couples, and the female imper-
sonator makes the females in front of the house
feel rather unhappy. It is hard for a woman to
find delight in the imitation of her winning ways
by a man when she considers her own awful
inability to retaliate. Also, the performance
comes rough upon Angelina’s best boy, who has
been enslaved by the little tricks and manners
which he thought peculiar to the sex until he saw
them given away by a man comedian. The gaiety
of nations gains nothing from female imper-
sonators. Woman, alas! is a cheap theatrical
animal in her merely alluring aspects, but man-
kind feels none the better for knowing that the
course of true love is a matter of stage business.
#* * *
“A Stranger in New York,” at the
Princess’s, frivols around an invisible French ball
with moderate results. Further consideration of
the frothy cock-tailed entertainment in three
acts reveals no cause for giving it a hearty
welcome. As a comedy it is weaker than even
“The Belle of New York,” which left a pleasant
taste in the mouth, anyhow. This narrative of an
inane struggle for a “good time” leaves a bad
taste in the mouth. The characters are an
unwholesome crowd, and the people who fill the
characters don’to furnish a smart enough variety
show to keep one from wondering what’s the
matter with ’Murkan life among the gilded
classes.
*# * *
The return of Cinquevalli is understood to
be nearly due at Melb. Bijou, but meanwhile
Richard Douglass, about the most popular atom
in the bill, delivers a temperance address to fellow-
drinkers. Douglass dances, too, in one of his new
turns, and his breezy suggestions of legs harmonise
with the rest of the little man’s humor. Another
very abbreviated joke, little Spry (of the firm of
Spry and Austin) leaves Melbourne for Sydney
this week.
** * *
On Saturday the Alexandra will open its
doors again, and a season of grand opera—short,
or long, according to how Melbourne takes to the
idea —will start with “ Faust. ” Miss Bertha Bird,
Louis Luscombe, Rebottaro and Ernest Sullivan,
said to be a beauteous tenor, are cast for the prin-
cipal parts, and the opera, which has had long re-
hearsal, will be conducted by James Ure.
** * *
Marshall Hall’s second orchestral concert
happens at the Town Hall to-night (Thursday),
but owing to a horticultural show Scherek’s usual
Saturday afternoon concert will be given at the
Bijou this week. Mdlle. Trebelli starts her song
recitals at the Town Hall on the 31st, and Amy
Castles will give a farewell warble there on
Wednesday, Sept. 6th, skipping for Europe next
day.
** * *
Rev. Father Robinson, the present guide,
philosopher and business manager of. Miss Amy
Castles, published last week a return showing the
amounts “realised,” i.e., nett profits from the
damsel’s boom up to date. It seems that the
three Castles concerts promoted by public com-
mittees in Melbourne, Bendigo and Ballarat
brought .£944 (average £315), including £lOO, or
more, privately donated to the fund: apart from
donations £2Bl per concert. The 13 concerts pro-
moted by Father G. A. Robinson have brought
about £3lOO, estimating the Brisbane receipts at
£3OO (average £240). Melbourne people are particu-
larly struck by the fact that four Robinson-managed
concerts in the Adelaide Exhibition building appear
to have realised only £475, although hundreds
of people, said the Melb. press pars., were turned
away from each concert. The Bendigo committee
had shown £204 for one concert, and the Mel-
bourne committee netted nearly £5OO by its one
concert for Miss Castles. Again, there are the
Sydney figures of £1036 from three concerts en-
gineered by Father Robinson and others in the
great Centennial Hall, which seats between
four and five thousand people (£345 per con-
cert). Considering the rush for five-shilling
tickets for the first of those three concerts
and the reported crowds at the other two
concerts, many people are surprised at the finan-
cial result. It would seeir, that the Castles Boom
has been extravagantly managed on tour, or else
that it wasn’t anything like the Boom it appeared
to be in Melbourne press pars. The publication
of full balance-sheets showing gross receipts and
expenditures would be interesting.
** * #
Item No. 4 in the winter series of
“wonderful talent ” from Bendigo arrived at
Melb. Town Hall on Saturday. The novelty in
this case was a Miss Alice Crawford who elocutes
with more than ordinary effect, and is altogether
a very likely angel to achieve distinction as an,
actress, if she wants to go upon'the styge, and will
be prepared to go slow at the start. Miss Craw-
ford recited an assortment of pieces successfully.
Other girls can do as much as that, and the world
is not very anxious to hear them make the
attempt in a Town Hall. The Bulletin has no
enthusiasm to waste on Miss Crawford’s mere
recitations, but if she would like to know whether
she possesses the necessary charm, strength,
dramatic instinct, aud magnetism to make an
important actress in the future this journal’s
opinion is Yes, with a capital Y. Miss Crawford’s
show included three or four vocalists, also
Benno Scherek at the piano, and the gem of the
entertainment was a speech by Scherek apolo-
gising for his evening appearance in the frock
coat he had worn at his afternoon orchestral con-
cert. He intended to wear a lovely dress-suit for
the occasion, he said, but somehow his dazzling
claw-hammer and continuations had been mis-
carried by the train. However, he managed to
play the accompaniments as beautifully as usual.
Among the singers, basso W. G. Barker, being in
good form, took first honours because he has a fine
voice and a finished style of producing it, and
would be an almost great artist if he didn’t wear
a tall stiff collar and an inflexible aspect. He
warbled of the damp, rolling deep as though he
were a bank director reading a lot of dry statistics.
Miss Lili Sharp gave four songs very creditably,
and a Miss Iva Lynne appeared as a new con-
tralto. The one thing wanting in Miss Lynne is
a large musical voice. Gifted with a nice new
organ of sound she’d be an interesting young lady,
but under present conditions she labours in vain.
Why not learn to play the violin, or something ?
** * *
The theatres are mostly getting along with
the same old bill as before. At the Royal, the
evil designs of a bad bank director continue to be
furthered by the night watchman for the imagi-
nary sake of his child, and the wall of the strong-
room is laid low by nitro-glycerine six times a
week regularly. Also Miss Gill persists in wooing
Bland Holt by storm, and Miss Ireland relent-
lessly ensnares the inebriated youth from New
Guinea, and Mrs. Holt captures the bank manager,
whilst Walter Baker is putting Miss Ross to his
credit. The “War of Wealth” altereth not.
This afternoon (Thursday), at 2 o’clock sharp,
Miss Sophie Osmond, a penlady who looks plump
and pleasing in her advt. photographs, will take a
benefit at the Royal. The programme promises to
be amusing, and inasmuch as the average jour-
nalist dies without having dreamed of a benefit,
enterprising Miss Osmond deserves credit for
arousing the tired fraternity and sisternity of
puff paragraphists to a sense of what they want.
In a land of chivalry and dreams, the France of
auld lang syne
(We see it through a gorgeous haze of beauty, love
and wine)
King Louis had a motley guard of drinky warriors
three;
Their arms were long ; their hair was long ; their
swords were long and free.
They sang and laughed and drank red wine in
taverns old and grey;
They hugged fair women as they passed upon their
careless way.
They left big footprints down the vale of many
dusky years,
We drop a tear upon their bones —King Louis’s
Musketeers.
They fought against whatever odds might chance
to come their way;
They didn’t ask the reason why, but went into the
fray.
They loved whatever girl might come if she was
young and fair;
And never asked whose girl she was because they
didn’t care.
They gaily stepped on Cardinals who got into
their road;
They stepped on kings as well—in fact, they
nearly stepped on God,
They were but spectres in a book, and yet three
husky cheers
We give them even now—King Louis’s gaudy
Musketeers.
At Poverty Point.
“ The 500th night of the ‘ Belle of New York,”’ writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “ was another
triumph in audacity of the phlegmatic and imperturbable
George Musgrove. Right in the larynx of all English
precedent no souvenir of any kind was given to the
audience. It is a sensible and exceptional innovation. As
the theatre will only hold so many no souvenirs could get
any more in, and the ,‘ Belle’ draws like Phil May at his
best. There is only one B. of N.Y., and that’s Edna
May, and I never pitied my country before until a sub-
stitute was ‘ rung in ’ on ‘ you below.’ For E.M. is the
‘ subject of all the town talk,’ and may be later the wife
of Mr. John Cavendish (nephew of Duke of Devonshire).”
“ D.J.” : Some of ‘bese nights there will be murder at
Sydney Theatre Royal. i'Le last night of “The Second Mrs.
Tanqueray ” about three hundred tons of female hog
hauled itself along the darkened corridor and rustled its
silk petticoats, and shuffled its large flat feet, and
wheezed, and discussed Mrs. Brown’s bonnet and Mrs.
Smith’s opera cloak in the dressing-room in a voice that
drowned the play.
Amy Castles struck Adelaide just when half the holy
city was making imaginary piles out of the Westralian
jump and Far North telluride specs., and the artless little
thing went away £475 to the good. But Amy’s perform-
ance was just crude enough to encourage thoughts of
exporting a couple of locally-grown prodigies—Lulu
Gillespie and Meta Buring. The former is, alas 1 noto-
riously married, and so can’t be put into short frocks
and starred as 16 or under ; the other, who hasn’t com-
mitted matrimony, is already being made the subject of
a fund. It was Amy Sherwin who claimed the discovery
of Miss Buring—“a pure contralto,” she told a Sydney
interviewer, “who ought to go to Europe for training,
for she has the makings of a fine singer.” But not till
the Castles boom showed what things are possible did a
European training seem anything better than a dream.
Miss Lulu Gillespie, the S. Australian soprano, who will
shortly go to Europe for training (with a little public help
perhaps; though in this ease it is understood not to be
absolutely essential) owns to 22 years of life. Though
Mrs. Albert Fairbairn in private, she has kept her girl’s-
name for amateur-theatrical purposes. Burra copper-
field was her birth-place; it was Mdme. Albani who
encouraged her to look much farther afield than that.
Assuming that Amy Castles were the possible “ future
Melba ” she was said to be when the Boom started, the
apology offered by the Rev. Father Robinson for taking
her raw marvellous voice round the country is very
amusing. Says the damsel’s musical and ghostly adviser :
“ During the whole of this time Miss Castles and her
friends have had the services of a throat specialist, and
the greatest care has been taken to see that the young
lady did not appear on the concert platform under con-
ditions that would by any possibility be injurious to her.
The limit has now, however, been reached.” Apart from
everything else, what did Miss Castles’ friends want a
throat specialist for? Even if they were all as hoarse as
crows, the public isn’t interested about their throats.
“Ada Colley,” writes The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, “ is perhaps the most eclipsing star of music-
halldorn ever ejected from the Southern Cross. She has
paralysed Europe—Paris, Berlin, St. Petersburg—puzzled,
at a high price, London, at the leading hall —the Empire,
and, doubtless by now, mortified the country that let her
go—because it couldn’t ‘ keep ’ her. Maybe she was, and
is, only a freak vocally, but her success and salary are such
that every flap-eared, reputationised critic is frothing to
convince his ‘ swearers-by ’ that Ada is really (‘cut “me”
throat if I lie’) a genuine vocalistio genius. To Will
Crackles, who (in certain senses of the word) steered her
here, Miss C. is indebted much, but Will is feeling feeble
just now for fear he should be tempted once more to the
Land where it’s never so sunny-hot as in London o’ June
days.”
“Of others known to Bulletiners,” continues our
correspondent, “ Bert Gilbert goes to the ‘ Palace,’ fol-
lowing in the tracks of his best pal—and wife —AdaKeeve,
who, as you rightly recently pointed out, has an enorm-
ously enhanced reputation since her return from Aus-
tralia. Increased vitality, appearance, and nous are the
secrets, however, of such balloonjstic. leaps after Aus-
tralian tours. Harry Gribben, the much-buried, abso-
lutely refuses to keep his shroud on, and gets among the
‘ quick’ from the dead with much more fat —financial and
fizical —than the worms had formerly a hope of cutting
their eye-molars in. He is flourishing like a banana-tree,
and has too much grip on the earth to ever get off it.
Finally, the Permans have transplanted the‘old folks’
back to this bone-sown soil, and accepted engagements
so far into the twentieth century that the name of the
trio is to be changed to the Permanents, to give the
agents a chance of getting their commissions. All the
Florries (Young, Forde and Esdaile) are ‘flourishing,’
more or less ; and A. G. Poulton is still the marvel of
Australia abroad.”
“Constance Clyde”: The Bulletin theatre - man
laments that Sydney crowds go to see the dreadful
deeds of the “ Kelly Gang,” and probably scents blood-
thirsty tendencies in that lamentable fact. Let him be
calm ; it is merely the localism that attracts. Recipe for
writing an Australian novel: Mix in plenty of casual
allusions to Woolloomooloo and Burragorang, and things
like that, and there you are. Similarly with plays—lug
in the chronic bluegum, and get the characters to say
things about Manly, and likewise remark that the Aus-
tralian girl knows how to take care of herself ; which is
just what the Australian girl doesn’t know'—she knows
everything else—and there you are again. That is the
explanation of Young Australia’s dreadful rush to the
Criterion, not any sentimental sympathy with the unfor-
tunate gang who only asked to be allowed to make off
with their neitrhbors’ cattle in peace and quietness, but
whom a remorseless law persecuted into the tomb.
Re present “ Kelly Gang” boom. The first dramatisa-
tion of this classic subject was produced in Wellington,
M.L., in 1880, within 60 hours of the gang’s burst-up.
It was written by Jack Utting, then on N.Z. Times staff,
and “ Ned ” was played by R. Love, now of Harmston’s
Circus.
Culchawed Prof. Tucker, of Melb. University, lecturing
recently on the “Supreme Literary Art ” : “Music, too,
we are told, can interpret the action of war; but the
action is usually found on the annotated margin of the
programme.” At hearing which, an unmistakable wave
of Philistine approval passed over an audience wearied of
Marshall Hall’s wire-drawn transcendentalism—of the
“prophetic bar,” and the “unsounded note,” which by
its awful omission spells Fate 1
Royal and Princess managements might, had they
chosen, have worked off an interesting little experiment
on the public during the run of “The King’s Musketeer”
in Melbourne. Henrietta Watson, at the Princess’s, was
so like Elizabeth Watson at the Royal, that it would have
been worth while to make an exchange of leading ladies
for one consecutive night, just to see if the patrons
would discover the fraud.
“There were kings before Agamemnon!” When
Cinquevalli was a boy, 30 years ago, a French juggler,
named Agoust, was the talk of Europe. With the scanty
paraphernalia of 4 bottles, 4 oranges, and a small square
of board, he astounded audiences in all the big cities of
Europe and America. He eventually joined the Hanlon-
Lees, and with them produced those pantomimic sketches,
such as the “ Voyage en Suisse,” which became so popu-
lar in London.
The superior musical critic of Melbourne Herald re-
marks that “ two young ladies joined in a pleasing ren-
dering of the ‘ Gavall'eria ’ Intermezzo, ruthlessly and
irreverently tortured into the shape of an ‘ Ave Maria.’
This outrage on sacred words has surely never been
sanctioned by Masoagni.” Why, the Intermezzo music
was written for an Ave Maria ! The dropping of it into the
opera as a link between the first and second acts was an
afterthought of Mascagni’s.
Possible that Adelaide Theatre Royal will have another
lessee in 1900, as Wybert Reeve contemplates retirement
at Xmas. Adelaide’s only other theatre, the Bijou, now
leased by Hudson, will shortly be tenantless. A chance
for Rickards.
What has become of “ The Christian ” ? Its production
in Australia was promised months ago ; in fact, it was
pronounced almost ready for production when Wilson
Barrett was here. Where is the hitch ? Asa matter of
fact, Barrett told a friend, when he was in Melbourne,
that he had not even started on the dramatic version, but
meant to begin it in Adelaide.
“ Medi-cuss” : The long-haired “ Oliver Bainbridge ” is
not a native of Toowoomba, Q. The distinguished
pitcher of fairy-tales was born and raised in Grafton,
Clarence River, where his father is a civil servant.
“ Bainbridge ” once astonished Grafton Bench by setting
up a unique defence when charged with assault. The
only Oliver, having been born tired, refused to work for
his keep, so his father “gave him the bounce.”
However, as fast as the old man fired his hopeful
out of the front door he would slip in at the ba<k.
This game went on till, at last, “ Bainbridge,” Esq.,
proceeded to urge his views on the sire with a club. At
the Police Court he calmly asserted that, as he was only
18 years old, he, therefore, was in the eyes of the law an
infant and his father was bound to provide for him. A
bowelless P.M. refused to take this most reasonable view
and Oliver had to pay the piper. The young man has
really excellent natural talents, a good public school
education and elocutionary powers far above the aveiage.
He also sketches fairly well and has a memory in a
thousand. This scribe had the distinguished honor of
entertaining the wanderer for a few days just prior to his
starting on his pilgrimage, but at that time he billed him-
self as the “Marvellous Boy Orator.” One of the most
apropos things ever seen was one of his programmes
stuck on a bellows in a local store.
From “ M.A.P.” : “ What is Sarah Bernhardt’s secret!?
What is it that keeps her up? What makes her the
wonder of her sex, her profession, and the world? Here
she is well past her prime, a youthful grandmother, arid
still an inexhaustable force. She is always playing, never
rests, never tires, and does the eternal round of her
‘ repertoire ’ without ‘ ennui ’or fatigue. How does she
do it? Well, to begin with, Sarah is an enormous eater.
She will sup ‘ en prince’ with her friends, but when they
have gone, and the lights are out, and she has at last got
to her own room, she will set to work feeding again, and
will cook four or five eggs hard, and devour them before
she retires for the night! ’’ But, after all, it is not merely
because Sarah eats so enormously that she is so strong ;
the secret is in her splendid digestion.

Norman Godkin, of 31 Queen-street, Melbourne,
is prepared to purchase or develope, with option of
purchase, “ big prospecting shows ” (either gold or
mineral) in any part of Australia. Properties offered
must be subject to inspection.*
Mephan Ferguson, engineer, boiler-maker, etc.,
of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, has just secured a
contract for the manufacture and supply of all the bolts,
nuts and washers which may be required by the Victorian
railways during- the next three years. He has also con-
tracted to supply all the carbonic acid gas necessary for
working the railway refrigerating and ice-making plant—
this is one of I. and E. Hall’s patent carbonic anhydride-
machines, the same system as has been adopted by the
Govts, of South Australia, Queensland, and Western Aus-
tralia. Mr. Ferguson has a large stock of these machines
(suitable for meat-preserving co.’s dairies, butchers, and
others) on hand at present, which he is selling at greatly-
reduced prices.*

COLUMN
Three provincial Treasurers have now issued
their estimated revenue and expenditure for 1899-1900.
This is how the three look forward :
Estimated Estimated Estimated
Revenue. Expenditure. Surplus.
Maoriland « £5,300,000 £5,059,729 £240,271
Victoria „ 7,156,225 7,136,755 19,470
Estimated
Deficit.
N.S. Wales .. 9,782,643 9,909,371 £126,728
Seddon’s is the only attempt at respectable financing
among the three. He has no old deficit to pay off, but
he aims at having a little over for public works purposes.
The only mistake is that he ought to aim at having at
least half-a-million over ; or, better still, he ought to pass
a law making the annual payment of something between
£500,000 and £1,000,000 into the public works fund a
fixed and absolutely cast-iron charge upon the
revenue. N.S.W.’s expenditure includes a payment
of £150,000 a year off the old deficit, so if
things go as Treasurer Carruthers expects he will pay
£150,000 off the old deficit and have a new deficit of
£126,728 —therefore he will really reduce the accumulated
shortage by only £23,272. In Victoria there is no fixed
annual payment in reduction of the old deficit—the Trea-
surer simply applies to that purpose whatever sum he
thinks fit out of his surplus, if he has one. And Turner,
by reducing the income-tax and the railway freights, and
providing no new source of revenue in lieu thereof, leaves
himself in such a position that, if his estimates pan out as
expected, he will have only j 19,470 to reduce the old
deficit with. His only hope of paying anything off the
venerable burden lies in the chance that he may have
under-estimated the year’s receipts. Why Turner doesn’t
make a payment of, say, £200,000 a year" towards the old
shortage a fixed charge on the revenue passes under-
standing.
* * * *
Premier Reid, of N.S.W., lately had the ghastly
effrontery to write to Premier Seddon, of Maoriland, that
“ the new system of finance and fiscal policy inaugurated
by the present (Reid) Government in 1895 has shown in
every one of the four years since a credit balance
after paying in reduction of the deficiency sums
amounting to about half-a-million.” And the Auditor-
General, who is the last court of appeal on N.S.W.
finance, sets forth on page 154 of his report for 1898 that
Premier Reid had a huge deficit (over ±400,000) in 1896,
and another big shortage in 1897. Also, that, so far from
paying half-a-miilion off the deficit, that burden was
larger at June, ’9B, than when Reid took office. And tw'o
or three days before this report appeared, George Reid
fled from the Treasury, and took refuge in the quiet
billet of Attorney-General. The Attorney-General isn’t
audited.
» * * •
The Victorian [Government has decided to face
the problem of its insolvent irrigation and water trusts.
When the Gillies-Deakin Ministry took to irrigation with
a rush in the boom t mes it put its headjdown and ran at
the problem bull-fashion. Labor was expensive then ;
materials ditto ; management was ditto and loose into
the bargain ; and for want of sufficient knowledge the
works were built at great expense all round. Very few of
the trusts have been able to keep up their interest pay-
ments, and the money they owe the State is, largely, a
bad debt. Out of £1,024,552 capital advanced by the
State to the trusts £535,217 is to be written off or “sus-
pended” indefinitely; and out of £332,707 arrears of
interest £332,627 is to be written off, leaving only £BO
recoverable. But as regards the balance, the trusts are to
be made to walk a relentless chalk-line. When a trust
falls in arrear hereafter the Audit Commissioners are
bound by law to put a bailiff in, and levy a large enough
rate to meet the interest and arrears, and otherwise wake
up the defaulter in a most violent fashion.
m m m *
A feeble attempt was recently made in Maori-
land Parliament by a private member (Millar, of Dunedin)
to pass a Bill providing that no new life insurance co.
should be allowed to start in the province without a paid-
up capital of at least ±lO,OOO. It went where it de-
served to do —into the waste-paper basket. All attempts
to make life insurance business safe by providing that a
co. must have so much paid-up capital are only a feeble
buzzing round the outside of the problem. A co.
with a small business and £5OOO capital may be
safe ; one with a large business and £lOO,OOO capi-
tal may be rotten. A life assurance co. with
£5OOO capital intact and safely invested, and
with perhaps £lOO,OOO of assurers’ funds also safely
invested, may be splendidly sound; one with £50,000
capital invested in shaky mortgages and £lOO,OOO assur-
ance fund mostly in depreciated freeholds may be utterly
rotten. Moreover, there is very little good in providing
for the safety of any assurance offices which may start
hereafter, when Maoriland has already the most lament-
able assurance co. in Australasia on its hands. What is
wanted is a rigid State audit and valuation of all assur-
ances offices, present and future, and an enactment that
no assurance office shall base its estimates on a future
assumed interest rate of more than 3£ per cent.

The First Sydney and N.S.W. Tontine Invest-
ment Fund (whose head-quarters are at 20 Vickery’s
Chambers, 76 Pitt-street, Sydney), is an interesting
gamble in life and death. The idea isn’t new in these
provinces, there being already two such institutions in
Maoriland. The principle is simple enough. A certain,
or uncertain, number of members insure their lives for
any sum not less than £250 or more than £5OOO, each,
and assign their policies to the Fund. (The Perpe-
tual Trustee Co., a most reliable institution with a
large capital, is to hold'the policies and all' other assets,
so the safety of the cash ought to be sufficiently provided
for.) When the amount of the policies assigned
reaches £250,000, the fund closes. Then it sits
down to wait for 20 years dating from Ist September,
1899. All that each member has to do is to pay
his premium regularly and pray for the death of
the other members. During the 20 years, whenever a
member dies, the Trust collects the amount of Ins policy ;
pays back to his representatives the sum which he paid
in premiums, but without any interest; and keeps the
balance, which will be either invested in good-looking
securities or in endowment policies on the lives of sur-
viving members. Said endowments will be arranged to
expire before the close of the Tontine, so that all the
assets can be turned into cash for division. And, at the
end of the 20 years, each member then living gets back
his own policy', which becomes his own property again,
and also his share (according to the size of his policy) in
the profit made out of the premature death of his part-
ners.
* * * *
Everything seems to depend on the unhealthi-
ness of the members. If, out of the £250,000 worth of
policies held by the fund, £125,000 worth fall in by death
during the £2O years, there will probably, after handing
back to the represents ives of the deceased members
the full amount of the premiums paid by the late
lamented, be from £60,000 to £lOO,OOO to divide among
the survivors when the fund closes. This is assuming
that the fund fills, and that the money as it comes in is
well invested. If, by any utterly incredible ill-luck,
none of the members died in the 20 years, there would be
no profit for anybody—only' the loss involved by working
expenses. If very few died there wouldn’t be much profit.
If the bubonic plague broke out, and the members all
died except one, he ought to strike immense wealth, no
matter how small his policy' was. If the plague was so bad
that the members all died it doesn’t seem to be explained
who would get the cash—presumably the heirs of that
last man would take it all. If the Tontine is nearly
closing, and there are only two survivors, and one dies
suddenly under suspicious circumstances, the other
will probably be arrested right there. If the man
who died suspiciously had a £5OOO policy, and the
other a £250 policy, then the £250 man, by dropping a
brick judiciously on the head of the £5OOO man, would
get the whole fund instead of less than a twentieth of it,
which would make the death of the £5OOO policy-holder
all the more suspicious. If the fund was £200,000, then
the small policy-holder would have rather over £190,000 to
gain by murdering the large one.
It is provided that every member is to insure
his life on the “ 20-year limited payment plan with pro-
fits,” and on every £lOO of insurance he has to pay 2s. per
annum to cover expenses. The Bulletin doesn’t know
in what investments the funds are to be deposited, but
they are specified in the Trust Deed which intending
members can inspect. If the investments are safe,
then the Tontine seems an innocent gamble in
the diseases of one’s fellow-man. Every healthy
young man who joins should dissuade all other healthy
young men from joining, and urge all the people whose
health he has doubts about to go in for large sums. Only
it needs to be clearly specified who gets the money if the
members all die. And The Bulletin, while noting that
Mr. H. G. Scott is “general agent” for the Tontine, re-
grets that it doesn’t know that gentleman from a crow
(not that that is anything to his discredit, for most of the
people The Bulletin knows are anything but respectable,
and Mr. H. G. Scott isn’t responsible for this paper’s want
of knowledge of him, and it is only this paper’s beastly
ignorance, anyhow. The fact that the sound and emi-
nently reliable Perpetual Trustee Co. is mixed up with
the affair should be quite sufficient guarantee for Mr.
Scott’s bond-fides). Still another item is that “ all
policies are to be effected through the general
agent of the fund, Mr. H. G. Scott.” If that
means that he gets the agent’s commission, then
it seems to this paper that he will make a
very handsome sum of ± 5000 or more. And if he doesn’t
get the commission who does? Of course, there is no
possible reason why Mr. Scott shouldn’t get the commis-
sion as well as anybody else—still, if one of the big
Trustee Co.’s would start a Tontine on its own account,
and arrange that the £5OOO or so commission on the
policies should form the nucleus of the fund for ultimate
distribution among the survivors—well, at the end of 20
years, with compound interest, there would be from
£lO,OOO to £20,000 the more to divide among the sur-
vivors. And that is a point of some little importance.
* « * *
The Equitable Life Assurance Society of New
York sends along a little book in which is detailed every
separate mortgage which the society owns (location,
value of property, and amount lent); all its bonds, stocks,
and securities, with the book-value and present market-
value of each, and the interest which each investment
produces ; its office properties—in fact, every asset down
to the very smallest. As a guarantee of the soundness of
the investments this is a valuable idea. And it would be
a very valuable shock to old imported British prejudices
if Australian societies would take the public into their
confidence in like manner.
* * * *
A company, capital JJ170,000 in 170,000 <£l
shares, is being formed to buy up the sole rights for Aus-
tralia of the Dunlop pneumatic tyre. The Australian
branch of the Dunlop tyre business was formed in 1893,
and the profits in 1897 were £11,975. In 1898 they were
£14,708. The public is offered 80,000 7 per cent, prefer-
ence shares, on which the dividend will be cumulative.
The price to be paid for the business is £135,000 —£45,000
cash ; £20,000 in 20,000 7£ per cent, second preference
shares on which the divirlend is not cumulative ; and
£70,000 in 70,000 ordinary shares. The fact that the
shares held by the public have an absolute first claim on
the profits up to 7 per cent is a good thing for
the public. Also the fact that the vendors are
willing to take the bulk of their money in shares
which have no claim whatever for dividends till the
public has got its 7 per cent, looks as if they had plenty
of faith in the venture. It is provided, by the way, that
the dividend on ordinary shares is not to exceed 9 per
cent. The 7 per cent, dividend to preference share-
holders (£5600), 7£ per cent, to second preference ditto
(£1500), and 9 per cent, to ordinary (±6300), would absorb
£13,400 a year, and the highest profits, so far, have been
£14,708. It is intended to set aside one-fourth of the net
profits, after payment of dividends on the 90,000 pre-
ference shares, to form a reserve-fund of £40,000, and the
co. certainly ought also to make provision for writing-off
the goodwill, which u'ill be a huge paper item among its
assets, for all that goodwill w'ill be dust and ashes some
day—no one knows how soon —when some new invention
makes the Dunlop tyre obsolete.
* * • *
Two English companies are negotiating to buy
up the antiquated private horse-trams of Adelaide for
£214,000, with the promise to abolish the attenuated
plates of meat that now haul the vehicles along and estab-
lish an electric system. But they ask that the State
shall give up all rights of compulsory purchase for at
least 21 years, and if the State knows what it is about it
will refuse to have any dealings on these terms, and will
prefer to take over the business itself. The example of
Melbourne’s private tramway co , with its long hours and
miserable wages and evil repute generally, should make
every other capital swear off tram syndicates.
* * * *
The Ashton Magazine Rifle Co. (Lim.) seems to
have dispersed a cloud of mystery which was beginning
to excite suspicion. This is the little co. formed two
years ago for the purpose of selling Victorian inventor
Ashton's patent-rights in Europe, and the shareholders
naturally wanted to know what had happened to their
subscribed capital (±2500), their agent Pudney, and
Ashton himself in the meantime. At the recent Melb.
meeting a rather extraordinary course of business came
to light. The whole £2500 seems to have been placed to
the credit of airent Pudney, who was followed to England
by inventor Ashton. “A certain amount of ill-feeling
arose” between the two men, and for about 18 months or
so they snorted at one another whilst the shareholders in
distant Melbourne were waiting anxiously for results.
The “ directors” of the 00., who have no visible power in
the matter, are now able to report that Ashton and
Pudney have “settled their long-standing differences”
and are again playing speaks “ in the interest of the com-
pany.” If they don’t resume their quarrel, and if certain
alterations in the rifle are carried out, and if nothing
occurs to prevent some public trials of the weapon as
arranged by Pudney aforesaid, the shareholders are not
unlikely to 'hear of something to their advantage. Any-
way, they seem to be the lawful owners of patent rights
in a rifle which is possibly a valuable invention, seeing
that Ashton’s first trials of it in Melbourne made a favor-
able impression. By the way, Detective Dungey has re-
signed his little seat on the board.
* » * *
Silver ton Tramway Co. proposes to throw a sop
to Cerberus —-Cerberus in this case being the Broken Hill
people, who have begun prodding their members to bar-
rack harder for a Govt, line to compete with the Silverton
Co.’B. Adelaide-Broken Hill merchants requisitioned for
a reduction of the goods tariff ; and the manager’s reply
was almost effusive—the directors, he felt, would be
delighted to oblige. The vast bulk of the co.’s freight is
ore and mine requisites ; and, as it is not proposed to
touch these, the huge profits will not be affected to a
degree worth mentioning. Last year 600,000 tons of stuff
passed over the line, of which only about 20,000 tons was
merchandise of the classes now mentioned ; and it has
just been announced that so far this year the figures of
1898 have been exceeded.

For the first time in its history the Mnama and
Deniliquin Railway Co. passed a half-yearly meeting
without a div. The experience was due partly to drought,
partly to the introduction of the Westinghouse brake —
from which latter it may be inferred that the co. is be-
coming riotously extravagant. Still, despite all this, 3j
per cent, profit was earned ; but railway shareholders in
Australia would only be insulted by 3J per cent.

' “ Ginger ”: You were wrong in your last week’s
estimate of the quantity of tailings at the Occidental
mine. There are at present over 60,000 tons awaiting
treatment. Of course, this means that the management
will not go for all the clean sand at once, but will have a
fair amount of slime mixed in. Should the directorate
not work on the lines indicated above, they vi ill have a
successful run for, say, six months on clean sand, and
then worse than misery, or be compelled to spend a few
thousands on a slime plant.
* * * *
B. H. North Central, which looked like dying
any time up to a few months ago, kicks again more vig-
orously than for years past. The directors “ congratulate
the shareholders on the improved prospects ” and think
there is “ much better hope of success than at any pre-
vious period.” This is on the strength of what is called
an indicator vein ; anyway it is “ exactly similar to the
indicator in the Gonsols mine when in transition rock.”
But whatever it is, it is some guide to prospecting ; before
they fell in with this they were groping blindly. There
may not be anything in the shape of payable ore shoots
along its course ; but the Consols experience encourages
them to see. Certainly it would be foolish to throw up
the prospect at this stage. The co. has about £4OOO to
get along with, though it isn’t all available at call.

Mt. David, N.S.W., 400 tons for 1340 z. The

• * * »
Hillgrove, N.S.W., is a long-languishing gold-
camp which has seen good days, and isn’t likely to see
any better. There is no disguising the fact that the
Eleanora is in a bad way from want of development and
from want of a little more gold to the ton. It only
manages to pay its way under great difficulties, and
another reconstruction is far more certain than another
div. The Baker’s Creek is on fairly good stone at
Nos. 7, 11 and 12 levels. Vein in bottom much
pinched, but the mine manages to squeeze out an
occasional div. Baker’s Creek Consols has dis-
charged nearly all hands for the present; putting
in a new boiler and erecting pumping-plant prior
to sinking that main shaft for another lift. West Sun-
light has shut down ; trying to raise fresh capital while
resting. The energetic manager Martyn, has resigned ;
is said to have tried to keep the show going out of his own
pocket, but jibbed at last. Sunlight : Still grinding
away at very low-grade ore, thus managing to evade
calls without any prospects of a div. Hillgrove Proprie-
tary has got over 160 ft. into the gorge with the crosscut ;
making for the Golden Gate reefs. This co. must have
spent upwards of £20,000, and would no doubt like to see
its money back.
» * * *
Gallymont (N.S.W.) tributors are still working
the mine at a profit, and want an extension of time to
sink the main shaft, which would imply that they are
only doing well by following narrow shoots of ore, and
that the mine is what writer said of it years ago—made
up of narrow shoots with considerable intervening blanks.
Bimbimbie, another of the Anglo-Australian alleged great
mines, is also being worked by tributors. Another at
Florida, not far from Cohar, has been shut down, pro-
bably for a long rest. In fact, outside the Gibraltar, the
A.-A. Co. seems to have got terrible knocks.
•*• * *
The Wilfley concentrator has only been a short
time before the mining public, but has already demon-
strated its superiority, over 1200 being sold within the
past two years. -This machine has a capacity' of from
25 to 30 tons per day, and will save more sulphuretts,
float gold, amalgam or quicksilver, and make a cleaner
and better separation than any belt concentrator made.
It can be easily' and rapidly adjusted while in motion, and
requires no expert attention. The Austral Otis Engineer-
ing Coy., South Melb, have been appointed sole manufac-
turing agents, and are now engaged constructing machines
for the colonies. The price of the machine is £ll5.

Great Cobar Copper Mine, N.S.W., has become
a colossal affair. Weekly production from four furnaces,
nearly 80 tons of copper, 300 oz. gold, and 3000 oz. silver.
A fifth furnace is being fitted for pyritic smelting.
Monthly tonnage, between 10,000 and 12,000 tons : mine
working three levels (54, 74 and 90 fathoms), and prepar-
ing for the next.

from the Govt, works a little over 50z., and a report that

copper. The mine-owner used much blue language at
the Govt, works.
» * * *
Of 500 or so claims exploited in Broken Hill dis-
trict during the past 10 years only two off the main lode
have paid divs.—the Consols and the Little Broken Hill.
In earlier day's, with silver at 4s. 6d., half-a-dozen others
made a trifle; but that was mostly from rich surface
stuff. The Little Broken Hill has just paid its first; it is
owned by an unpretentious little local co., which has a
fair lead body in the Rockwell Paddock to work on—the
district which was once broken-up by a huge English co.,
that spent £5OO on reports and £3OO on a prospecting pit,
and then liquidated. The most remarkable thing of all
about the Little Broken Hill, though, is that it got a
grant from the Prospering Vote, and paid it back—the
first instance on reoord of a silver-lead mine doing suoh a
daring thing.
* • • *
Overflow (N.S.W.) : Thirty hands discharged
“pending a meeting of shareholders.” This co. must
surely face reconstruction, and the sooner the necessity
is recognised the better for everybody. Mines of this
sort, however well handled, need lots of money, and
thousands seem to have been frittered away in this par-
ticular instance.

Green Hills rush (Etheridge), which during ’96
and ’97 turned out somewhere between 10,000 oz. and
20,0000 z. of gold, mostly in slugs, is now almost deserted.
Pubs, closed, police removed, and the last of the women
and goats about to leave.
A cheerful young bounder, describing himself
as the sub-editor of The Bulletin and a son of a wealthy
Vic. M.L.C., is buzzing around lately with gorgeous gold
mining schemes up his sleeve. When last heard of he
was in Tasmania, inviting Victorian acquaintances, by
wire, to send their cheques for shares in a mine which
Tiie Bulletin —so be represented—had sent him to report
upon. Australia seems to be full of bogus Bulletin men,
and this paper, in a general way, is too tired to protest
against the imposition.
• • • »
David Auraria James, who made such a lucky
start at Broken Hill—he was up that way with his teams
when Hasp pegged out the big mine—now owns, with two
partners, the Kapunda copper-mine. For many years
this one claim marie Kapunda about the most prosperous
S.A. town outside Adelaide. Then, after 22 years’ life,
water poured into the mine in a tremendous flood ; and
soon afterwards the copper-market was knockel-out.
The partners make no stir to get the water under;
most likely J. Bull will be invited to do that.

At a meeting in London of the North Mt. Lyell
Co., Mr. Wm. Jacks, who, by the way, is a Doctor of Laws,
stated the co.’s steamer North Lvell had been sold to the
U.S.S. of M.L. at a profit of a few-thousands. When the
steamer was built Mr. Jacks said the directors expected
to ship 1600 tons of ore a week, but now 1000 tons a day
could be raised ! Taking copper at £5O a ton, they had
±6,000,000 worth of orein sight. The chairman thought the
reason shareholders had not taken up the debentures was
because they thought the shares a safer investment at 6!
A certain Mr. Hennessey who had just returned from his
sixth annual visit to the North Lyell, then made a few
three-star remarks about the modesty of the directors in
stating the value of the mine at £6,C00,000. “ Oh !” said
Brother Jacks, “ that is just the ore in sight calculated at
£5O a ton. THERE WILL BE 20 TIMES THAT BEHIND
IT!” Great Zadkiel Jacks! Hennes-y retorted that a
scientific man told him there was £13,000,000 worth of
ore in sight. Surely these birds study astronomy and
get their figures mixed.
A nice row brewing between New Brothers’
Home and the Briseis Tin co.’s. Mr. Mackenzie, C.E., in
an elaborate report to the former co., advised it to
spend £41,159 in hydraulic sluicing to get at tin-drift
worth, at £lOO a ton, £167,078. Inter alia, Mackenzie
stated there was 400,000 cubic yards of payable drift to
be taken from the Briseis side of the Brothers’ Home
eastern boundary, and he advised arbitration before
encroaching on the Briseis ground. Now the Briseis
directors say they will strenuously oppose any entrance
on their ground by the Brothers’ co. Further, they don’t
want any interference with their tail-race, 1200 ft. long
through basalt.

Dicky Bumble, one of the old aboriginals who
haunt Albany, W.A., showed in that town pieces of coal
which he said he had obtained 40 miles away to
the eastward. He offered to show where he got the coal
for £2O, and a resident took him at his word. Dick a.nd
his woman and the resident drove out to the scene of the
discovery, and sure enough from some holes coal was got,
but, singularly enough, in stray pieces. As there was no
appearance of it being a coal district, Dicky was charged
with salting the holes, and, after pressure, owned up to
having got the coal on Middleton Beach and having
placed it in the holes. The resident, though he had been
taken more than 40 miles on a fool’s errand, laughed
heartily at Dicky’s idea of salting a coal-mine. As a
punishment, however, he allowed Dicky and his woman
to find their way back to town as best they could. Pro-
bably the first case on record of salting a coal-mine.

The fuss continues in Tasmania about the
enormous number of letters and wires sent free by
M’s.P. It is also said that members’ wives (who
get free railway passes as well as their husbands)
send their passes to lady friends—which is likely
to be rough on poor hubby. A shareholder in a
mining co. has written one of the papers saying he
had just got free franked circulars from two mem-
bers, asking him to vote for them as directors of
the co. He says there are 700 shareholders, and if
they all got circulars from the two members the
State lost £5 on the transaction, as members are
entitled to post and wire free only on public busi-
ness. A member stated in the Assembly that
“leaders” were wired free from Hobart to a
Launceston paper. Thirty years ago a row was
made about the Government postage-stamps in
Vic. Parliament, and it was found on inquiry that
one of the largest illicit consumers was James
Palmer, President of the Leg. Council. Just at
that same time Palmer prosecuted a man for steal-
ing a few penno’th of firewood from his back yard
at Hawthorn.

When in criminal cases a jury disagrees
and a re-trial is set down, should the same Judge
“THE STRAIGHT TIP,"
ATTORNEY (to lady client applying for information) after reading over to her the legal
grounds for divorce : “ Has your husband been guilty of any of these acts? If
so, you could get a divorce.”
THE LADY: “ He's a young man I don’t like; but there’s nothing there I could get a
divorce from him for. Rut there’s some things he could get a divorce from
me for. ”
re-try the case ? That has been treated as a puzzle-
question in Sydney during the past week. The
i obvious answer is—The same Judge should be as
. incompetent to preside at the second trial as the
. same jury to sit in the box. Judge and jury will
necessarily have formed opinions at the first trial,
-and. neither would come to the second trial with a
•'•wholly-unprejudiced mind —a right to which
accused is entitled.
•' Disagreeing juries have handicapped liberal
vlaw-reformers ever since the introduction of the
system. In Henry ll.’s time 12 constituted a
jury, but another 12 were sworrl, and if the first 12
disagreed, six were withdrawn and six of the
sworn sent into the box, and so on till a verdict
of 1-2 was secured. Even still in English grand
juries and in trials before the Master in Lunacy
20 jurors are often sworn, a verdict of 12 being
accepted. In 1492 juries were liable to be tried
for their verdict by other juries of 24, and were
punished if decreed guilty of returning a wrong
-verdict. Later, if one juror disagreed with 11,
that one was fined.
The precedence question at issue between
Primate Smith and Cardinal Moran is somewhat
different from such a question in England, and
different now from what the question was 30 years
ago. In England there is an Established Church
which confers precedence ; in Australia no such
ground for precedence exists. But 30 years ago
the Pope was a temporal ruler, with power to
make princes just as Emperor William made
Bismarck one. These princes were necessarily
entitled to their rank everywhere, not in virtue
of being bishops of a church, but because they
were raised to princedom by a valid king-Pope.
That is not the case to-dav, as the Pope is now an
Italian citizen and a subject of King Humbert.
Therefore, Primate Smith lacks a valid claim for
precedence, and so, it appears, does Card. Moran.
Seniority in holding the Sydney see, as proposed
by Gov. Jersey, is the fairest way out of the
difficulty. In the early days, Australian Gover-
nors invited the spiritual heads of the Protestant
and R.C. communities to Govt. House on alternate
dinners, and the R.C. and C.E. prelates said grace
in turns.

The St. Mary’s Cathedral requiem, the
other day, denoted the 16th anniversary of the
death of Archbishop Vaughan. Nearly every
ecclesiastic prominently connected with the Arch-
bishop’s first requiem is now dead. John

antagonistic to the Archbishop are also dead.

A N.Q. hubby, coming home unexpectedly
the other day, found wifey and a batch of other
she’s hanging upon the utterances of a greasy
“ palmist ” ; ordered him out, and, when cheeked
by the ladies’ favorite, smote him violently in the
eye, slung him down the front-steps, and ducked
his head in the water-butt. A lot of approving
house-fathers have pooled to pay any fine that
may be inflicted upon the summary-jurisdiction-
man, but so far the seer has made no sign.

with the dessert—a strapping boy, whose health was
enthusiastically drunk, and who probably had the
record number of godfathers and god-mammas—or has,
for he adorns an adjacent province at the present day.
But that is long ago, and the modern woman is, per-
haps, not quite so hasty. Writer (a medico), however,
has assisted at an impromptu accouchement in a London
’bus on Ludgate Hill—the lady’s sixth. She was apolo-
getic, but distinctly proud of the feat, subsequently
informing this scribe that her second saw the light half-
way up the steps of the Monument, while the third inter-
fered for a while with the progress of a procession in the
Strand. At big maternity institutions the doormat is a
favorite place for scratch obstetrics, discreet nurses
screening the place off from the gaze of an astonished
cabby, who drives off thanking his stars that she had got
out in time. The moral of all this is that ladies in a
certain condition should never leave off their wedding-
rings. One never knows —and the mercy of lovely woman
to her unringed sister in the time of trouble is as the
loving kindness of the crow to the crippled lamb.
Writer knows a Melbourne man who kept
a little pet monkey, of which he was very fond,
a.nd to which he permitted
many liberties. One

ing an interesting book,
and the ape crept to his
shoulder, and began pro-
specting amongst his
hair. The monkey
browsed round for an
hour, and then the reader
discovered a certain irri-
tation, and found his
head bleeding in places.
Result: Blood-poisoning,
erysipelas, and a long,
tough and expensive
struggle for life. That
man is off monkeys now
and for ever.

* * » *
There is one side of the policeman’s-
revolver question that no one seems to touch—the
legal side. Nobody has a legal right to use a
deadly weapon, except in defence of his own life
or property, or to prevent the commission of a
felony. A constable may lawfully kill a man
charged with felony if all other means have been
tried for his arrest without avail; but he may not
so kill a misdemeanant. Similarly, a constable
may kill a prisoner escaping from custody when
all other means of detaining him fail. Uow, no
man who fires a bullet at another can be sure it
will not kill. Therefore the policeman who fires
at a fleeing man before he has used all other means
to take him is a murderer in intent, if not in act.

Henry Hockings writes (in S.M. Herald)
that on the allotment on which Herald office now
stands a hut stood in the early days, and an old
woman’s washing used to hang on a line where now
is the front of the office. Verily a HERALD-like
retrospect.
* * * *
The Sydney axiom, “ If you love me, show
me your revolver,” received new demonstration
last week when the usual young man who was
determined to marry the girl whether she would or
no, called round and insisted on coming in to
worship at the shrine where he wasn’t wanted.
A male friend who happened to have called in on
his way from work pushed him out, whereupon he
is alleged to have rushed off in a cab, bought a
revolver, returned, knocked at the door and when
the same young man attempted to prevent him
entering the house a second time, shot him dead.
He then, it would appear, proceeded to shoot in-
discriminately and wounded the two sisters of the
girl he wanted to transplant to his bosom. He is
said to have tried to shoot her too, and in the door
is a hole which looks suggestive. The world is
full of cranks who are ready to shoot women
because they won’t marry them, and six months
after they do are ready to shoot them because
they did.
* * * *
In the case of the man Griblett, who lately
shot one Williams at Pyrmont (Sydney), one
local paper has introduced a new feature into
the “trial by newspaper” which always follows
such occurrences. Giblett’s account of the affair
is that it was while being man-handled by
Williams he fired the fatal shot; and though, of
course, this may not be true, it is nothing short of
iniquitous to practically contradict the man’s
story by publishing an imaginary view of the
occurrence, in which Giblett is represented as
deliberately “ potting ” the other man at a couple
of yards’ distance.
* * • *
In recent Sydney case, a man, who was
medically advised, on his wife and family’s ac-
count, to leave an unwholesome tenement, took
with him his poor £5-worth of furniture ; where-
upon, as he owed a few weeks’ rent, the landlord
was down upon him with the “ fraudulent - re-
moval” charge, and had him fined £lO, or six
months’ gaol. The law is worthy of Botany Bay
that permits such punishment for an act in which
—as proved by the ex-tenant’s interim offer to pay ,
off the landlord by instalments—there was no
felonious intent.
* * * *
Maoriland law brought up the other day,
with a shock, against this problem—is a man
entitled to kiss the girl he is engaged to? The
girl admitted the engagement, and also acknow-
ledged that she refused to' let him kiss her. She
didn’t twine round him, or crush her ruby lips
against his moustache, or let him playfully bite
her ear. And when at last he tried to kiss her by
force she resisted, and in the struggle her two
small feet flew up, and she fell in the mud and
lost her hat. She charged him next day with
assault, and produced the muddy clothes in court
as evidence, and the court said that it didn’t
know what to do—it had never before met an
engaged girl who refused to be kissed. So it
found a non-committal verdict—something to the
effect that it didn’t think the clothes were much
damaged, and hoped the mud would brush off
when it was dry, and that it expected the hat had
blown away down the street.

kicked at baptism.

VON KOTZE.
Recent returns show that there are nine
members of the Clifton family occupying high
positions in the Westralian Govt, service. One
is Principal Under-Secretary for Lands, another
is Warden and R.M. at Lawlers, while others are
in the Customs, Railways and Education Depts.
Warden Clifton was in the milk business in a
Perth suburb when the Coolgardie goldfields were
discovered. Another family well represented is
Roe. The eldest is chief P.M. of Perth, another
is Sheriff, and a third is Warden in a N.W. dis-
trict. The service is stuffed to repletion with re-
presentatives of the early ’Gropers, but as it is a
certainty that the Public Service Board to be ap-
pointed will consist of men of the same class the
system will be perpetuated rather than otherwise.
* * * *
The whole of the Hobart Water Police
force has been disabled. It comprised one sergeant
with no men under him. When all the municipal
and Govt, police were amalgamated this year the
new commissioner saw the fun of the Water
Police Force and squashed it ; took the stripes off
the sergeant, but left him his pay and put him to
work in the streets.

* * * •
Ramsay Smith, whom S.A. Govt, brought
out as senior physician during the Adelaide Hos-
pital rumpus, declares that they don’t useanti-toxin
at the institution, yet it has not lost in his time
a single patient from diphtheria. The method
adopted is one which he credits to Dr. Cockburn,
now the province’s Agent-General. Also, under
similar methods of treatment, not one of 69 cases
of measles and 115 cases of scarlet fever have been
lost.
* * * *
S.A. Govt, is still bringing medical men
from England for Adelaide Hospital. Their en-
gagement was kept so quiet that the arrival at
Adelaide last week of Dr. Prior, one of the three
engaged, was the first intimation received locally.
The Adelaide medicos have at last grown so tame
that new importations are not boycotted socially
now as were Napier and Smith when they arrived.
* • * *
The Kingston Govt, has advantaged itself
of Dr. Whittell’s resignation to appoint Dr.
Ramsay Smith, the bete noir of Adelaide striking
doctors, Inspector of Schools of Anatomy and
to superintend the University School of Anatomy.
“ This,” as one of the surgical antagonists of the
Govt, acknowledged, “ will give Smith an oppor-
tunity of being damned nasty,” which perhaps
was what he and the Premier wanted. Who
knows!
* • * *
The latest N.S.W. police absurdity was an
escape from custody. A prisoner in charge of a
single constable was being taken by coach at
night from Hillgrove to Armidale. The coach
broke down, whereupon prisoner blew out the
light and bolted into the bush. Constable blazed
away with his “squirt” into the darkness, then
went in pursuit and was lost all night in the bush.
About 24 hourslater the man way re-taken 160 miles
away, and the absurd part of the business was that
he hadn’t run off at all, but simply hung about in
the darkness while the constable was emptying his
revolver, and then, when the coach started,
climbed up behind and rode to Armidale, where
he took a cab to the railway station, jumped into
an empty compartment in a train just starting,
and travelled unnoticed over 150 miles. When
detected, he jumped out of the carriage-window,
and might have got clear had he not mistaken a
policeman in plain clothes for a railway-ganger.
Another “smart capture,” of course !
• • * *
The path to Lee Weller’s “grave” at
Glenbrook is not much used now—the pious pil-
grims go elsewhere for excitement. For months
after Butler was hanged Glenbrook was a favorite
picnic-ground with holiday excursionists from
Sydney ; and local cicerones made a good thing
out of them. “ You see, missus, Butler ’e stood
’ere; and Lee Weller ’e was a-bent-up there
diggin’; and Butler ’e shot ’im just there. Hi
didn’t ’ear ’im myself; but they say ’e groaned
horful.” Then the ladies —they were mostly
ladies—would goggle their eyes, and gasp, and
boil the cheerful billy on the edge of the ex-
cavation, before settling their skirts and
squatting to discuss the “tragedy ” till the
train went. Of course, when the body was
removed, Govt, filled the hole up—it wasn’t
a large hole, for Butler was an impatient
man, and sparing with his holes; but
the pilgrims opened it again ; and so
many ladies took away handkerchiefs full
of sand for keepsakes that you could almost
get a horse and cart in before they were
done. And in many suburban homes now,
when visitors have “ seen the halbum,” the
hostess proudly takes down a “ lovely
vaws ” from the drawing - room mantel-
piece : 11 There! That’s sand from Lee
Weller’s grave ! ”
One morning about (say)
six of seven years ago a notori-
ous jury-squarer in a certain
Australian city, strolling lei-
surely down to the law-courts,
met a legal ex-employer, who had
been lately raised to the bench.
A half - friendly, half - surreptitious
nod was met by a haughty stare of non-recog-
nition. “Hm ! ” said the squarer to a pal,
a little later, “ got the straight tip this time,
anyhow. Met Judge X just now. Looked me
straight in the eye and never moved a muscle. If
ever I come before him for ‘ arranging a verdict ’
I’ll get 10 years, sure’s death.” “Why,” said his
pal, “ I thought you fixed up a case for him not
long ago?” “So I did. And he hasn’t forgotten
it. That’s why.”

A recently-widowed
South Melbourneite, finding it
impossible to give adequate expression to his
grief through the in memoriam columns and
the usual trappings of woe, tied great bunches of
crape about his chimney tops, and let them remain
there for seven days.
* * * *
On the eternal lodge-doctor question,
“ M. 8.” to The Bulletin
If “C. 0.” (5/8/’99) doesn’t “approve of the present
lodge practice of insuring against ill-health,” why doesn’t
he “ contract with the doctor direct ” ? It is a better
arrangement for all concerned, and he will find that
almost any reputable general practitioner will be only toO
glad to have him. With regard to other matters, the
assertion that the single fee may include perhaps
20 persons is not nonsense, for the writer once had
to attend 22 under these circumstances. As for the
amount of premium, the chemist’s whack amounts
usually to one-fourth, often more, and the doctor gets
the remainder, which in many cases is even below the
14s. quoted by “ C.C.” But it is only too true that
these things are made possible by throat-cutting medical
competition. The fight is growing even harder, and
“C.C's” suggestion that the struggling young medico
should advertise his willingness to form a private club
under improved conditions is not far off its fulfilment.
There are man3 r private clubs in existence, and they work
well; but the advertising would rouse the big lodges to
use every effort to crush private associations. In addi-
tion, the poor rag of “ medical etiquette ” is all that the
young doctor has to cover the wounds of his dignity.
Advertising deprives him of it, and relegates him to the
MOTH AND RUST.
THE NIPPER (to benevolent old lady, who has j ust
given him, a penny): “Say, old girl, if yer’d
kept that brown any longer it would er turned
green. ”
ranks of the “young man’s friend” class, for ever out of
the running for the top place to which every young
medico aspires—while he is young. “ C. 0.” has strength,
but his science is lacking—he judges from the outside.
Still, there is growing up a generation of young doctors
who think much as “ 0.0.” does. The age of direct
medical advertising is not far off—and the big lodges
recognise and fear the fact.

From Norfolk Island :—“ These people
are very religious ; attend service two or three
times a day. Won’t do a hand’s-turn on Sun-
days ; if they see a man smoking on that day
think he is going straight to hell. Not too moral
—that is, among themselves ; but they are very
strict with outsiders, and suffer no liberties.”
(Writer is 70 or so.) “ They are very lazy.” The
island has, it seems, one lawyer ; they tax him
specially. Which, anyhow, doesn’t look like a
bad idea.
* * * *
A fair Australienne “ doing ” the Islands
recently was slightly embarrassed at Apia by the
attentions of a stalwart native, who followed the
lady and her party all over the place, chattering
his own lingo the while. He had a war-dance
executed for them, took them to various points of
interest, and when the time came for re-embark-
ing and the lady shook hands with him, he mur-
mured in English (the first he had spoken that
day): “Me handsome feller ! ” It was the re-
mark she had made herself on first seeing him !
* * * *
The Aug. sittings of the Mackay (Q.) Dis-
trict Court were held at a novel hour. There were
but two cases for hearing—an application to quash
a conviction and a case of indecent assault, to
which accused had pleaded guilty, so that the sit-
tings would be over in a very short time. Owing
to the tides the tender was to leave the wharf at
5 a.m., and the Judge wanted to catch than boat.
The court had, however, been gazetted for 15th
inst., and so had to be held on that day. The sit-
tings were therefore opened at one minute after
midnight, and closed about Bmin. later.
* * * *
The quickness with which the Victorian
referendum poll was published outside the
Argus office recalls the Melbourne posting of
the results of last N.S.W. referendum. The
Argus was in direct communication with the S. M.
Herald so that the results were wired direct to
the office while a certain other Melbourne paper
had to depend for its results on the ordinary press
telegrams through the Government office. But
that paper’s jackals were not to be “ done.” They
had a man posted outside the Argus office, and
as soon as the Argus hoisted, say, “9,ooo’’the
jackal scooted down the street and got 11,000
hoisted at his office ; and this at a time when the
figures of (say) 11,000 were not even known at the
Electoral Registrar’s office in Sydney. The Argus
was too proud to paragraph the fact, but it
tumbled to the sharp practice all the same.
* * * m
The trial of Anastatia Butler, at Melb.
Criminal Court, resulted in a decisive defeat of
the ’tectives. Perhaps it was well for the woman
that she was actually put on her trial for
the “ murder ” ; and, anyway, the failure of the
police attempt, to get incriminating statements
from the scared spinster is a matter for honest
satisfaction. Old man Butler’s body was dis-
covered under circumstances which suggest that
daughter Anastatia had made an attempt to get
rid of it, in accordance with her allegation of its
having “gone away” on its live legs. The case
against Anastatia began and ended there, but the
detectives introduced a certain spade into their
evidence without mentioning the existence of an-
other spade, also a “shos'el,” both of which
tended to make the first spade look inno-
cent. Moreover, after “ obtaining a state-
ment ” from Anastatia Butler, and getting
her to sign it, they asked her brother to
question her on their behalf, and when the wor-
ried woman had upset their calculations by saying
nothing to justify her arrest, they arrested her.
This was prior to the inquest. The coroner’s
jury found no evidence of old man Butler having
been murdered, therefore Anastatia was set at
liberty. A month or two passed, nothing arose to
cast new suspicion on Anastatia, but the detec-
tives swore a new information to the Crown, and
arrested her some more. And now a Criminal
Court jury has voted Anastatia not guilty with-
out waiting to hear the Crown case ridiculed by
the prisoner’s counsel. Unfortunately the jury’s
hasty acquittal of the prisoner saved the police
from the “ roasting ” they richly deserved. With
several theories to choose from they chose the
least reasonable theory, apparently because it was
the only one by which they could hope to get
Anastatia hanged.
* * * *
Broken Hill municipal council has been
having another good time. Ordinarily it consists
of six Labor men and six others ; but Buzaeott,
M.L.C., having to resign so that he might come
to Sydney, the “others ” were for the moment six
to five. Led by Aid. Jabez Wright, the Laborites
resolved to block things until the equilibrium was
restored. Two midnight sittings were held, and
another which lasted a whole morning, all inside
five days ; and the Laborites stonewalled all the
time. One proposal was that, as Gov. Beauchamp
was going up that way, the town should grant the'
Mayor an extra J2lOO to spend in entertaining
him. To this scores of amendments were moved
—including some that the Mayor (who isn’t a
Laborite) be allowed “30 pieces of silver ” for the
purpose; that he be allowed Is,, then Is. Id., and
so on. On the shilling proposal (a fair sample of
the proceedings):—
Alderman Wright: You can’t stop an alderman from
speaking. I want a division.
The division was taken.
Alderman Warren seconded the amendment.
Alderman Wise supporied. They would not he able to
supply the Governor with champagne arid turkey for a
shilling, but saveloys were not. too bad and
Alderman Waugh (indignantly): Are there any saveloys
before the meeting, Mr. Mayor?
Alderman Wise : I wish there were. I could do with a
saveloy and something else just now.
The Mayor ruled Alderman Wise out of order.
The closure was at last applied wholesale, and in
a pandemonium the £lOO proposal was carried.
Of the three meetings held in five days two
broke up in disorder, and the third was only
ended by the arrival of Sunday morning. The
Laborites will, no doubt, have their way; under
the circumstances it is quite unlikely the
Governor will visit Broken Hill.
