A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, August 22, 1899.
My Dear Myee,—
I believe it is too funny for aphorisms to see
certain people scanning the list of the V.R. Even-
ing Parties, and enunciating reasons why This and
That had no claim on a card. It must be some of
these uninvited ones who were heard describing
how “ She went up to spend the evening in her
hat, and she said in the dressing-room, ‘ I can’t
take my ’at off, my ’air aint done !’ ” And how a
gentleman from No-where-at-all wore brown
gloves in to dinner, and spread them out by the
side of his soup. And many other stories. But,
after all, which would be jollier ? For Darling
Point to have the choosing? Or for His Ex.?
What do you think ?
If you can go by what the people who
have been left out say, there would be
nobody but themselves to dally with the V.R.
strawberries if they had the asking. Awfully
jolly for His Ex.! But wouldn’t it be a little
quiet for them ? As for the people who will never
put their patent toes inside Govt. House—no, not
even if they are begged by His Excellency to do
it —their names are— —. But that’s telling a lot.
And now comes this awful extract from an
official notice: “As writing a name is not
only an act of courtesy to the Governor, but still
more a mark of respect to the Queen, whose repre-
sentative he is, His Excellency is unable to invite
anyone whose name is not written in the book,
but it is evident that writing a name does not entitle
anyone to an invitation ” /
Buried word for the ladies of Potts Point. “If
it is evident that writing a name does not entitle
everyone to an invitation, it must be evident that
something else does.” Find the Something Else.
The Society of Artists had their picture-show
opened by Lord Beauchamp on Saturday. It was
wet, as it always is at picture-show time, but a. good
sprinkling of visitors, and patrons who always know
agood tiling when they see a regular daub, responded
to the S.A.’s invitations. Evidently our private
views have not begun to be the occasions of the
weird night-like robing of the old country shows.
All the lady artists were in fine feathers, sartori-
ally. Miss Theo. Cowan’s violet tulle hat, worn
with a white lace veil, was snappy enough to com-
pete with the millinery of any short-haired circle.
Pretty Miss Theo. Proctor, whose elongated
studies of women have attracted a good deal of
notice lately, and were rather fancied by His Ex.
on Saturday, sheltered behind a big brown hat.
Miss Alice Muskett was in black. The Premier
and Mr. Hogue represented the Ministry. At 12
His Ex. mounted the dais, and opened the show
with a particularly nice sentiment. Said he : “I
do so want to urge on you the evil of buying what
is not beautiful. ” At which the Society of Artists
smiled and thought of—well, their thoughts did not
go very far down the street. By the way, the Art
Society is nearly next door—but of their pictures
anon.
Little Sid. Long then took his Ex. round the
show, and the crowd scattered, and you had the
opportunity of observing what a boyish-looking
lot most of these S.A. artists are. Long, Lam-
bert, Howard Ashton, Garlick, are all striplings
to look at. I believe they only boast two beards
in this fraternity: thosp of Tom Roberts and Van
der Velden.
Here you’ll find our picture of the year, “ Across
the Black-soil Plains,” by George Lambert.
The Art Gallery snapped it up for a mere
hundred guineas. The picture is a ten-
feet -by - three panel framed in dull green
wood. Thirteen horses dragging a wool-waggon
struggle realistically along its length. Every
horse nas a personality—or animality—of its own.
To many people the canvas is intensely pathetic.
There is such a forlorn, hopeless never-never feel-
ing about the long team and the country and the
atmosphere generally. And the spirit and
action and grip of it are splendid !
Other pictures bought by the Gallery are Sid
Long’s poetical woman rising from the rushes;
Tom Roberts’ “ Jeptha’s Daughter,” in which you
will recognise at once a pretty Sydney Hebress,
although there’s no fieur de riz on the picture;
and an entrancing “Old Man’s Head ” by the little
newly-arrived Italian, Dattilo Rubbo. Tom
Roberts shows a quaint portrait of Grace Noble in
Rose Trelawney’s clothes. Brown predominates ;
she wears a hideous little brown felt hat with a
flying veil, and a brownish-green jacket over
crinoline skirts. It isn’t a flattering picture. The
same artist puts in a portrait of Mrs. W. A. B.
Greaves, a most masterly study of white hair and
dignity. Longstaff’s full-length lady is reminis-
cent of his charming Gallery picture, “ The Lady
in Black, ” but the piercing fascination of the older
picture is missing. Many people are interested
in Miss Meston’s picture of Prof. Anderson Stuart
with the edge off. But what does a prominent
art critic mean by reporting this as a “ youthful
and flattering likeness?” Just to show that it
wasn’t, the Prof, hovered round conspicuously on
Saturday. Also, while asking questions, what is
the name of the garment Julian Ashton’s waking
maiden in “ Dawn ”is wearing ? I ask because it
was unknown to all the women at the Show on
Saturday. It seems to be a kind of robe-de-nuit
cut low and adapted for the balls you go to in
nightmares.
Another opening disclosed itself on Monday
when His Ex. made public the Art Society’s
Annual Exhibition. Frank Mahony was widely
admired in a top hat, but otherwise there was
little dressing. Again it was pouring heavily, but
the long narrow rooms were densely crowded, and
His Ex. had to work a difficult passage along the
walls when he started to do the rounds. His
admiration of Mrs. Stoddart’s miniatures, which
he had observed at the smoke-night on Saturday,,
resulted in a commission for Lady Mary’s portrait.
Though this isn’t the place for attempted criticism,
let me ask you in passing to glance at a strange
oil called “The Light Beyond the Gloom.”'
Whether it is well-painted or not I cannot say,
but I do know that when I was fifteen and in love
with a sunset, I was continually painting that,
identical picture in the eye of my mind. In the
distance was that same sunset and the city leaning
dimly silhouetted against it. Nearer were those
lonely fields, and that desolate, windy hillside,
with just that long, weird, shapeless figure
sweeping impossibly through the gloom. My
heart gave a great leap when I looked up and
saw all those things recorded there. _ But I
don’t offer this information to you with any
deductions. It may be the sort of picture
everybody dreams at fifteen. I can only say that
it fascinated me, and I know of others who have
been similarly attracted. I would not dare to live
in the house with it, any more than anyone would
like to live with their dreams, but I would be glad
to know what becomes of it, and get an entree to
visit it occasionally.
The Art Gallery hasn’t patronised this old
Society very liberally. Only one picture, Joe
Wolinski’s kitchen-scene, animated with a small
boy eating. Many of the artists put this parsi-
mony down to the much-abused trustee who
belongs to the rival Society. Of course they have
to put it down to something, but I hear that this
same trustee refused to sell his own big picture
to the Art Gallery just because he is a trustee.
In looking through the catalogue to find out the
author of “ The Spirit of True Love Leadeth Up-
wards ” (it turned out to be by Moore-Jones, and
was attractive because the man and girl were both
refreshingly good-looking), I came upon some in-
comprehensible notices at the bottom of some
sketches of pictures. Are these advts. ? Because
there’s something rather dangerous about a state-
ment like this : “It was first exhibited in ISB9,
and has always been regarded as one of the best
pictures he has yet painted.” And now it’s 1899 1
The Fair and the weather were the only topics
last week; just as in the old three-bottle days.
Then all the fair were women, but this one is
largely managed by men. Business is doing
moderately well—up till Saturday they had taken
£3OOO. The latest innovation is a bar and a grill-
chop room, run in connection with Mrs. Willie
Mitchell’s cigar-divan. The prettiest women and
frocks are on view about here. Miss Maud
Gowlland and Miss Wade Brown assist some
days. Grace Noble was running raffles on Satur-
day in a little grey toque with violets bunched up
on one side, a chine violet blouse, and short,
ignoble skirt, for she generally trails yards of
well-cut material after her. Mrs. Mitchell was
adorably dressed that same day in a cream frock
with an open lace yoke, and a lovely hat with
white and rose-tinted feathers sweeping round the
face. These stall-holders change their dresses
frequently. In fact, only half-a-dozen women in
the bazaar keep to their original liveries.
Did you see Mrs. St. Hill’s reading of Lady
Mary’s handsinthe Pkess News? It runs candidly,
thus “ Very refined; fastidious; sensitive.
. . . Conventional and very correct in ideas. . . .
Action very circumscribed. ... Not fond of
society, and very difficult to please. . . . Cold-
hearted, and without enthusiasm in her affections,
but very constant and true. . . . Very clever head
in its own line, but not versatile.”
The Press News, by the way, is the Press
Bazaar newspaper, published at the Fair twice a
week. Lady Mary edits it conscientiously, reads
everything herself, and goes to no little trouble.
Her sensible, rather stiffly-written, article on
Part Singing bears out her reputation as a music-
lover. Mrs. Aronson contributes bright Fair
gossip every week. Mrs. Brough has recorded her
deep affection for Paula Tanqueray, her favorite
part, and Brough fires off some comic verses. A
special editorial room is a feature of the Fair, fur-
nished cosily for Lady Mary’s use.
Apropos of the Fair, somebody confides to the
Telegraph his grief because drinking bars exist in
the Press Bazaar (fancy any Press affair without
one), and likewise because little boys are lured to
the penny dip at the risk of eternal hell fire.
Curious the modernity ot this crusade against
gambling. A 30-year-old number of the “ Sunday
at Home ” gives a story of a woman who has faith
in God that her lottery ticket will win the prize,
and it is even so ; likewise in those days the hero
of the Sunday-school yarn could be a brewer with-
out having to give up his vats in the last chapter.
Yet those were the days when fiction reading and
light conversation were reprobated with a horror
hardly known nowadays, Evidently no genera-
tion has time to be good all round ; but we must
give the Deity a lot of trouble sometimes keeping
up with us.
Have you heard of the quiet wedding that came
off in Sydney last week ? Mr. J. C. Williamson,
of the “ Firm, ” ex-husband of Maggie Moore, to
Miss Mary Weir.
A brilliant Jewish wedding, that will have come
off yesterday when you read this, happens at the
Synagogue to-morrow (Wednesday), uniting pretty
Miss Moss-Davies (from Maonland) with Mr.
Robert Lionel Moss. Next week, I will tell you
all about the reception at ‘ ‘ The Australia, ” and
the gorgeous wedding-presents now pouring in.
As a graceful tribute from one Miladi to
another, Lilian Wheeler gave a little tea last
week for Henrietta Watson. Mrs. Brough and
Miss Temple, the Misses Hollander, Mr. Leo
Dobbin and Mr. Cyril Keightly were among the
tea-drinkers who foregathered at the Art Dep6t in
Vickery’s Chambers. By the way, these two
Miladis are as unlike as possible in looks. Lilian
"Wheeler is a tall, slim woman, with golden hair
and large soft blue eyes. Henrietta W atson is
not nearly as tall as she looks on the stage, dark-
eyed, with a dark complexion, peculiar, flexible
mouth, and that sinuous grace of movement which
•characterises her sister Elizabeth.
It seems that these Thespians will even go to
hear recitations in their off hours ; I saw many
theatrical people—Mr. George Rignold among
them—at Mrs. Hannibal Williams’ recital of a
“ Midsummer Night’s Dream ’’ last week. There is
also a Mr. Hannibal Williams who recites whole
plays, and appears to be favorably known in the
States. Mrs. Hannibal is from New York, and has
a broad Yankee inflection that rather takes the
cream off her work for an Australian audience.
But, still, she never offends the intelligence of
anybody who brings along that commodity, and
her memory acts excellently. She was frocked in
white brocade with a rose-pink fold along the
bodice, and had a big bowl of violets aesthetically
arranged on a tiny table. After that, lam afraid
I have exhausted all the admiration points. The
recitress was too smileful for a soulful Shake-
speare audience, whereas for a giddy pleasure-
evening house there wasn’t enough personal
•charm to help out the lengthy recital. Miss Edith
King chirped behind the scenes, and Miss Minnie
Daniels and Signor Podenzana and Mr. P. M.
'Carter brought in some well-rehearsed incidental
music.
At Rand wick Races, on Wednesday, the New
Woman was seen as comic artists have beheld her
in dreams for a long time. That is to say, I believe
she was the New Woman. The only doubt per-
taining to the matter rests on the query: Wasn’t she
a man ? The personage in question wore a grey
sac coat, a white shirt, a grey pot hat, and a
simple sort of skirt that didn’t invite you to look
at it, and thereby shatter the delusion that, take
her for all and all, she was a man. She drove out
in a dog-cart, and walked the lawn with a calm
nerve that was really splendid if she wasn’t a
woman after all. As for the women who
couldn’t be mistaken for anything else, the
grounds were sprinkled chiefly with little fawn
coats and bright-colored toques. It wasn’t a well-
dressed day by any means. Potts Point was all
at the Fair. The only stallholder whom you
would have expected to dress hugged a long tan
cloak with a shaped frill, but unwisely wore no
veil in the face of a cutting wind. Only one
woman in the world could do this without mixing
the cream and carnation in a horribly unbecoming
manner. Lily Langtry used to be that woman,
but even she can’t do that now.
Our popular Madair a Kellerman goes off on a
concert tour with-New! u y and Spada in October,
and takes a well-deserved benefit concert at the
Town Hall on October 4th.
And a new soprano i 3 Miss Fanny Bauer, at
whose private recital last week several connois-
seurs were convinced that her voice would be an
organ worth looking after. She gives a concert in
the To wn Hall early in October.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ His Excellency is very ‘touchy’ nowadays on
the subject of ‘falling-off.’ The last time he
made an unpremeditated descent from his fiery
steed the calamity was ‘ suppressed ’ by the news-
papers, at the urgent request of Lord Richard,
who rushed round to the editorial sanctums, says
the story, with anxiety in his eye and a finger to
his lip. The other day Lord B. got a nasty spill
from his noble bike on Toorak road. Some work-
men ran to pick him up (fortunately he was un-
broken), and the inevitable ‘kind lady’ came
upon the scene and dusted him as though he were
a bit of old Dresden, but the solicitude of the
Samaritans was not very graciously accepted.
Grumbling the glad tidings, ■ I’m not hurt, ’ the
reckless acrobat mounted his ‘ safety ’ and wob-
bled off.
“ The first concert of the present Marshall Hall
series drew a comfortable crowd to the Town Hall.
There was rather more of a musical heavy brigade
to the fore than at Scherek’s fixtures, also, maybe,
a thicker sprinkling of Naicest. Taking into con-
sideration the higher prices, the Marshall Hall
venture looks like safe financial sailing. The
magnetic director has a strong personal following.
Then there are conscientious people, Germans and
others, who subscribe to a course of ‘good
concerts ’ for the same reason as they read a daily
portion of Shakespeare and Schiller.
“ Scherek’s patrons drop in for an afternoon’s
‘nice music’ when so inclined, but are influenced
only by a wish for pleasure, not by a sense of duty
to themselves. Some slaves groan under the yoke
of alleged ‘duty.’ ‘No! I don’t read Australian
authors ! ’ they’ll tell you. ‘ There isn’t even time
for the books one ought to read ! ’ Ail the same,
Marshall Hall instils some of his own fire into his
orchestra and their renderings reflect the glow.
“Elsie Hall, to whom the first concert was
tendered, wore all white and played like her poeti-
cal, intellectual self. The Castles boom makes
one feel sick when noticing the comparative lack
of general appreciation of this fine Australian-
born artist. Castles, at best, is utterly immature,
a girl with a voice, who, since great artists
are made as much by their high intelligence
as by their voices, may possibly never attain any
important rank. Elsie Hall is a first-class inter-
pretive artist,_ whose equipment is the result of
years of laborious study combined with natural
aptitude. Writer has heard Stavenhagen,
Sophie Menter and Leonard Borwick —all first-
rankers in Europe—play Beethoven’s ‘ Emperor ’
concerto, but in respect to delicacy, beauty of tone,
and pure poetry, gives the palm to Elsie Hall. If
her name were Hallistovsky —or something
like that—the great public would rush and say,
‘ How wonderful ! ’ As it is, the best judges
say that she is the finest Australian pianist up to
date, and one of the best of her time.”
“ The people who came late, and those who went
early, at the Elsie Hall orchestral concert, had a
bad time. Marshall Hall won’t go on with the
music till every one is seated, and on this occasion
he just turned round and kept on hammering with
his baton upon the conductor’s desk till the
offenders had subsided. A woman in a red opera
cloak blushed a deeper shade than her garment, as
she felt every eye in the place riveted upon her.
A youth who left with his fiancee, or sister, before
the end, amid a cannonade of baton taps, had to
return to fetch a shawl left behind in the scared
exit. It was like facing a Maxim gun in operation.
A thousand eyes were directed towards the
unhappy youth, and the baton banged at the desk
furiously. But he coolly fetched the shawl, and
didn’t even turn red. Nor did he put his tongue
out. Such unabashed coolness deserves the Vic-
toria Cross. Nevertheless, people earn the gib-
betting. Music is spoiled for those who are
disturbed by late comers or early goers. Marshall
Hall is teaching good manners to his audiences—
though he adopts a very rude way of doing it.
“ Three different varieties of the Amy Castles
costume at the Town Hall last Satur-
day night. First came Una Bourne in her very
abbreviated sample of the ‘infant phenomenon’
garment, and a brave display of white stocking.
Brainy, blonde-haired Una is getting on for 18, or
thereabouts, and the frock, being four years
younger than her age, which is fully five years
younger than her face, gave her the appearance of
a serious humorist. Alice Crawford (from Ben-
digo) elected to recite under the absurd disadvan-
tage of a white frock that fitted her nowhere, and
was too short to give her graceful bearing a fair
chance. Miss Crawford is a tall, dark and decidedly
pretty girl. of , nqt more than 21 or 22, and the
reason why she didn’t let herself go in a full-sized
platform gown would have been a painful mystery
but for the Castles boom. It is believed by the
Bendigonians that a short frock spells a Melb.
triumph for debutantes. After the handsome
Alice had retired with a cargo of floral tributes
and all the dignity possible (considering the length
of her skirt), Lili Sharp came forward. The garb
of early innocence sported by Lili at her first
concert, a few Saturdays back, had given place
to a primitive salmon-pinky frock, which fell
a trifle short of her ankles; and she looked the
oldest-fashioned young damsel of, say, 22 to 25,
ever seen outside a comic opera. Her ingenuous
smile has been toned down, it is true, since a cer-
tain ‘ scurrilous rag ’ passed remarks upon it, but
the excitement of getting her voice discovered
seems to have aged Miss S. for the present, and
she might as well be 35 for all the difference that
a juvenile rig-out can make to her. Even her
voice is settling down into a sober mezzo-soprano.
, “The female committee at Melb. Women’s Hos-
pital has trouble again—this time because the
medical officers will not turn out of bed before
about 10 a.m. How to get them up is the problem.
The committee, being very married, should be able
to manage a little thing like that.
“The Annual Bicycle Club Ball in Masonic
Hall (Aug. 10) was a cheery, unstarched hop.
The dancers were nearly all active young things,
long-winded and light-heeled through much up-
hill pedalling. The Mayoress, in pale-green
brocade and transparent sleeves, looked quite well
again. Mrs. Jack Parsons wore shimmering
jetted net over electric green, slipping off the
shoulders, snake-fit above, massed frills below.
The only thorn in the rose of the ball was a
draped, ever-revolving whirligig, by way of
decoration. It giddied the observer like the
heroine whose ‘ life was turning, turning in mazes
of dizzy sound.’

husband of a well-known Victorian pen woman has
excited a wide-spread feeling of the deepest
sympathy.

Roderic Quinn’s charming selection of
verses, “The Hidden Tide,” has been warmly
welcomed everywhere. The price is Is. 9d., post-
free from The Bulletin office.
The “Encyclopedia Britannica ” on time-
payment, as issued by Melb. Argus, is neither the
wondrous bargain that the Argus represents it to
be, nor the “ take-in ” suggested by the snarling
Age. Every likely purchaser of an Encyclopedia
is supposed to know pretty well the merits and
shortcomings* of the work, as a book in 24
volumes, the 25th volume being a not very neces-
sary index. Information under letter Ais about
twenty-three years old, and the Z end of the
work is about eleven years old. Many matters of
recent interest are not touched; on the other hand
there is any amount of reading matter which is
none the worse for having been written long ago.
The real question at issue is the price question.
Letters in the Age would make the public believe
that the Argus is offering for 17 guineas a publi-
cation elsewhere offered for about a third of the
money, and the suggestion i's contemptibly unfair,
for the Yankee photographed edition is no
cheaper at the price than the more expen-
sive article. Paper, printing and binding of the
Encyclopedia offered for sale through the Argus
are satisfactory enough. The half-morocco ’’
edition at 22 guineas is the usual order, and,
granting that “ half-morocco ’’ is a bookseller’s
term, meaning more boards than leather, the 25
volumes are undoubtedly fair value at 18s. each,

PERSONAL ITEMS.
“ Banjo” Paterson has written a “bushy
book (in prose) of about 120,000 words, and is now
revising it in his less tired moments.
Why doesn’t John Norton write an “ Open
Letter ’’ to himself, telling John in simple allitera-
tive language what Norton thinks about him ?
You may say what you like against ,
but there's this in his favor : he never accused any
man of any crime that he wasn’t game to go and
commit himself.
J. T. Bennetts, who stood around and
cheered while the flag was run up at the “ pro-
clamation ” of S. Aus., died a week or two ago in
the south of the province.
There is a member in an Australian Par-
liament who prosecuted his servant-girl for using
butter when she was ordered to use dripping. The
magistrate fined the girl Is. and paid the fine
himself.
There are still in the N.S.W. State-aid-to-
Religion pension list, 17 C.E., 10 R.C., 4 Presby-
terian and 5 Wesleyan Methodist clergymen.
Total of the 36 pensions, .£5868, or an average of
about .£163 each.
Attorney-General R. W. Pennefather, of
Westralia, is son of a Melbourne police officer who
got big compensation and a substantial pension
for having his skull smashed by a shovel in the
hands of a prisoner.
That somewhat pantalooney politician,
John M‘lntyre, has been created, at his earnest
request, an honorary colonel of the Melb. Scottish
Regiment. This realises the dream of his exist-
ence—to wear a kilt in defence of his adopted
country.

agonising death-bed repentance just about due in
clerical circles ? When it comes it will no doubt
include his last despairing speech, a heartrending
cry, and various details of pre-visions of “ the
burning ' ”

E. A. Rennie, N.S.W. Auditor-General,
whose figures cannon so disastrously against those
of ex-Treasurer Reid, was born in 1820. He
draws a salary of =£92o. It is an open secret that
Reid has for long manoeuvred to oust the ancient
with the object of putting Statist Coghlan in his
place. A further card in the same hand is the
introduction of Edward Pulsford, M.L.C., to ah
office in the Statist’s Department. But Rennie
declares he won’t retire, and Reid can’t maihe
him.
Just died at Coolgardie a well-known ex-
Queenslander, 5 -Hume Black, of dropsy. Black
started his career with pastoral pursuits, but
finally drifted into the sugar industry at Mackay.
In ’Bl—’B3 and ’BB he represented Mackay in
Parliament, and held the position of Minister for
Lands under MTlwraith. For a time he prac-
tically Headed the now defunct Northern Separa-
tion movement, but finally accepted a Govt, billet
to go as lecturer and commercial agent to the old
country. An able man and, bitter partisan.

Q ne Q f J U( Jge Redmond Barry’s whims
was to insist or ‘ his monogram, R. 8., in big
letters, being let into the centre of the pavement,
in Melb. Public Library entrance hall. To avoid

One grave mistake was made by G. Lam
bert, the young artist whose splendid dashing
picture, “ Across the Blacksoil Plains,” is now the
talk of Sydney : He catalogued his picture at
£lO5,- at which price it was bought by N.S.W.
National Gallery. Had he ticketed it at .£3OO, or
even <£4oo, the trustees would simply have had to
buy it.. Lambert gets less than mere laborer’s
wages for what is one of the landmarks of Aus-
tralian art.
For years past the Melb. office of the
A.M.P. Society has had on one of its walls a
bronze plate testifying that John Batman built
the first house in Melbourne on that spot. Re-
cently, however, the society hauled down this
tablet and supplanted it with another advertising
the virtues of the A.M.P. Society. There’s no
maudlin sentimentality about a business man.
In N.S. Wales M.’sP. are never served
with subpoena or writ within the sacred precincts
of Parliament House, though this privilege,
which applies to British law-makers, does
not legally extend to “the colonies or In-
dia.” Nathless, the “profession” pre-
serves it. M.’sP., to a greater number
than any well-assured party now existent
in N.S.W. Assembly, were subpoenaed
to give evidence in the Norton-Smith
case. But no one was served at the
House. Some were not visible at their
offices, and carefully cabbed it to Par-
liament. Speaker Abbott, however,
unguardedly strayed into King-
street, and was promptly appioached
by the pamphleteer. Mi;. Speaker
was “not taking any” blue paper,
particularly Norton - Smith blue
paper, but Smith was pressing,
and, finally, persuaded Mister S.
to partake. Mr. John See (one of
John Norton’s political sponsors)
was sighted leaving Randwick
tram at King-street. He cast the
subpoena to the pavement, and,
having squashed it with both feet,
kicked it with emphasis over the kerb
and into the gutter. Wonder why? Some-
body, less careless of court orders, picked
it up and preserves it as a curio of a
sensational case.
Gov. Brassey, circularised, to-
gether with other local celebrities,
by Melbourne Bookloveb, supplies
a list of books read by him during
the present year. His note is
quaintly characteristic :
Having once commenced reading a book I rarely
lay it down until I have read it through. It is some-
times a weary task.
What a plodding old fellow ! Having bought
a book he feels bound to get his money’s worth
out of it, even though it be “a weary task.”
How can a man who thus confesses to boring
himself, be expected not to bore other people?
R. C. Bishop Torregiani, of Armidale,
N.S.W., who scales something over 20st., is set
down as the heaviest bishop in the world. Church
of England Bishops, taking them all round, only
reach middle weight. Dr. Torregiani, too, is a
monk who fasts, prays long hours, and suffers
under a vow of poverty.
Re The Bulletin's recent notice of bar-
rister J. B. Gregory’s case, a correspondent writes
that during the three consecutive years that
Mr. Gregory has been in a Lunatic
Asylum he has been allowed to retain the
Government office he holds, and -his family to
draw the pay, , Mr. Gregory is certifying officer
under the Friendly Societies Act. Another
Government official, connected with the G.P.0.,
is allowed to act as warming-pan or locum-tenens,
but without extra pay for doing so. Curious, if
true !
E. C. Westlake, who died in Sydney the
other day, was one of the trio who endeavored to
set fire to Melbourne with a little semi-political,
semi-literary sheet over a dozen years ago. Editor
Short, now of the Leader, another of the three,
did practically all the writing. Westlake, copious
anecdote-monger, used to tell how, when the
necessary obituary of the paper had to be written,
the boss penman put the pathos of the situation
into the brief phrase, “Short was its life.” To
which Westlake surreptitiously added, “ and
Short was its death.’’
J. W. Sutherland, just appointed to the
Golden Horseshoe, is the most juvenile manager
of a front rank Westralian (or any other ’Strafian)
mine. Hard work took him up the Broken Hill
Prop.’s metallurgical staff by leaps, and then
going West he remembered the Barrier experi-
ence and forthwith wrestled with the sulphides,
and was rewarded with the post of metallurgist
to the L.V. Consols. He has got the big sulphide
plant running there, and now steps over to the
Horseshoe. To two men more than all others
Broken Hill seniors point by way of encourage-
ment to the youngsters fresh from the mining
schools—Sutherland and Savage, the latter of
Cockle Creek works.
A Far-Northern correspondent :
Harry Ricketson, now in Cairns gaol charged with the
murder of a kanaka on Mulgrave plantation, is a son of
Riverina squatter Ricketson, famous for his 20 years’ law
fight with Barbour. Ricketson, jun., came to Queensland
some months ago, and has had a battle for existence ever
since. He comes of fighting stock, however, and did not
hesitate to tackle a job of scrub-cutting at 255. per acre
(some of the scrub was 6ft. thick). By working night and
day he just managed to make tucker. Luck seemed to
turn at last, when he got on as plantation overseer, but
it was only to play him a more scurvy trick. On his first
day the kanaka *‘set him,” thinking to show his courage
before the new-chum overseer. On the second day a
crowd rushed him over into a 30ft. cutting, hut as he
rolled down he took his ..principal assailant along with
him. At the bottom three of them piled upon him, but
he arose with the darkies clinging to him like bull-ants,
and threw them off. The leader rushed again, and
Ricketson fired, but failed to stop his enemv, who,
though mortally wounded, continued on the offensive]
and literally died fighting in his slayer’s arms. Ricket-
son is a fair, broad-shouldered, blue-eyed, square-jawed
man—one of the prompt sort—and no doubt he owes his
life to his promptitude. By the time the expenses of his
trial are totted up he will owe a lot more, as bail has
been refused and he cannot get out to earn even his food.
Maurice O’Rorke, who was first returned
in 1861, and has sat through 40 sessions, is the
father of Maoriland House of Representatives,
with a large margin to spare. “Dismal Bill”
Rolleston, first elected in ’6B, and with 28 sessions
to his credit, comes next, and may be called a sort
of uncle to the House,

The “ Mr. Tarleton, a former member of
an Australian Legislature,” who chaired the much-
talked-of meeting of Australians in the Transvaal,
is a man whose misfortune it is to have the
initials R.A.T., and who all the time used to
make one think of tliem. Originally a Congrega-
tional parson with a pulpit in Tasmania, he got a
taste for finance, went to S. Australia, became a
squatter, entered the Leg. Council, was a director
of the Commercial Bank of S.A., and left soon
after that unfortunate concern got into difficulties.
Mr. Tarleton had a large family connection round
Adelaide, and has drawn several well-known
families to S. Africa—among them that of Harry
Turner, long one of Adelaide’s best-known
financial men-about : town.
Friday (Aug. 25) marks the centenary of
the birth of patriot, politician, and divine John
Dunmore Lang, the vital principle of Australian
Presbyterianism for nearly 60 years. The Langs
were stubborn Scots, who hated the Stuarts and
fled into exile, returning to their native heath
with William of Orange in 1688. The first
imported slip of the stock landed in Syd-
ney in 1821, and the sturdy young “meenis-
ter ” followed. He died in August, 1878,
at the age of 79. The bronze statue standing
in Wynyard-square, Sydney, is a good likeness in
pose and feature, but old identities miss some-
thing as they gaze admiringly at it. Where’s that
gamp—the doctor’s inseparable weapon of offence
and defence ?

FLOOD TIME ON THE DAWSON.
SWAGGY (in tree): “’Ow yer gettin’ on, Bill?”
DITTO (in water) : “Oh, swimmin’!”
“ Greengum ” : He snakes living without
food. I was splitting posts on the Darling and
felled a belar. The first three lengths were solid,
but the fourth was a “pipe,” which when halved
disclosed a carpet snake —a mere skeleton,
but still alive. There was no sign of any opening
to the outer air, and I reckon that snake must
have been imprisoned at least seven or eight
years—possibly 15. It must have entered at root,
as its head was upwards and there was no room to
turn. The tree must have taken to healthy
growth and closed in on its tenant.
* * * *
People living in the vicinity of the Western
Tiers (Tas.) have of late been scared by explosions
and subterranean rumblings similar to those heard
in the hot-lakes districts of Maoriland. Dr.
Thomson, of Launceston, attributes the phe-
nomena to escapes of carbonic oxide which he says
has killed nearly all the trees in the vicinity of
Lake Echo, which were supposed to have been
destroyed by an exceptionally severe frost. Also,
he thinks a couple of young men who were mysteri-
ously lost in the Lake country have been killed by
this gas.

An aboriginality with a vengeance :
“R.A.F.” : In the early 50’s I took 1000 head of cattle
from a Richmond River station to one on the Upper Mary
(QA Just after arrival one of the bullocks was killed for
rations for the station hands, and noticing a lot of niggers
about, the boss offered them the head and offal ; but,
strange to say, they refused this, saying they had plenty
meat of their own. After supper, the boss took me over
to the blacks’ camp about 400 yards away, and never
shall I forget the sight that met our eyes. On about a
dozen fires, besides wallabies and ’possums, were roasting
human arms and legs ! and it turned me sick to see an
old gin, squatted on her haunches, gnawing at the toes
of a foot, and then passing it on to another to have a
bite. The men were moving about from one to another
as if to partake of each dead enemy in turn. They
offered us some, causing us to clear out quick. I smelt
burnt blaekfellow for weeks after. These nigs, had killed
about 40 of another tribe, and were making their way
home up river.
Ihe boy had that day returned
from the “ ticnioal college,” and Donnelly
gave a party in honor of the event. About
89 couples rolled up to dance in a room
18x24, to the accompaniment of a broken-
down mouth-organ. Donnelly was anxious
that all should enjoy themselves, but
after fixing up a couple of sets, there
still remained a fair sprinkling of “wall-
flowers.” The host looked a bit disap-
pointed, and walked into the yard ; then
he poked his head round the door and
said, “ Don’t know what the divvle people
as can’t danch want going to dandies for.
Some of ye women there, widout min,
had better come out and have a fly round
on the old grey mare that's here on me
arrum. That’s the best I can do for yis.”
* * * *
Dear Bulletin.—Very question-
able, after all, if ’possum does eat gum-
leaves. Used to do much professional
’possum-shooting in the Blue Mountains,
and took trouble to investigate the
matter. In groves of young gum or
apple trees, which are much frequented
by the ’possums, I have shot a dozen or
more out of one tree ; yet, on examining
the foliage closely by daylight, have never
seen even a leaf broken, although these
spots so abound with the animals that, if
they fed upon the foliage, the trees
would very soon be completely stripped.
Also, the frequent examinations which I
made of ’possum-stomachs never revealed
anything but grass. The ’possums we
ate we found d-ry and insipid enough,
but having no more eucalyptus-flavor, or
odor, than the flesh of wallaby.—J.P.

Re the unfenced state of M.L.
railways. On July 17, a mob of lambs
got on an unfenced part of Taranaki line.
Train came by, caught them in cutting,
and killed 53. Strange to say, speed of
train was not even checked.
“ Waipero ” : The Maori knows a wrinkle
about the accursed der-rink. In Wellington
(M.L.), recently, met a tanyata very drunk, who
asked me for something to get another drink.
Enquired how much he’d had, and he said, “ Only
one ! ” but explained that that one had been a
mixture of whisky, brandy, gin and rum, at a cost
of 6d., whereas it would have taken shillings’-worth
of beer to produce same effect.
Another Maori put me up to this dodge : Takes
two small flasks of same size, one of which he
fills with a colored fluid, like unto brandy ; goes
into pub., asks for Is. worth of brandy, and
tenders empty flask and cronk shilling. Barmaid
serves him, and then discovers cronkness of coin,
whereupon Maori, having exchanged the flasks in
his pocket, passes back the fake-stuff, receives
back his pewter shilling, and goes on to repeat at
next pub. !

“ C.E.” : Re cockies’ hours. When a
mate of mine was once harvesting for a farmer
near Orange (N.S.W.), work lasted from 4 a.m.
(with two 20-minute spells) till 5 p.m. Mate
turned up five minutes late one morning, and the
cockie wanted to know “ where he’d bin dawdlin’
all the forenoon ? ”

“ C.H.W.” : I sometimes go long trips
over the Queensland plains, and as soon as I start
a few crows will start, too, and keep with me the
whole trip. Have also known the ominous devils
to follow a party for weeks, with an eye to possi-
bilities.

Downs (W.Q.) 18 months ago
was a big wether goat

with same. After emascu-

hard race with his feathered
mates be would come in,
tongueing, a very bad last. This strange mate*

with a goat 1

“J.C.G.” : In the bush at night I have
been hearing for years a low “ Hoo, hoo, hoo”
sound repeated rather quickly, from about 15 to
30 times, at about 2min. intervals. Pitch about
“ middle-O.” I have never been able to discover
the producer, and do not agree with bush opinions
that it is quail, pigeon, iguana, or frog. Can any
reader name it, for certain ?

“A.E.G.”: Bob was a terror on bees.
He knew all about ’em, and could “ handle ’em
with any man in the country.” One Sunday as
we were driving along, Bob spied a swarm on a
young wattle ; and, of course, must secure it. He
broke off the branch, and put the lot into box at
back of trap, assuring me it was all right. Result:
One horse killed, and trap and harness smashed to
pieces—about £5O gone for ever. Got home on
foot next morning, praying and taking out stings
all the way.

“Nemo” : Knew a bullock-team to get
the heavily-loaded waggon stuck nave-deep in the
mud, and after numerous desperate efforts to make
them shift her, bullocky went back to mate’s camp
for more strength. Returning with eight extras
he was astonished to find that the bullocks had
meanwhile accomplished the task on their “ own,”
and he has actually begun to have doubts about
the efficacy of cursing 1
“Xerxes”: No; the blackfellow won’t
work nowadays. Have endeavored, in vain, for
past 12 months to get a few boomerangs. Tried
one noted “camp” personally and by deputy,
and offered cash on delivery, without result,
though the blacks were ready enough with pro-
mises in return for spot cash. I wrote to various
mission stations, and was told that the blacks
would not trouble to make boomerangs.

Dear Bulletin.—lt’s surely time we were
hearing no more of the preposterous bush notion
about the marsupial fcetus being formed in the
teat. A word to “Winch” and his like. In
marsupials the young are born at a very early
stage of development. In comparison with youngof
other mammals they are very minute, quite blind
and utterly helpless ; and in color like an earth-
worm. After parturition the mother with her
mouth places them upon the teats, to which they
at once adhere strongly ; but as even then they are
not able to suck for a considerable time, milk is
Squeezed into them by a special muscle which
surrounds each teat. Now, the bushman, per-
ceiving how helpless the tiny creature is, how
strongly it is attached, and how impossible it
would be for it to reach the pouch of itself, jumps
to the conclusion that “ it grew there.”—D.G.S.

“ Crosby’s ” : Re snakes and music
(“Kooka,” B. 15/7/’99). Writer was sitting at the
mouth of an old drive, tootling on a mouth-organ,
when he heard a rustling in the timber overhead,
and looked up to see a 4ft. 6in. brown snake
gently swinging from a beam, around which he
had twisted his tail. When the music stopped
the snake disappeared, but on its renewal Re-
turned.

“Groper”: Lying in tent one wet day,
and idly watching small lizard on top of tucker-
box catching flies, I happened to start whistling a
waltz, and was surprised to see lizard, in process
of swallowing fly, suddenly become motionless,
gazing intently towards me. Recalling what I’d
read of snake-charming, I began waving hand to
and fro, keeping time to music and gradually get-
ting closer to lizard, which appeared quite mes-
merised—eyes closed, body rigid, and fly still in
mouth. Stroked the little reptile with finger and
turned it over on its back, but it remained dead to
the world until I stopped whistling, when it
pulled itself together in a dazed manner, s" al-
lowed the fly with a gulp and scooted.

A N. S. W. small station-owner was suspected
of running an illicit still, and, one night, the
police raided him, caught him (as they thought)
red-handed, and conveyed him and the “still ” 28
miles to the station. The inspector was then
called in, and wanted to know what the devil they
had brought along the incubator for?
PADDY MINOGUE’S MOSQUITO NET.
'Twas red-hot weather on Croydon field—
The days ere bad, but the nights were worse,
For the sultry nights no respite yield,
And mosquitoes there are a screaming curse :
They’d devour a man in a night, I’d bet,
If he didn’t have a mosquito-net.
The man, or the madman, who sought repose
Without his curtains was courting death ;
Blankets were “off,” and the thought of clothes
Brought stifling sensations and gasps for breath.
We’d lie on the top of our bunks and sweat,
Stark-naked, beneath a mosquito-net.
A cry of “ Murder ! ” rang through the night,
We didn't stir—it was beastly dark ;
And it wasn’t our funeral —let ’em fight,
Or it might be someone having a lark.
Besides it would cause us extreme regret
To have to quit our mosquito-net.
But Paddy Minogue, in the tent next ours,
Excitable fellow ! was not the one
To lie at his ease—“ be the holy powers”—
While murder most foul was being done.
In a moment Minogue was out of his tent
Arrayed in his boots, and away he went.
The cries receded as Paddy neared,
And shortly ceased and the night was still.
The deadly deed had been done, he feared,
He fossicked about the foot of the hill
In his office of good Samaritan,
But failed to discover a wounded man.
Pat’s only apparel was on his feet,
So Pat, in his nakedness, bping beset
By swarms of mosquitoes made haste to retreat
To the folds of his white mosquito-net.
But torrents of “ language” found sudden vent
The moment Minogue re-entered his tent.
“ What are you prayin’ for, mate ? ” we cried,
As the crimson stream of his speech flowed on.
“ Oh ! the black-hearted villains ! ” poor Pat replied—
“ Me lovely muskeeter-curtain’s gone.”
The “ murder ” was simply a scheme to get
Poor Paddy Minogue’s mosquito-net.
JAMES SAYERS.
Cone Over To The E[?]MY
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Pensively the quid he chewed,
He spat upon his battered pup,
And sighed: “ She fell from recktertood,
An’ then—an’ then I slung ’er up.
“ She did the bleeding deed o’ shame
When none was by to make her see
Her Dooty to her father’s name,
An’ bash her systematiclee.
“ Her ol’ man’s in fer life, o’ course,
So when a stretch her brother did,
An’ when a difference with the force
Was keepin’ me from sunlight hid,
“ Without a guardian’s ’arid to stay,
She went an’ joined the Army. Now
Down Little Bourke in broad o’ day
She’s rescooin’ the slant-eyed Chow!"

• * * *
“ R.A.” : Last summer at Angledool
(N.S.W.), writer saw a 9ft. “carpet,” in a wilga
tree, swallow a bird about as large as a mag-
pie. The snake had about 2ft. of body coiled
round the bird and started swallowing head-first,
gradually unfolding as the head was worked down.
Also in the mountains between Bathurst and
Hargraves, saw a 12ft. “carpet” up a yellow
box tree, and when I cut the tree down found
the snake helpless. He had swallowed a brown
squirrel about as large as an opossum.

“F. P.” •. All bosh about Hindoos
(“6Y.H.,” 8., 8/7/’99) walking 10 miles to work,
working 16 hours, and walking 10 miles back.
Average Hindoo walks two miles an hour, which
for the 20 miles equals 10 hours, which, plus 16
hours’ graft, totals 26 hours. Doesn’t leave much
out of the 24 for sleep and recreation, eh ?

“ Tamarind ” : The less can some-
times contain the greater. Once saw a
mantis, 3in. long and no thicker than a
wooden match, eat nearly the whole of a
fair-sized frog, which still continued to
cry out even when half its body was con-
sumed. Have preserved in spirits the
mantis and the small remains of the
frog.

Tas. Assembly, the other night, passed
the Federation address to the Queen with un-
musical honors. No sooner had the Speaker
intimated that the “ ayes ” had it, than Premier
Braddon sourly called to Crosby Gilmore to
“strike up!” Gilmore, who sings on the
slightest provocation, in public or private,
struck up accordingly, and the dreadful noise
almost shook the Constitution to smithereens.
An old Ballarat resident who claims to
have been the maker of the. “ rebel ” flag which
floated over Eureka stockade, casually mentions
that the insurgents in their excitement forgot to
pay for the bit of bunting. Heroes are apt to be
indifferent to small details. Some of the grandest
fights for freedom have been made under a flag
that was never paid for.
A certain M.L.C. is a choice specimen of
the Perth landowner. He owns three houses of
two rooms each, and a fourth containing one room
only, in one of the lowest parts of the town. The
houses and surroundings are so vile that the signi-
ficant title of the “Cowyard” was bestowed on
them by the police, who brought up some of the
tenants at regular intervals. Of course, the noble
landlord made the usual excuse —“ left everything
to his agent.” That is what another snuffling
W.A. chapel-light pleaded when recently admon-
ished for letting his houses to some of Nana’s
sisters. Only the latter added that he had seen
the young ladies himself, and they were so nicely
dressed that anybody could have been taken in.
WEARY AND HEAVY LADEN.
COOKIC FARMER (to new hand from the “ould sod,”
who is for the second time discovered sleeping
in the lee of a haystack): “Here you , Paddy,
what do you mean by this? You've only done
two hours’ work, and I had you snorin’ like a
fat publican.”
New Hand (drowsily): “ Throth, yer hard on a
work in’ malm in this counthry. Now, in moy
counthry, whin a mahn is toired wid wurk, he
loies down and rests himself for a day, or two
days , or three days, maybe.”

Bulletin, as often previously remarked by us, is not run
for the good of its proprietors’ health,, but for the base
purpose of making money. This paper, therefore, is most
grateful to the rev. gentleman who succeeded in having
it removed from the table of your School of Arts, where
it was formerly read free. In fact, the Bulletin proprietary
undertakes to send the paper free for a year to any clergy-

L.D. : (1) In England no man who is an alien can be regis-
tered as a voter (see article “Franchise” in Hazell’s
Annual). An alien can’t vote at municipal or Parliamen-
tary or other elections—not- even at school-board elec-
tions. The only way to become naturalised in Britain is
to get a special Act of Parliament, or to put in five years
: ■ the service of the Crown, or to reside in the United
Kingdom for five years, and then to furnish evidence that
the applicant intends to continue residing there. Then,
on payment of £5, he can be naturalised, and have a vote;
but the Government can refuse to naturalise him if it
feels so inclined. Even after being naturalised he can
never become eligible for a seat in the Commons. But an
alien pays the same taxes as anybody else. (2)
The British suzerainty over the Transvaal is just
this : The Transvaal isn’t allowed to make any
treaty with a foreign power —the Orange Free
State excepted without Britain’s consent

Masto : You and the Heydons, Wants, Slatterys, Hig-
ginses, Nelsons and others will find that a year or two of
the Federal Parliament, comprehensively surveying all
the States, will largely mitigate the jealousies the States
now entertain, one for another Inverell: An un-
diplomaed medical practitioner is no more amenable to
law if his patient dies than is a diplomaed practitioner.
Either is only amenable for gross rashness, or want of
caution. But where the difference comes in is that, if a
diplomaed doctor’s patient dies and an investigation is
held, his brother medicos generally support him, whereas
if an undiplomaed man’s patient dies the profession, to
put it mildly, won’t go out of its way to help him out of
his fix Euko: The law appears to stand just about
thus : If challenged to fight you are legally bound to de-
cline. If you are struck you are bound to run away if
you can. If you can run away and don’t, but strike
back and kill, you are guilty of manslaughter. If, while
running away, your assailant follows, and if, while you
run, you hit backand kill, you are justified. If you cannot
run away, and hit back and kill, you are justified. If
a man attacks you in your house you may hit back, and if
you kill will be justified, for you are not expected to quit
your house; but if the attack be made in another’s
house you are expected to run out; if you can’t
get out, and strike and kill him, you are justified

A THREATENED SYDNEY DELUGE—A “BULLETIN” ARK.


One of the most wonderful inventions of
the age is Willson’s discovery of crystalline carbide
of calcium. There is little doubt that acetylene
gas is about to revolutionise artificial lighting.
As with all successful patents, a host of pirates
have started up to try and rob Willson of his
rights. However, the recent decision of the High
Courts at Leipsic, Germany, will somewhat damp
the ardour of Willson’s opponents. It has been
decided that Willson is undoubtedly the discoverer
of crystalline carbide of calcium. Following up
this success, the English Acetylene Company have
issued a batch of writs against infringers, and the
Acetylene Gas Co. of Australasia Ltd., of 36 Pitt-
street, Sydney—who own the Willson patents for
Australasia—are getting ready to give the Aus-
tralian pirates a surprise. Thisco. have now fitted
up some hundreds of installations around Sydney,
and are prepared to give estimates for any kind of
acetylene installations. Write direct to 36 Pitt-
street, and beware of infringers.*
Which is the easiest, to know or to find
out? Every sensible person will answer “to
know.” Read on ; then you will know that there

Yic. Postmaster-General Duffy has a
touching story re his efforts to induce the Melb.
people to cultivate a conscience in their dealings
with the telephone. Duffy hoped to make all
who used the ’phones pay for the privilege, but
failed ignominously. The other day an angry
man burst into his office and declared that he
would never more use the telephones. He had
been in the habit of using his neighbor’s machine,
he said, but never again. “ Ah,” said the P.M.G.,
“I am delighted to find that at least one man has
been convinced by the fairness of my arguments.”
“Pooh!” replied the visitor, “nothing of the
kind. I decline to use the telephone because I
have taken to the religious life, and it is painful
to me to hear over the wires the echoes of the sub
scribers cursing your idiotic management.”
Several towns in America are lit up with
Acetylene, which quite revolutionises our present
system of gas lighting. Brandt Bros., lamp
manufacturers, of Sydney and Melbourne, have
been appointed agents for Australasia, and are
busy lighting up several country towns, and are
also making carriage, buggy and table lamps
for domestic use, as well as altering the present
buggy lamps, which burn candles, into Acetylene
lamps, with a light more powerful than the
electric incandescent, at a cost of not more than
that of an ordinary candle.*
Concerning a trick of the theatrical
trade :
Years ago a young English rhymester brought out a
short-lived volume of thin verse, and a large London
critic fell on him with needless fury considering the
smallness of the animal. “ Mr. Smith’s so-called poems, ’
he said, “ are all that such things should not be—dull,
pretentious, lame, halt, and without a spark or gleam of
poetic instinct, and the writer’s obvious and boundless
conceit makes them all the more ridiculous. Plainly in
Mr. Smith’s own opinion his verses are far ahead of Shake-
speare and other bygone gods of literature.” And
Smith’s publisher reprinted this notice quite cheerfully ;
“ Mr. Smith’s . . . verses are far ahead of Shakespeare
and other bygone gods of literature,” and put the name
of the infuriated critic in full. And when the critic
saw' it he burst with rage, or was run over by an
omnibus while going to take 6s. Bd. worth of
legal advice about the outrage. The Firm gets at
Tub Bulletin in a somewhat similar fashion. The
“ Trip to Chinatou'n ” struck this paper’s Sydney critic
in one of his more Christian moments, and'he'Wrote
“ As for ‘ Chinatown ’ itself, this paper feels less critical
than it did in ’96. It has gone through many harrowing
experiences since then. It has seen the ‘Stranger in
New York,’ for one thing, and after that anybody would
feel less critical and more thankful for middle-sized
mercies. It has discovered that American musical
comedy generally contains a giddy old man who amble,
on two unreliable legs after girls, and a divorced females
and a ball, and some girls who stay out all night in short
frocks, and a fat man who swims in cocktails. And it has
learned to take these things as they come, like washing-
day and the coal-bill and other advantages and cala-
mities. By compa..oon, the ‘Trip to Chinatown’
is all that this paper 3aid it wasn’t three years ago—a
bright, clever production full of smart dialogue, and
without a dull moment, or a dull saying, or a
dull character in it from first to last. The per-
son who doesn’t see it will thereby make a mistake
that he will regret for ever afterwards. This isn’t exactly
w'hat The Bulletin remarked in its issue of 4th July,
1896, but it is how it feels now—after seeing ‘The
Stranger in New' York.’ ” And the good old Firm puts it
briefly in its Melbourne advt. : “The Bulletin says a
‘ Trip to Chinatown ’ is a bright, clever production, full
of smart dialogue, and without a dull moment, or a dull
saying, or a dull character in it from first to last.” This
w'riter had a vague idea of retaliating, but owing to the
wet weather and other things he didn’t. He once re-
ceived a perfectly harmless letter from the Firm about
nothing in particular, and by reprinting it with W'ords
judiciously left out here and there he could have shown J.
C. Williamson assolemnly alleging that he wasadoor.and
a newspaper, and a flight of steps, and that he hoped
some day to be a very bad drama in three acts, and other
strange remarks. But out of a sneaking regard for the
cheerful Cassius W. the letter w'asn’t published.
Messrs. Stewart Dawson and Co., the
popular Sydney Jewellers, have lately executed to
the order of Mr. Goouliff, of Batavia, Java, a very
unique watchchain. Mr. Goodliff sent his own
gold, and the design that he gave Stewart Daw-
son and Co. to work out was his own name, with
an Australian animal alternating with each letter
of the name. The watchchain weighs 10J ounces,
and the owner has written to Stewart Dawson and
Co., informing them that he has sent his waist-
coat to the blacksmith’s to have steel buttons put
on it.*
A LETTER from a LADY who is DESIROUS of
ASSISTING OTHERS who may be SIMILARLY
AFFLICTED.
84 Bridge-road, Richmond, Victoria,
18th July, 1899.
Mr. S. A. Palmer,
47 Bourke-street, Melbourne.
Sir,—
I certify that, for about two and a half years, I was
suffering from inflammation, peritonitis, and lung com-
plaint. I was about five months confined to bed, and
attended by seven doctors at various times; but I was
not improving under their .care, and I was told that the
only means of saving my life was by an operation, as they,
had at that time done all they could for me ; but I was
then too weak to risk the danger of an operation.
I could not eat or sleep, and was really in a very weak
condition.
I need scarcely say that doctors’ and nurses’ expenses
amounted to a considerable sum of money.
As a last resource, I was induced to try “ Vitadatio,”
and, after persevering with eight bottles, I am thankful to
say that my troubles have been overcome, and I have
been cured. I am now able to attend to my business
here, and am willing to give particulars of my case and
cure to any lady who may be suffering.
I consider it my duty to let others know that there is a
cure, because the restoration to health after a term of ill-
ness is a blessing I cannot be too thankful for. You can
use this letter for the good of others.
Yours sincerely,
(Mrs.) ANNIE V. COHEN.
Tobacconist, 84 Bridge-road, Richmond.

Mr. S. A. Palmer.
Mr. Stephen Parker, an old resident of Bairnsdale, is
very pleased to testify to the benefits he derived from
taking “ Vitadatio.” He had been ailing for about three
years, and constantly under medical treatment, until his
illness culminated in a complete collapse during the
autumn of this year. The medical opinions were diverse,
one being that he was suffering from “abscess on the
liver,” another from heart disease, another from Bright’s
disease. However, he was reduced to such a state of
prostration and suffering that the doctor did not disguise
the fact that he could not survive much longer, and that
no other medical treatment could avail than that which
had been given. Mr. Parker writes : At this stage I was
induced to try the “Vitadatio,” which I obtained locally.
The first few doses caused great pain, but advised to re-
duce the dose, I did so, with the result that I soon
began to rally and gain strength. I persevered
with the reduced dose, and before I commenced
the second bottle I observed clusters of discs com-
ing from me, which increased in number as the
medicine continued to take effect. This showed
evidently that the internal tumor or abscess was nothing
else than “ a nest of hydatids,” the presence of which
was not suspected by the doctors. I continued, and after
taking six bottles in all am thankful to say that I am as
well as I was ten years ago, and am completely restored
to health. I have no doubt whatever, through the bless-
ing of God, the medicine taken has made me quite well.
It is only right that others who may be similarly afflicted
should be encouraged to make a trial with the same
remedy.
I have great pleasure in subscribing my name,
STEPHEN PARKER.
Witness-EDWARD G. VEAL.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“ T.M.” : A recent Bulletin par. comments on
a postal official, left in charge of an office, receiving only
£75, whilst a subordinate letter-carrier received £l5O per
year. These miserable screws to men in responsible posi-
tions seem to be the rule in Sydney now, the Bull-bossed
banks being the worst offenders. A director of one of
these institutions, lately interviewed, leant back in a
well-padded chair, puffed an expensive cigar, and said :
“We don’t give enough to make a fortune out of, but we give
sufficient to enable a clerk to live in comfort, and to save
money if he is careful. They enter, at 16 to IS years old,
at 130 to £4O per annum, and, in about 10 years, reach
£lOO to £120.” Ye gods ! save money. In one institution
there is a clerk who has served 13 years, and now gets
£7O ; while another, who put in seven months without
salary, is, after another two years, getting £4O. The two
chief tellers in one large Sydney bank receive £135 per
annum ; thousands of pounds pass through their hands
daily, and they are responsible for any loss through counter-
feits, mistakes in change, &c., but, if cash is over —that’s
the bank’s perks. The ledger-keepers get from £BO to
£135, managers of branches £lOO to £3OO, with quarters.
Also, in some of them, an receiving under £2OO
is not allowed to marry. Since ’93, many branches have
closed, resulting in dismissal of least promising officers.
The managers and sub-managers, reduced to ranks, are
entitled to first promotion, and this has practically
blocked all chance of a rise to those lower in the service ;
hence a clerk entering now would be about 40 before he
could hope for matrimony. And meanwhile ?

A kanaka lately filed his insolvency-schedule
up North in forma pauperis. Transpired he had settled
two cottages, horse, buggy, &c., on “the missus.” Sun-
day-school boy, of course, Nothing approaches the modern
Sunday-school for sound commercial education.
* * * *
M‘Gregor, M.P., Vic., gives notice of a bill to
abolish the coupon system in business. The coupon
system, he argues, is mostly 50 per cent, plain steal,
since usually only half value is given when the holder of
the coupons goes to claim his goods. The system means
another middleman where there are already dozens, and
the fool customer pays the piper, mainly in receiving
14ozs. in the pound. But as each one argues: “It
would make no difference if I stood out,” legislative
action alone can squelch the evil.
* V * *
“T.A.”: Heard a spieler tell how he used to
“beat” publicans for board-and-lodging. On arrival,
would go into bar and “ shout” for landlord, changing a
fiver, and casually mentioning intention of stopping a
week or so. Would then go to bank and convert his loose
money into fiver again, and in a couple more days change
it in bar. Which appearance of flushness would make
him right for a week or two.

Went to next jeweller, who examined watch, and said
sprinsr had just been broken. Told him the facts.
“Ah,” said he, “an old game of his. Go back and
threaten to sue him.” Went back, and by dint of threats
got new'spring gratis. That lasted two weeks.

“8.5.”: In Vic. country districts you can get
beer or spirits in almost any wine-shop. I know a town-
ship in which the stores and fruitshops turn out more
drunks in a week than the local pubs, do in a year. Even
a tailor fills in slack times and Sundays at the game—
within cooee of the loik-up. Where" local-option has a
“ leg-in ” matters are still worse. The Govt, seeks to cul-
tivate a taste for wine, but until it enacts a sane and
liberal licensing-law arid shuts down hard on illicit-gi'Sg
dealers, its efforts will be wasted.

The house-removing business contrives to thrive
in Melbourne. One building society lately transplanted
17 houses from Footscray, where they stood empty, to a
block of land at Hawthorn, where they are all let.

“R.W.S.” : Mystery how art-union committees
become so promptly aware of who have won prizes. I
buy ticket in street from man who doesn’t know me from
a crow, and doesn’t take my address. Yet, on morning
after drawing, the papers give names and addresses of
winners of principal prizes, who, by the way, appear
always to live in the country. Under straight manage-
ment, the result could not possibly be known to country-
people until newspapers with result reached them. Does
any Bulletin reader know how the biz. is worked ?

“ taxameter,” the authorities should make it a compul-
sory appendage to every hire-vehicle.

“Ken.” : At an A.N.A. lodge recently I was
disgusted to hear the chief speaker denouncing the A.N.A.
art-union and the idea of only giving 2|d. out of every
shilling to charity, whereas not long before the same man
and his two charming daughters did good business raffling
£1 notes with an unlimited company (355. for 205.) in aid
of a church. And when I won one of the notes this man
sooled the young ladies on to me to cadge it back. My
wife was there, and so of course they didn’t get it. Some
people have queer notions of the Lord and charity.

Mildura fruit-growers have, in 10 years, tried a
thousand suggested ways of keeping oranges from winter,
when they are ripe, to summer, when they are most
wanted. Success is now claimed for the plan of burying
the fruit in damp sand. Oranges which have been buried
for two years have been taken up lately in a fairly satis-
factory state.
* * * *
An “ hotel barber,” the other day, in Melbourne,
was charged, among other things, with stealing a quan-
tity of “ precious stones.” Owner of precious stones said
he bought them at Colombo, but didn’t know their value,
as he hadn’t had them tested. “ Ah,” said the barber,
with a fine air of disgust, “ but I did —an' they’re no
good.”
* * • •
Vio. Factories Aot provides that no apprentice
shall be paid less than 2s. 6d. per week as wages, and
several sweating employers hit on the plan of taking
on their apprentices at a premium, said premium to be
paid at the rate of half-a-crown a week, which practically
meant that said apprentices are not paid at all. The law,
however, has lately sat heavily on this little arrangement
with costs. The attempts of the sweating employer to
drive his private buggy through Vic. Factories Act have
been many and various, but so far their success has not
equalled their ingenuity.

HE DECLARES BEFORE A J.P.
A THOROUGH CURE EFFECTED BY BILE BEANS,
THE 19th CENTURY SPECIFIC!
(From the Adelaide Advertiser.)
Mr. Thomas Henry Ames, of George-street, Parkside, when interviewed
by a reporter of the Adelaide Advertiser, as to his marvellous
recovery by Bile Beans. “By occupation I am a gardener.
About 10 years ago I contracted a severe attack of yellow
jaundice, and went into the Adelaide Hospital for treatment. My
friends did not expect me to recover, I was so bad. The hospital
doctors treated me well, and at the end of six months I left the
institution cured of the jaundice, but in a weak state of health. One of
the nurses told my wife that I would ‘ never be the same man again,’
because my stomach was in such a weak condition. My food did me
little good, and I had a bad taste in my mouth, suffered with severe
headaches, and had pains between the shoulders. My rest was also
broken, and some nights I used to toss about in bed, longing for sleep,
but was unable to obtain it. My urinary organs were very week, and I
occasionally had fits of retching ; and once or twice bringing up blood.
I took large quantities of different medicines, which I obtained from the
doctors and the chemists, but the relief the physic afforded me was
only temporary, and in a day or two all my troublesome symptoms
would return. I gave up all hopes of ever being well again.” “ But
you are well now, are you not?” “Yes. About 12 months ago I
noticed a testimonial in the Advertiser concerning a wonderful cure
through the use of Bile Beans for Biliousness. I thought I would give
the Beans a trial, although I was not over-sanguine of a cure being
effected in my case, I had been so bad for so many years. I bought a
box it Mr. Parker’s, a chemist in King William.street (near the Glenelg
railway-station), and took two of the beans for the first dose every three
days for a fortnight. After I had taken a box and a half I felt greatly relieved, the weakness
of the bowels, which was an after-effect of the jaundice, disappearing entirely. My other
organs began to perform their proper functions, the pains between the shoulders, the retching,
and the sleeplessness also disappeared within a couple of months. My appetite returned, and I
was quite restored to health. Since taking the beans I have gained 81b. in weight. I might add

State, and to substitute Declarations in lieu thereof, and for the more
entire suppression of voluntary and extra-judicial oaths and affidavits,
and to make other provisions for the abolition of unnecessary oaths.
“(Signed) THOMAS AMES.
“Declared and subscribed at Parkside in the province of South
Australia, this 24th day of June, 1899, before me.
“(Signed THOMAS SMITH, J.P.”
