POLITICAL
POINTS
The other name of the Transvaal is Na-
both’s Vineyard.
The Bung-party in Maoriland is self-
named “the Progressives.” A spiteful mis-
print in one of their own organs has converted
them into “ Grogressives.”
Maoriland is going to float another public
works loan of £1,000,000. There is very little
left now of the self-reliant, non-borrowing policy
with which the Ballance - Seddon Govt, com-
menced. All Australasian Govts, degenerate
much the same way, sooner or later.
Premier Kingston told S.A. Assembly last
week that he expects the first election to the
Federal Parliament will take place next April.
Of the present members of the Govt. Kingston
himself, Treas. Holder, and Works Minister Jen-
kins talk of trying for seats in the Central Legis-
lature.
Question put at a Gympie meeting by a
vigorous Anti-Fed. :—“lf N.S.W. had not
adopted the Commonwealth Bill, what would have
resulted?” The Chairman promptly:—“l will
reply to that question. If you had never been
born, you’d never have been able to ask so foolish
a question! ”
Brisbane Telegkaph rends its shoddy gar-
ment in grief at the imaginary grievances of the
Q. civil-servant under the rule of the Federal
Government and their possible inability to get
justice. This is red-hot, considering the sort of
life the woefully ill-paid Queensland civil-servant
leads now.
Vic. P.M.G. Duffy calmly told a recent
public gathering that Federation was mainly due
to the efforts of his illustrious father, “ whose ser-
vices had never received adequate recognition.”
The ungrateful province of Vic-
toria has only recognised thester-
ling servics of Charles Gavan
Duffy with the niggardly pen-
sion of £lOOO a-year—for the
last 40 years.
the federal situation up north.
SOUTHERN BILL: “Well, I must be going. Are
you Hominy with me? ”
NORTHERN ANTIBILL : “ You only want to rob
me.”
S. B. : “No I don’t.”
N. A. : “ That proves you do, ’cause you never
speak the truth.”
S. B. : “ All right—I do then.”
N. A. : “ There! I knew I'd make you admit it!"
Editor Wallace Nelson, of the People’s
Newspaper (Rockhampton), has recanted his anti-
Federation errors to the great disgust of the local
provincials, who have thus lost their brightest ad-
vocate. Nelson’s groundUfor abandoning the pro-
vincial policy are these : (1) The Separationists
themselves have no faith in any appeal to the
Imperial Parliament and are not likely to make
any such appeal. (2) The Colonial Boundaries’
Act renders the interference of the Imperial Par-
liament hopeless. (3) There is no hope of any
alliance between Northern and Central Separa-
tionists. Fourthly and lastly: There is little
enthusiasm for Separation in the Centre (outside
Rockhampton) and absolute apathy in the North ;
ar?d in face o’f all this the accomplishment of
territorial autonomy is at present impossible.
The other day,
Cameron, member for
Leloraine, moved a vote
of censure in Tas. Assem-
bly on Premier'Braddon for
something concerning the
Anchor tin-mine. The
House, Cameron excepted,
negatived the motion. A
few nights later it was pro-
posed to expunge the
motion from the records.
Cameron: “I knew you’d
whitewash the Premier, but
I didn’t think you’d pro-
ceed to varnish him.”
The amalgamation of
the two N.S.W. Savings
Banks into a State Bank,
which was promised in Pre-
mier Reid’sfirst programme
in 1894, turns up again look-
ing as fresh as ever on his
programme for 1899. But
apparently the idea of mak-
ing the State Bank the sole
note-issuing institution of
the province, which was in-
cluded in the 1894 pro-
gramme, has died out. The
idea now is to make a State
Land Bank out of the joint
institution. Probably by
1902 or thereabouts the idea
will turn up on the programme with still further
modifications.
The power of the President in the United
States wasoneof Hugh Nelson’s bugbears atarecent
anti-Federation demonstration. Considering that
Australia doesn’t propose to invest any individual
with the powers possessed by the American Presi-
dent, it’s rather hard to see where the analogy
comes in.
Not a single Tasmanian electorate showed
a vote against the Federal Bill. The smallest per-
centage of votes for was Richmond electorate, the
voting there being 97 for and 27 against. Zeehan
and Lyell (925 —18 and 542—10) respectively
showed over 50 to 1 in favor, while West Levon
polled 764 for and 6 against.
John Darling, jun., one of the numerous
leaders of S.A. Tory party, wants S. A. Parliament
to increase the “Property” qualification for the
Leg. Council instead of practically abolishing it,
as Kingston proposes. S.A. Council is now bossed
by a minority of 40,000 property owners, who con-
trol the adult voteless majority of 100,000.
M‘llwraith in 1888: —“ Anyone who has
watched my career since I came to the colony 35
years ago could not help seeing that I have been
working up to the idea of Australia’s being a
great independent nation. The time has now
come when the great National party should begin
its work.” It’s ever the way—from apostle to
apostate !
Rockhampton Morning Bulletin inveigh-
ing against Federation : “ Why should Rock-
hampton be sacrificed ? The Mitchell pastoral
district is the finest pastoral district in Queens-
land, and Rockhampton is its direct and natural
port.” Well, even if it is, the grass will grow in
the Mitchell pastoral district after Federation just
as it does now, and the cheerful bull will gambol
there just as it does now, and Rockhampton will
stand and swelter just where it does, and it will
be as natural a port for the Mitchell pastoral dis-
trict after Australia is united as now, when it is
disunited. There is no clause in the Common-
wealth Bill for shifting the Mitchell away
from Rockhampton in a wheelbarrow, or for
making lean and sorrowful the bulls and cOWs of
the Mitchell pastoral district.
A favorite anti-Federal gag in Queensland
just now is—“ The unemployed will rush us from
Melbourne and Sydney if we federate.” One
would imagine from this drivel that the unem-
ployed were prevented from rushing Queensland
at present by a border barrier 200 ft. high, or by a
law empowering a Queenslander to take the scalp
of any dead-beat coming in from the South. The
unemployed can rush Bananaland at present just
as much as they please, and they do rush it to
any extent that suits them. Furthermore, the un-
employed only rush to a land where times are good
and tucker plentiful, whence one can only conclude
that the anti-Federalist knows that Queensland is
going to be very prosperous under a Federal regime.
Consequently the alleged rush is a very strong
argument in favor of Federation.
Gympie Truth claims that 4 it would be
monstrous for Queensland to decide for Federa-
tion while one-third of its male population has no
vote, because that wrongly-disfranchised section
would be federated without its consent. It asks
Queensland to wjait till all male adults have a vote.
Ana when they‘have, it is quite willing’to go
ahead and .let all "the adult women of Queensland
be forced into Federation without their con-
sent. And meanwhile it is quite willing to keep
them both out of Federation without their consent.
Its excuse is that voting “yes ” commits Queens-
land irr vocably to Union, while if it votes “ No ”
it can reverse that decision later on. And so it
can, if the Commonwealth is willing to let it in
then, and'if the Jap. hasn’t got a vote by them
and doesn’t use it to keep Queensland out, and if
many other things don’t happen.
There is just this difference between
George Turner, of Victoria, and George Reid, of
N.S.W.— one is the man who does things, and the
other is the man who is going to do things.
Premier Reid announced his intention of starting
a State Land Bank before the Victorian man pro-
posed anything of the kind, but the Victorian
State Land Bank has been in operation for two
years, while the N.S.W. is just
stating all over again that it thinks about doing
something. The Reid Govt, is going to inquire
further into the matter of Old Age Pensions, and
the Treasurer hopes' before the end of the session
to be able to suggest something to his colleagues
with a possible view to business 'in 1900. The
Turner Govt, expects to take the second reading
of its Old Age Pension Bill this week, and to have
it in operation by .the end of the year or there-
abouts. The man who is Going to Do Things is a
weariness.
“COME BACK! COME BACK!” IS BEGINNING TO BE WHISTLED EVERYWHERE .
Drawn from the cabled press accounts of the sore knees, sore heads, sore feet, sprains, bruises, [?]t[?].
Though a wobbly lot generally, Reid’s
latest batch of appointments to N.S.W. Leg.
Council saved the Federal Bill in that Chamber.
The Old Guard would have at this final tussle
rejected the Address to the Queen, and thus inter-
posed a cause for delay.
Westralian Premier Forrest says that
“ under the Federation Bill the local Parliament
has no power to give differential or preferential
rates for the haulage of Collie coal,” and, he adds,
“ this is a serious matter and may mean the entire
wiping out of the industry.” Big John must have
been desperately hard pressed for a reason when
he suggested this. The Collie coal-fields are about
124 miles from Perth, and the usual rate for coal,
according to eastern ideas, is per ton per mile
-—making ss. 2d. per ton to the place where the
competition really begins. And the nearest
Australian coal-field which does an export trade
is in N.S.W., about 2500 miles away, and coal
costs somewhere between 10s. 6d. and 15s. a ton
freight to Fremantle. It has to be humped by
rail or otherwise to Perth after that. If Collie
coal can’t hold its own at Perth with a start of
from ss. to 10s. a ton, and wants differential
railway rates in addition, what kind of coal does
Bigjohn think it is ?
Several S.A. Tory politicians pledged
themselves last year to vote for Household
Suffrage if the people affirmed the principle
at the referendum. One of them, Homburg,
M. pledged himself publicly in the House.
There was a majority of 15,000 at the
referendum, and S.A. democrats are now
waiting to see how those Tory votes will go.
“The expense of franking M.L.A’s
letters will not be considerable,’’says P.M.G.
Yarney Parkes, one of the Govt, apologists
for the introduction of the system into
N. But a Labor member who
urged the system upon Govt, asserts
that his letters to constituents on Par-
liamentary business stand him in stamps
£4O a year. Another member puts his
expense at £1 per week. And these are
only moderately-accommodating members.
But taking £4O as an average, franking
M.L.A’s letters means £5OOO tax on the
Treasury. And, although M’sL.C. have no
constituents in the Assembly sense, still
they may equitably enough demand similar
concessions.
The local man in the street com-
mented strongly on the action of the, Perth
daily papers in suppressing as far as pos-
sible reference to the result of the refer-
endum on Federation in Victoria and
Tasmania. These paltry attempts to keep
the public in darkness were bad journalism
and bad business too. In view of the
possibility of the Enabling Act going to
the people, and the certainty of the exten-
sion of the franchise on the goldfields,
the Forrest crowd has all of a sudden
developed a warm admiration for woman
suffrage: The fact that more than half of W.A.
women live in the capital means, in the minds
of the W.A. Govt., that the goldfields’ vote will
be swamped.
Despite the furious assertions of Brisbane
Workeb that Q. 4 Democrats are opposed to
Federation, the fact remains that the most valu-
able advocacy of the Federal cause in the North
has come from the members of the Opposition.
Putting aside Premier Dickson, there is hardly a
Ministerialist of note who has vigorously stumped
the country, while Drake, Higgs, Dawson, Glas-
sey and Givens have done invaluable service to the
cause of,union.
R. Hawkins, the miners’ president who
represented the Broken Hill Labor bodies at the
Political Labor League conference held in Sydney
to “ try ” Ferguson and Sleath for their Federal
work and vote, went back to his - societies with a
gloomy account of political Labor organisation in
Sydney. The P.L.L. system, he said, was rotten;
and it was clear that several of the leagues
wanted to throw 'out Sleath and Ferguson in order
to run men of their own.
The mining population of Westralia begins
to hustle on the subject of Federation, and Yosper
is excuting strange contortions in his efforts to ex-
plain to his constituents why he went into the
camp of the enemy. The long-haired egotist was
bound to fall in sooner or later. Like the fly on the
chariot-wheel he took credit to himself for the
stand taken by the goldfield people during the
alluvial struggle. Vosper really fancied that the
goldfields rose up as one digger then because he
told them to do it. And he fanced they would
also join the anti-Federal party when he told
them to rise. And now the cold wind of un-
popularity blows through Vosper’s tangled mane,
and his life is full of trouble.

Dunlop Pneumatic Tyre
Company of Australasia Limited.
(To be Incorporated in Victoria under the Companies Acts).
CAPITAL - - - - £170,000,
(a) 80,000 7 per cent. Fixed Cumulative Preference Shares of £1 each, (Now offered to the Public),
fully paid.
DIVIDED INTO
(6) 20,000 per cent. Deferred Non-cumulative Preference Shares
of £1 each, fully paid.
(c) 70,000 Ordinary Shares of £1 each, fully paid, the dividend on
which, is not to exceed 9 per cent, in any year.
Reserved for the Vendor
in part payment of
purchase.
Issue of 80,000 7 per cent. Cumulative Preference Shares of £1 each.
To toe Fully Paid Up.
lIIESE SHARES will be Preferential as to Capital. They will also be entitled to a fixed cumulative Preferential Dividend of 7 per
cent, per annum, payable half-yearly, and will be entitled to participate pro rata with the Deferred Stock in all dividends
declared in any year after payment of a dividend of 9 per cent, on the Ordinary Stock. These Shares will be specially
protected by the Articles of the Company, which will provide, as a fundamental article, that no Debenture or Mortgage on
the assets of the Company shall at any time be given without the previous consent of at least two-thirds of the Preferential
Shareholders for the time being of such shares present personally or by proxy at a meeting to be specially convened for the
purpose.
PROVISION will also be made in the Company’s Articles for the creation of a Reserve Fund of £40,000 for the further benefit of
these Shares; and one-fourth of the net profits in each year, after payment of the dividends on the Fixed Preferential and
Deferred Preferential Shares, will be placed to the credit of this Fund.
The Dividend on these Shares will be paid Free of Exchange at the Bank of Australasia at Melbourne, Sydney, Adelaide, Brisbane, Perth;
and Christchurch and Auckland, N.Z.
SUBSCRIPTION for the above 80,000 Shares of £1 each is now invited, payable as follows:—2s. 6d. per Share on Application;
Balance on Allotment.
Note. Upon payment of the 20s. (twenty shillings) per Share as above mentioned, no further liability whatever will attach to these Shares, and an Official Certificate to that effect will then b e
issued by the Company.
The whole of this issue of 80,000 Shares has been underwritten.

PROSPECTUS.
THIS COMPANY is being formed to acquire as a going concern and carry on the well-known
* business m Australasia of THE DUNLOP PNEUMATIC TYRE COMPANY (AUS-
TRALASIA) LIMITED, together with its goodwill (including Trade Marks and Trade Name)
Patent Rights, Leases, Furniture and Plant in Australasia ; and an agreement has been entered into
securing to this Company the exclusive rights throughout Australasia for all Inventions and Patent
Rights relating to Eneumatic Tyres that may hereafter belong to THE DUNLOP PNEUMATIC
TYRE COMPANY LIMITED, of England, at cost price, and special facilities are also given to this
Company in regard to the purchase of raw material for the manufacture of Tyres. The English Dun-
lop Company has agreed not to compete with this Company in Australasia, thereby securing to this
Company the exclusive sales of the celebrated DUNLOP CYCLE TYRES, and also of the DUNLOP
CARRIAGE TYRES.
The Australasian business was formed by the world-famed DUNLOP PNEUMATIC TYRE
COMPANY, of England, in the year 1893. The net profits for the Australasian business for the last
year have been greater than for any previous year, and amounted to £14,707 19s. lid., and the
sales of Tyres in Australasia for the same year numbered 55,569.
The Head Office and Manufactory of the Australasian Company is situate in Melbourne, and
the Company has Branch Depots (where a full supply of Stock is kept) at Sydney, Adelaide, Brisbane
Perth, and Christchurch (New Zealand).
The business of The Dunlop Pneumatic Tyre Company (Australasia) Limited will be taken
over by this Company as a going concern from the Ist day of August, 1899, and the Company
will have the benefit of all profits from that date. The accounts of the Australasian business have
been submitted to the well-known Accountants, Thomas Brentnall, Esq., F.1.A.Y., and J. E.
Bowes, Esq., Chartered Accountants, who have given the following Certificates with respect to the
sales and profits for the last two years, and also as to the sales for the present year up to the
Ist July, and it will be noticed therefrom that the business is worked practically on a cash basis:
‘‘The Equitable Building, George-st., Sydney, 9th August, 1899.
Messrs. Mullens & Co. and Messrs. Randle & Willis, Sydney.
“ Gentlemen, —In accordance with your instructions, I have made a careful examination of the
books of the Dunlop Pneumatic Tyre Company, Limited, kept in Melbourne, for the two years
ended 26th January, 1899 (but treated by the Company as up to the last day of the month), and these
show that the net profits of the Company’s business in Australasia for that period have amounted
to .£26,683 Bs. 2d., viz. :
For the year ended 27th January, 1898 ... ... ... ... £11,975 8 3
» >. 26 th January, 1899 ... ... ... .” 14J07 19 11
...... £26,683 8 2
“ Ample provision, m my opinion, has been made for bad and doubtful debts, and also for the
depreciation and replacement of Tools, but I think an allowance of £3OO a year should be made for
depreciation of Furniture, Fittings, and Fixed Plant. This would still leave a NET profit of
£11,675 Bs. 3d. for the year ended 27th January, 1898, and £14,407 19s. lid. for the year ended 26th
January, 1899.
“The Net Sales for the year ended 27th January, 1898, amounted to £76,029 7s. 10d., and to
£91,870 os. lid. for the year ended 26th January, 1899. This shows an increase of £15,840 13s. Id. in
the sales of the latter year, and at the same time the percentage of net profit has, I find, been fully
ma in tai ned. —Y ours faith fully,
“J. E. BOWES, Chartered Accountant.”
“424 Chancery Lane, Melbourne, 20th July, 1899.
“Messrs. Moule, Hamilton, and Kiddle,
“ Solicitors, Melbourne.
“ Gentlemen, — .
“As requested, I have examined the Accounts of the DUNLOP PNEUMATIC TYRE
COMPANY (AUSTRALASIA) LIMITED for the Two Years ended 31st January, 1899
and beg to report as follows :
“The Sales for the year ended 31st January, 1898, amounted to £76,029 7s. lOd
and for the year ended 31st January, 1899, to £91,871 Os. lid., being an average of
£83,950 4s. 4d. per annum.
“The Net Profit for the same periods was £11,975 Bs. 3d. and £14,707 19s. lid.
respectively, being an average of £13,341 14s. id. per annum, and equal to over 15 per cent, per
annum on the net sales.
“The total Sales for the Six Months ended 30th June, 1899, have reached £48,854
Is. 5d., as compared with £44,296 7s. Id. for the corresponding period of 1898.
“ The number of Tyres sold during the last financial year amounted to 55,569.
“ The business is, practically, worked on a cash basis, the bad debts for the two years under
review representing less than (155.) fifteen shillings per £lOO upon the turn over for the whole
period.
“ The above figures speak for themselves as to the magnitude and soundness of the business of
the Company.
“ I am, Gentlemen, yours faithfully,
‘•THOS. BRENTNALL, F.LA.V.”
As appears from the Accountant’s Certificates, the net profits for the year ending January 31st last
were £14,707 19s. lid., and as the preferential dividend on the present issue of £BO,OOO only amounts
to £5600, THESE PROFITS WOULD ENABLE SUCH DIVIDEND TO BE PAID MORE
THAN TWO AND A-HALF TIMES OVER. In addition to this, it will be noticed from the
certificates that the sales of the Dunlop Tyres for the present year exceed the sales for the correspond-
ing months of last year.
The recent reductions on the cost of Cycles have brought them into universal use, and they are
now regarded in almost every household as a practical necessity, and there can be no doubt that the
demand for DUNLOP TYRES will continue to increase each year. It is intended to further extend
the scope of the business by. introducing into these colonies the DUNLOP PNEUMATIC
CARRIAGE TYRES, which are now so extensively used throughout Europe and America.
The DUNLOP TYRES are the world’s standard, and all the principal cycle manu-
facturers throughout the world fit their machines with Dunlop Tyres, and this Company will
acquire the exclusive right for Australasia to the valuable Trade Marks and Trade Names of this
business.
The Vendor and Promoter, Richard Garland, Esq., Managing Director of The Dunlop Tyre
Company Limited, Toronto, Canada, has fixed the total purchase price at £135,000, and has
included the Trade Marks and Trade Names, as well as the bemefit of the valuable contract
obtained from the English Dunlop Pneumatic Tyre Company Limited, as part of the goodwill of
the business sold to this Company, and has apportioned the value thereof at £125,000. Out of
the total purchase price, only £45,000 is payable in cash, the balance thereof being repre-
sented by the said deferred Preference Shares and the Ordinary Shares. The Com/am/ will
commence haziness free from debt, and with a cash working capital of £35,000 (less only the usual
brokers’ commission of sixpence per share) standing to its credit, which will be ample for all pur-
poses, and will enable it to carry on its business on the best cash terms.
The business will be carried on without interruption, upon the lines which have proved so suc-
cessful in the past, AND AS THE HEAD OFFICE OF THE COMPANY WILL NOW BE
IN AUSTRALIA instead of England as heretofore, it. is anticipated that the business will be
carried on to better advantage than ever.
The Contract for Sale and Purchase is dated 10th August, 1899, and is made between the said
Richard Garland of the one part, and Frederick Arthur Moule, Esq;, of Market-street Melbourne
Solicitor, as Trustee for the Company of the other part, and the contracts between ’tin* Dunlop
Pneumatic Tyre Company (Australasia) Limited, and the Dunlop Pneumatic Tyre Company Limited
respectively, and the said Richard Garland, are dated the 15th day of May, 1899.
The Vendor and Promoter will bear all cost in relation to the formation.of theDompany up to
Allotment, including the payment of a commission averaging not more than 10 per cent, for under-
writing the present issue, payable partly in cash and partly in the Ordinary Shares.
The only preliminary expenses which will be payable by the Company will be the usual brokerage
of sixpence per share on the Prefeience Shares subscribed for by the public.
The said Contracts, and Mr. Brentnall’s and Mr. Bowes’ Certificates above referred to, and the
proposed Memorandum and Articles of Association of the Company, may be inspected at the Office
of the Solicitors to the- Company.
Where no allotment is made the deposit will be refunded in full, and where the allotment is less
than the amount applied for, the balance of the deposit will be applied towards the instalment
payable on allotment. Allotment will be made in .the order of priority of application.
Prospectuses and Forms of Application can be obtained from the Brokers of the Company ; or at
the branches of the Bank of Australasia, or from the Solicitors of the Company. " ’
Dated this 19th day of August, 1899.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
A RULE OF THE A.J.O.
Set in verse by “ The Banjo.”
Gome all ye bold trainers attend to my song,
It’s a rule of the A.J.O.
You mustn’t train ponies, for that’s very wrong
By the rules of the A.J.O.
You have to wear winkers when crossing the street,
For fear that a pony you’d happen to meet ;
If you hear one about, you must beat a retreat—
That’s a rule of the A.J.O.
And all ye bold owners will find without fail
By the rules of the A.J.O.
The jockey-boys-’ fees you must pay at the scale—
It’s a rule of the A.J.O.
When your horse wins a fiver, you’ll laugh, I'll be bound,
But you won’t laugh so much by the time that you’ve
found
That the fee to the boy is exactly Ten Pound 1
That’s a rule of the A.J.O.
And all ye bold “ Books ” who are keeping a shop,
In the rules of the A.J.O.
There’s a new regulation that says you must stop !
That’s a rule of the A.J.O.
You must give up your shop with its pipes and cigars
To an unlicensed man who is thanking his stars,
While you go and bet in the threepenny bars—
That’s a rule of the A.J.O.
And all ye small jockeys who ride in a race,
In the rules of the A.J.O.
If owners’ instructions are “ Don’t get a place,"
By the rules of the A.J.O.
Youmust.ride the horse out,—though, of course, if you do
You will get no more mounts, it’s starvation to you.
But, bless you, you’ll always find plenty to chew
In the rules of the A.J.O.
* * * *
Another Q. “cert.” (Sweetheart) went down
badly in the Tramway Handicap at Sydney Tatt’s meet-
ing. Those behind the filly reckoned it all over bar
shouting, but she only got third, and that through sheer
gameness. Her Stradbroke Handicap performance at
Eagle Farm (Q.) made 6 furlongs, with 7.8 up, look par-
ticularly good. Punters piling it on Boreas for the
Epsom (through his Bananaland form) should note Sweet-
heart’s failure at Randwick, as well as that of Babel and
one or two other Northern fliers on same course.
* * * *
Yet another Gozo to the fore. In the important
Spring Handicap at Randwick last week, nothing had a
chance with the ugly nag Plutus, who got his large
Roman nose in front at half way and kept it there, win-
ning without being hit or kicked by half-a-dozen lengths.
The Splendor horse, Superb, who carried much
Sydney cash in last Caulfield Cup and got mixed up in
the Robin Hood smash, put up 9,4 and helped himself to
the Welter at Randwick t’other day in a very summary
manner. Superb is now favorite for the Epsom, and, if
“ after it,” should nearly win.
* * * *
The three place-getters in Hampden Stakes at
Randwick t’other day—“ Bill ” Forrester’s The Chief,
Cranberry, and The Grafter’s brother, Gauleon—should
have a say in deciding some of the coming big spring
events. Last season few horses could give The Chief a
start over any distance up to a mile, and he is not much
worse now. Since Cocos, Sydney hasn’t seen many better
youngsters than Cranberry, and he looks the best of good
things for A.J.O. Derby ; anyhow, whatever downs him
won’t miss the Melb. classic event by much. Gauleon has
grown into a really fine horse, and his two last runs when
half-well suggest that he is as good as his breed.
* * * *
Last year’s champion, Cocos, was seen out in
the weight-for-age event at Sydney TatWsaU’s Club
meet last week, and ran respectably consider/V that he
is only half-well, and not too fond of a wet Course. He
has changed little—has filled out some and looks rather
sounder about the pins, but still has that jaded, pes-
simistic look about the eyes. He started at “ your own
price,” so apparently in running fourth he did all that
Payten expected. * * *
Apropos Gamecock’s recent win of Bendigo
“Australian” Cup, it is worth mentioning that
owner Pearson has lately had a good time at coursing.
Waterhen, then freshly purchased, got away with Benalla
(Vic.) Waterloo for him. Aqua Fori is appropriated
Moo'nee Valley Oup, and Fanny Parnell secured Gipps-
land O.O’s championship. Hundreds of others have been
years at the game and never raised a flag. Luck or
judgment? Writer thinks the latter.,
Champion trotter Fritz has so far been the
only animal, man or beast, to run Gov. Beauchamp to a
pitch of enthusiasm. At Forbes (N.S.W.) show, when
Fritz trotted his mile on the slippery racecourse in a
shade under 2min. 14secs., His Ex. showed the liveliest
interest in the champion, and asked to be allowed to hold
him whilst being photo’d.
* * * *
The weak spot in Fleet Admiral’s chance for
the Melbourne Cup is a cracked heel, which threatens to
give a lot of trouble to t.he trainer. If the horse were
not so fleshy and hearty he could be safely rested awhile,
but a fair amount of work is necessary in his case, where-
fore the healing of the heel will be a slow process, any-
how.
Talking of horses that easily run to fat—how
will Chesney be atf the end of his voyage to England ? A
disposition to grossness is evident in this handsome colt,
whose own brother, Mai-olio, was ruined for racing
purposes by the pampering the Redfearns gave him after
he had won Melb. Cup. Safe thing to bet that Chesney
will be as slow as a funeral first time he is galloped on
t other side.
* * * •
The V.R.C. marked the return of its old
prosperity by voting a douceur of £lOOO to George Wat-
son, whose services as starter date back to 1852. A
recent Bulletin termed George Watson the “ ex-starter,’
whereas he is still on the joo, giving the signal for the
machine to let the gee-gees go, instead of merely drop-
ping a flag as in (lays of yore. Ihe machine is now the
Prince of Starters, and veteran George is the equerry
thereto.

, • * *
Re death of the French horse Holocauste in
last English Derby. Some London vets, ascribe the
occurrence to Tod Sloan’s method of riding—which, it is
averred, extorts speed by frightening the animal, and
while in position Sloan has no control over his mount.
On the morning of Derby day Holocauste could not have
been bought for 10,000 guineas, but in the afternoon he
wasn’t worth much more than two guineas. The slaughter-
man who put the great grey out of his misery said that
Holocauste dead and in the lump would realise 255.
Broken up and cooked he might fetch about 50s. or a
little more, thus; flesh (catsmeat) 365., hide 155., entrails
for dogs’ meat 2s, bones Is. Total £2 14s. The hoofs
usually yield 6d. to 9d. each, but the owner wanted them.
Up to the end of the fifth test-match the Aus-
tralian XI. had played 29 matches, winning 14, losing 3,
and drawing 12; and had scored 1i.,816 runs for 397
wickets against the Britishers’ 10,746 for 498 wickets, or
an average for each Australian wicket of 29,79 against
21.57 for each English individual effort. The highest
single innings score was England’s 576 in the fifth “test,
and the lowest was Leicestershire’s second-innings effort of
21. In the test events honors were fairly easy, Australia
having won one and four being drawn, three in England’s
favor. Although the present eleven is reckoned by many
to be well ahead of its predecessors, it hasn’t much the
best of the figures when compared with the 17 wins, 7
draws, and 5 losses of the ’96 team for.the same number
of matches. Allowing' 2 points for a win and 1 for a draw
Trott’s team gets home by 1 point.
* * * *
The Australians began well in their match
against Middlesex, scoring 300 and odd for five wickets.
Everybody was in double figures when this paper went to
press, and Darling and Iredale were not-out with 78 and
76 respectively.
* * * *
Thk Bulletin’s London correspondent:—“ The
fourth test-match, its morals and ini morals. Have you
ever tried to shift a middle-aged native bear from the 50-
feet high prong of a hoary gum ? You fill him with shot
in front, between and behind; rattle his knuckles with
lead, riddle his ears and pepper his hide generally, and
he ignores you and your cartridges beyond an occasional
supercilious glance downwards as if to enquire, ‘ What-
ever are you at ? ’ So Mr. Noble, of Sydney, at Old Traf-
ford this week. In a quarter of a century of the world’s
cricket I have never seen, read, or heard of a perform-
ance to equal it. A man with self-restraint like that
could be trusted with a full collection-plate at even-
ing service when the lights go out. In the first
innings ‘ M.A.’ went in third wicket down (for
14), and carried his bat out for 60, his first 10 taking-
one hour. In the follow-on he made 89, having
totalled 149 in eight and a-half hours, and g; t out to the
only bad stroke he made in the match. Tedious? Well,
Alick Bannerman some years ago stonewalled against
Victoria, and the Argus] Age & 00. howled. Two or
three days later A.B. repeated his comatose cricket but
for Australia ». England at Melbourne, and was crushed
with compliments. There is a belief here among ‘ green
and gold’supporters that the boys loaf, or ‘ laekadaise ’
when things took easy. Quaife, Fry, Ranji and Maclaren
for 47 was too flattering for them, and the attack may
have waned, though Hayward (missed three times) saved
England.
“ The crowd ” (continues our correspondent)
“were rough, but, through all, good-tempered. They
whistled ‘ The Dead March,’ ‘We Won’t Go Home Till
Morning,’ ‘Home, Sweet Home,’ &e., and when the um-
pire did not satisfactorily respond to an appeal, called
out, ‘ Change the ruddy umpire!’ Darling particularly
seemed to feel the ‘ ragging,’ which, combined with
memories of English ultra-keenness to waste time, &c.,
at Nottingham, nerved him to prevent Tyldesl'ey fielding
for Hayward, and J.D., cricketer he, has been got on the
‘ raw ’by the M. 0.0. For the committee of Lords abso-
lutely refused to allow the Australians any voice in the

if a certain man be selected we shall not play.’ Cries of
‘Name?’ ‘ Barlow,’ replied the Major. Lord Hawke
blusteringly jumped up, ‘ Oh, he’s been giving out lots
of Englishmen lately, too.’ Lord Harris (in chair),
‘ Well, as he does not appear to please either side,’ etc.
“Everyone will be glad to hear ” (concludes the
writer) “ that Frank Iredale has reached form and nerve
at last. His 67 in. two attempts for once out was im-
mensely valuable, and lie played without that wretched,
finicky, groping for ‘off-balls’ that has so often contri-
buted towards his necklace. Clem. Hill, operated on for

puts all his ill-luck down to the cross-eyed people he

* * * *
The discarding of Hirst and the non-inclusion
of Burnup (of Kent), whose bowling might have “come
off” just once again, were the only possible mistakes
which the Englishmen made in selecting their final test-
team. With the luck of the toss in their favor and an
exceptionally big and fast score to start with, they failed
to win the game—simply because their bowling and field-
ing didn’t beat the Australians. One bowler, Lockwood,
put up the fine performance of seven wickets for 71 in
their first innings, but he was not even decently sup-
ported except by A- O. Jones, whereas in the two first
tests where our Jones bowled brilliantly, he got respect-
able assistance from Howell, Trumble, and Noble. When
the batting and bowling , averages for the full English
season come to be published, the consistent success of
the Australians will appear rather puzzling. Their bowl-
ing in all matches has not been up to the figures of
Trott’s last team in England, yet they have done much
better against picked teams on the whole. They have,
as a rule, got rid of the “crack” English batsmen
cheaply in classic matches, whilst the English bowlers
with the top averages—notably Mead and Rhodes—have
been unable to get rid of them cheaply in the hour
of Hingland’s need, although it must be admitted
that the Australians were practically beaten by the
bowlers in the % third test-match when rain intervened.
The one certain fact discoverable from the test-match
figures is the comparative consistency and solidity of the
Australian bowlers. Jones took one or more (usually more)
wickets in each of the seven innings where he was called
upon. Trumble and Noble took wickets six times out of .
seven, and Howell five times out of six. The Englishmen
had no professional bowler who seemed good enough for
them to swear by. Rhodes and Hearne were the only
ones they tried in three matches. Hearne took wickets
five times out of six attempts. Rhodes vva's : successful in
four out of five. Young didn’t fail in either of his four
trials, but Bradley drew a blank twice out of four times,
and Lockwood once out of twice. In all five test, matches
the Australians took 67 wickets, whereas the Englishmen
toolt'7s'Australian wickets. There Were sundry “run
outs” on both sides, and one Englishman (Briggs)
couldn’t bat for his side. The principal bowling averages
come out as follows :

figures for test-matches and all matches. Figures will
prove anything, but in this case they can’t disprove the
solidity of the cricket played by Darling and co. The Aus-
tralians were always “there or thereabouts” when wanted;
sometimes they got there more than at other times, but
they have never seemed to be outbatted and outbowled
in one and the same match—test or otherwise. Their
aggregate average for the five tests was rather better
than the all-England’s— -viz,, 2475 runs for 80 wickets

Five Australian batsmen—Noble, Hill, Wor-
rall, Trumble, and M‘Leod -have a much better average
for test than for all matches. Trumper and Iredale lost
very little ground in the tests, but Darling, Gregory,
Kelly, Laver, and Jones fell considerably below their
figures for “ all matches.” For All-England, the most
conspicuous test failures were Townsend, Quaife, Fry,
Tyldesley, Gunn, Storer, and Jessop ; and Hayward alone
was a conspicuous success.
* * » »
London Referee said of the second test-match :
“ The downfall of nine of our picked batsmen for 73 runs
on a good wicket is inexplicable, though by many it is
attributed to natural nervousness over the engagement.
It was really the bowling of Jones that worked all the
mischief, for at the outset he was unplayable, and even
so gifted a batsman as C. B. Fry was mixed up by his
deliveries, while Ranjitsinghi, after one beautiful stroke
in the first innings, was hopelessly beaten. In the field,
our men were simply outclassed by the colonials, and our
attack was very weak. Our need is a fast bowler. Hirst
signalised his removal from the eleven by capturing
eight of the Australian wickets for 48 and five for 101,
when they opposed Yorkshire earlier in the week, and it
is to be regretted that he did not again find a place in
the team.’’
The prattle of learned cricket commentators on
the latest cable-reports is always amusing. Re the open-
ing of the final test-match, the Argus savant observed
“ All the conditions of play up to lunch-time point to the
fact that the Englishmen, having won the toss, have set
themselves resolutely to the task of making defeat im-
possible. Both Jackson and Hayward evidently deter-
mined to wear out the bowling.” The result of the Eng-
lishmen’s policy was 435 runs, for 4 wickets, when stumps
were drawn —a fairly striking contrast to the score of 238
which the Australians made when they got first-day’s use
of a fast wicket in the No. 1 test. An All-England team
can only play one sort of batting game effectively, and
that’s the spirited game. When nervousness, or a little
bad luck at the outset, prompts them co play to keep their
wickets up, their wickets go down. Their policy should
always be to collar the bowling, or perish in the attempt,
seeing that the Australian bowling is always dangerous to
them until it gets badly punished. No English team has
played a successful “steady” game against it this
season—barring the Kent men, who won because
they hadn’t many runs to make altogether.
Here is the marked point of difference between
Australian and English play. Our batsmen, with
unlimited time at their disposal, learn to beat the
bowling without slogging it, as when they slowly piled
up the score in the second innings of the fourth test what
time the disappointed Manchester crowd were w histling
“ Dead March in Saul.” Undoubtt dly they have a gr< at
pull over their rivals in this respect. Their patience is a
double virtue, serving them both ways when they make a
long slow score. The Englishmen, left with a big chance
of defeat and no chance of victory, can’t play a spirited
game. Then out they go. It will be said that this excuse
doesn’t apply to the sick performances of Stoddart’s last
team in Australia. True ; but the men of Stoddart s last
team were mostly nervous batsmen in the four test
matches they lost , as Tiib Bulletin remarked at the time.

Letters from England to his family in Adelaide
describe cricketer Clem Hill’s illness as being 1 bronchitis,
and when last mail left he was unable even to write,
members of the team doing that for him. But what
puzzles Australians is the absolute silence of the cable-
man.
* * * *
Printed that prior to the beginning of the fourth
test-match the Australians were practically a band of
cripples. Wort-all, Darling, Howell, Jones, Gregory, and
Trumble had the services of masseurs right through the
three days. Excitement reached bursting-point, and all
were brought up to the, highest tension on the last day.
So great was the anxiety that Darling, Trumble, Hill, and
Johns simply couldn’t watch the play, and sought relief
by lying dow-n in the dressing-room.
JJ ”« * * *
M‘Le,od’s 108 (for once out) in the fifth test
saved Australia and enables the 10th Eleven to come
back the only team that has not lost a test-match. His
success was all the more singular because he is admittedly
the failure of the team. His performance will atone for
many of his failures in the tour.

Footballing Melb. saw what was practically its
first Rugby match on Saturday, for all previous exposi-
tions of the English game were played in the presence of
hut a few enthusiasts. But Rev. Mullineux and his team
were so judiciously boomed that 10,000 turned up, and of
these at least 9500 left under the belief that his reverence
ought to be boiled alive for false pretences. The Rugby
game suffered, in the first instance, by comparison. The
afternoon opened with a match under Australian rules
between the Vic. League combined clubs and Ballarat.
Despite heavy wind, the game was lively and scientific,
the goldfielders eventually scoring 6 goals 1 behind to 4
goals 11 behinds, or 37 points to 35.
Then everyone anxiously awaited the Rugby
game. The crowd had some misgivings when the goal-
posts were erected so close together ; “ Blime 1 they
remarked, “any of our blokes’d kick a goal from atf
way in that scrap of a ground.” When the game started
there was no hostility, only contempt, and the crowd
roared with amusement when the first scrum was formed.
“Looks like a chap feedin’ fowls,” was the comment;
but the frequency of the scrimmage and out-of-bounds-
plav soon roused the indignation of the spectators. Ihe
off-side rule also bothered a lotr “ Booh- ooh ! you funk!
they howled at a Victorian as he stood by and let one of
the Englishmen mark the ball, and it was only when
a big local man got an Englishman by the neck
and tried to tear his head off, that they saw
a single acceptable point in the game. Iha t
touched the hearts of all. But there vyere few such
incidents, and as it was apparent through all that the
Victorians were getting badly licked, the crowd began to
clear out lOmin. after the start. By half-time only a
small proportion of spectators was left, and the prevail-
ing opinion was that they never saw such a silly game in
all their lives, and that, if that was football, the onty
thing to be thankful for was that the Englishmen only
came out here once in 12 years or so.
Those who knew anything about Rugby saw
that the game was not one to impress an outsider. The
Englishmen, and especially Ranji Mullineux, who looked
like a small bov who had strayed into a man s match,
played all over the locals, though it was only on about
four or five occasions that they exhibited any of the
brisk and lively passing that is the feature of Rugby.
Outside the rev. captain himself, the man most watched
was the one sinner who repented Timms, a prominent
Vic. footballer years ago, when he was wing-man for
Geelomr. Since then, Timms has gone to England and
learnt Rugby, but apparently he is not the crack
at it that he was in the game of his youth.
The Vies, on Saturday turned out in white togs
and the white man’s burden proved much too heavy for
15 men got together with a fortnight s practice. Though
they numbered no Australian-game players among them
being all English, Sydneysiders and Maorilanders—they
seemed to have become infused with its principles, and
went in for a lot of long kicking under circumstances
that did more harm than good. In the Australian game
it is always best for a man to take his kick rather than
try to pass an opponent. In Rugby a run is worth 20
kicks, yet the Victorians attempted to run scarcely at an.
Little wonder was it then that the game concluded . Eng-
land 5 goals 1 try—3o points to nil.
In one of the England v. N.S.W. Rugby
matches, O’Donnell, a Cornstalk “five-eights, got a
little out of position, and rev. captain Mullmeux called
to him in a hurt voice : “ I say, faive-eighths, you are in
my way.” O’Donnell grinned, and a big forward sang
out—“Ohlbaijove! Get out of the gentlemans way-
how very unthought.fnl of you.” Then ihe ball came out
of the scrum, and the big forward rushed and sat down
on the little parson’s head.

The most screechingly - humorous of, P ar f on
Mullineux’s list of “ dirty little tricks ” pine: i <ed by Aus-
tralian Rugby players is his “fourihy (.my beloved
brethren) —the shouting after an opponent for a pass.
The beastly colonial who first started this most have been
a bit of a humorist. One can imagine the disgust of the
little parson on discovering that the person io whom he
had just trustfully passed over his shoulder was a large,
bull-beaded Australian forward. Though it is playing it
rather low, the trick is rather clever than otherwise, tot
the player who passes the ball out promiscuously to
whomsoever calls for it, deserves to be taken down occa-
sionally.

The average football barracker is not much im-
pressed with fair-play as an axiom. A few weeks ago
writer was beside an English barracker who jubilated
much at a manifestly mistaken free-kick awarded the
Britishers, and which got them out of a tight place, lhe
other Saturday he was next to an Australian ditto, who
yelled when Spragg got the ball and secured a try.
“ Why, that was passed forward fully five yards surely
they’ll never get that!” They did, however, and lie
cheered lustily, and objurgated the Britishers—who were
one point ahead—for wasting time and trying to ‘ psint
Australia out of the game.
* * * *
“ F.J.” : Certain N.S.W. Rugby bigwigs seem
to have been completely under the domination of
Mully and Co. These parasites (some of whom
never played football in their lives) would rather
cringe to our visitors and Govt. House for the
sake of a little tinpot social recognition than en-
danger their present precarious popularity by insisting
on the choosing of our best representative teams. At
the recent Sydney matches there was in the members
stand a big- contingent of strov-hatted johnnies, who
applauded frantically any good English footballer s work,
but totally ignored any brilliant light-blue play. Yet
such is the inconsistency of these cattle that they went
into raptures over the news of Trurnper’s big cricket-
score. Gussie is half a woman, anyhow !
* ». * *
In answer to Melbourne Mayor M‘Eacharn s
condemnation of football as low, brutal, and un-English,
they trotted out a newspaper-account of a recent
English match, during the course of which 23 damaged
policemen were carried off the'field.
Rain materially affected Sydney, theatre-audi-
ences on Saturday night hut not the usual weekly gathering
at Golden Gate A.O. People wedged themselves in till
900 were there, and breathing-space was at a premium.
Eighteen of 29 entrants (a record) knocked lumps off each
other iii the first, portion of the initial round of an 8.7
novice competition. They rolled over one another,
fought back on to the ropes, hanged into the padded
posts, and dozens of times went within an ace-of hitting
too-close ring-siders. On occasions, excitement was in-
tense, then amusement would reach the same pitch.
Screeches of laughter alternated with yells of applause. At
such a furious clip did they all “ get down to it ” that a
single round saw some heats through, while most ended
in two, and the longest only occupied four.
* « * *
Little “Bert” Weekes, who knocked-out
Summer Hill “ Billy ” Smith at Golden Gate Club (Syd-
ney), Wednesday last, can fight under 7st., and is as stiff
a piece of pug-machinery as N.S.W. can boast. Gritty,
quick and clever, he’s “ there ” all the t ime : getting out
of distance with clock-like precision, he hustles and
harries the life out of the other man. The Sydney midget
wants a “go” with any on the continent, and will give
away lbs. (within reason) sooner than miss a chance.
* * » •
“ Old Timer ” : In face of Sydney police action
re the (prohibited) Dunn-Conlon 20-rounds bout two
weeks ago, it seems strange that several fierce bashing
battles have since occurred at Golden Gate Club free
from blue-coated interference. Probably the same occult
influence that sweeps the freshly-started tote-man out of
existence, while it allows the fat and old-established
“ little-double ” layer to continue unmolested and still
further fatten, is responsible? Had those handling the
Dunu-Conlon affair “ known the ropes” that fight would
have now been ancient history.

The unco guid had a night out when Mr.
Griffith’s “ Bill to amend the Towns Police Act in so far
as it prohibits wholesome athletic sports on Sundays”
was before N.S.W. Assembly. Pharisaical hogwash was
much on top until “no quorum ” closed the debate.
Mr. Griffith’s idea is to have football, cricket, golf, and
such like games countenanced within certain distances of
any place of worship. Nowhere but in a parson-ridden
country would opposition be offered to so up-to-date and
rational a proposal. Cycling, driving, yachting, &c., are
not (and could not be) tabooed ; then why other pastimes?
Similar arguments were adduced against opening the Art
Gallery and the Zoo on the Sabbath. How far have the
objections then offered been borne out ? The fact of
many thousands going miles to see hurling-matches
Sunday after Sunday shows conclusively that a big sec-
tion of the community want some means of making the
Day of Rust more agreeable. One member contended
that “the Continental Sunday” and a demand for the
opening of business-places would follow! Golfers seem
scared concerning probable action by the police with
regard to their game, but there’s no occasion for alarm.
As they make no noise whatever, it would be hard to find
a pretext for shutting them up.
* * * *
The mining boom, which has recommenced since
the Bier scare comparatively died down, has distinctly
benefited the Sydney Safe Deposit. Brokers and buyers
alike see the folly of keeping valuable scrip either in office
safes or private residences, and there have been large
numbers of visitors to view the massive strength of the
Deposit vaults, with a proportionate increase in the
number of clients. We are requested to state that the
management freely invite a visit. The Deposit is situated
in Ash-street, (back of Paling and Co’s), off George-street.*

The old wooden bicycle on which the immortal
Blondin gyrated across a rope stretched over Niagara
Falls was recently bought by a collector of curios at an
auction sale in Paris for three francs. And yet that ride
was one of the wonders of the world.

In One Man’s Life.
When completed, the cathedrals of St. Patrick
in Melbourne and of St. Mary in Sydney,
will be imposing examples of Australian religious
fervour. They bring Art to the aid of Faith ; an
impressive architecture adorns and enforces the
hereditary instinct of trust in a creed, the heredi-
tary ache for assurance of a divinity superior to
humanity, of a world beyond the grave.
Yet there is a sense in which every cathedral is
a tomb. Reason lies buried there. Science lies
buried there. To gain what we hope we must
yield what we know. That one cathedral may
triumph to the skies, a hundred thousand minds
are submerged in unillumined deeps. Was there
ever a stained window’s gorgeous glory so majesti-
eal as the white dawn of Truth, if we could see it,
in some groping human brain ?
Yet the saying is not conclusive. We can learn
so little ; stumble so pitifully along the vast roads
of the confronting universe. Surely it is possible
to merge the legend in the poetry ; to forget the
dogma in remembering the human source from
which it flows; and to consider the cathedral a
symbol in stone, the token of our common struggle,
our enduring aspiration ?
* • • •
The architect of both St. Patrick’s and St.
Mary’s Cathedral is William Wilkinson Wardell, a
W. W. WARD ELL.
Photo., J. Habert New[?].
follower of that marvellous Pugin who, early in
this century, led what is known as the Gothic re-
vival in architecture. When Mr. Wardell came
to Australia forty - one years ago he was in
the heyday of his talent, a member of the
Civil Engineers’ Institute, an associate of the
Institute of Architects. At that time his life
was despaired of by his doctor. He has lived to
watch the building of the Melbourne .cathedral
through forty years to its opening in 1897 ; and
still, in his 76th year, he may be found daily
superintending the progress of the Sydney
cathedral. To few men is it given to attempt
such a life-work, and so to watch it grow towards
consummation.
Of the two edifices, St. Patrick’s is perhaps the
grander ; St. Mary’s the more magnificent. Both
are conceived in the decorated Gothic style ; but
the adornment of the Sydney structure, built of
Pyrmont freestone, is much richer and more com-
prehensive than in St. Patrick’s, where bluestone
is the main material. To this bluestone is due the
somewhat sombre effect of the Melbourne build-
ing, which will persist at least until the towers
are finished. These will add .£50,000 to the
£230,000 already expended ;an 1 tin; completion
of the architect’s design is of nee <-;;y postponed.
The building of St. Patrick’s is an unbroken
record of struggle by the R.C. clergy and their
adherents in Melbourne. During 40 years, though
the work often lagged, it never stopped entirely.
If prayers could erect a cathedral, St. Patrick’s
would have been built by one man’s prayers alone
—Dr. Fitzpatrick’s. The life of this worthy priest
was consecrated to the work, and in spirit he raised
every stone of the walls. He haunted the building
like its ghost, Day after day, after year, he
watched the edifice rising; and often the workmen
were startled to see his slight, bent figure, with the
cassock gathered round it, crossing from aisle to
aisle on the f«ot-wide windy walls, 70ft. above the
ground.
Every pound he could give, every pound he
could stimulate his clerical brethren to give, Dr.
Fitzpatrick devoted to the cathedral; and when
he died they found half-a-crown in his pocket—all
his worldly possessions. When funds grew low,
the priest exhorted his generous congregation to
fresh efforts —to further self-denial. “ ’Tis God’s
own building ; ye would never see it stopped ! ”
And from the poor Cath dies of Melbourne—from
Bridget the servant and Michael the laborer—
flowed the main stream of contributions which
have built the cathedral.
In Sydney, for St. Mary’s, members of Protes-
tant religions have helped much ; in Melbourne,
they helped little. St. Mary’s Cathedral dates
from the fire which reduced the previous building
to ashes in 1865. Upwards of £ 150,000 has been
spent to bring the Cathedral to its present stage ;
and some £100,009 is required to extend the nave
to its full length and add the entrance and twin
towers which are the imposing key of the struc-
ture.
The plans, to the last line, are complete in the
drawers of Mr. Wardell’s Sydney office. Syd-
ney : for after holding for 20 years the post of
head of the Victorian Public Works Department,
and executing, among other tasks, the Williams-
town Dock, Mr. Wardell was dismissed with the
County Court Judges, with the heads of other de-
partments—“with every man who wore a clean
shirt ” —on Graham Berry’s famous Black Wednes-
day ; and recommenced private practice in New
South Wales, where he could watch the building
of St Mary’s.
* * • •
That William Wardell can live to see the utmost
completion of the vast fabrics woven by many
hands, but spun from his single brain, is beyond
hopes. Sufficient that the splendid design is so
far attained that the designer’s merit is manifest.
Exe/ji rmnumentum. For generation upon gener-
ation the memory of him will be perpetuated by
praying hands, by yearning spires, by the eter-
nally pointing and supplicating hands and spires.
A.G.S.
The Fortieth Anniversary of the
Equitable.
We have received from the Equitable Life Assur-
ance Society a copy of the Fortieth Anniversary
Number of the “ Equitable Record.” The num-
ber is practically devoted to a retrospect of the
Society’s history. The following are some extracts
of interest:—
“In reviewing the history of the Equitable we
instinctively turn to the one central figure with
which the name of the Society will for ever be
inseparably associated. Such was the personal
influence and magnetism of the late Henry B.
Hyde, founder of the Equitable, that even as a
young man of 25 years of age he succeeded in
securing the assistance of a number of prominent
men in the work of establishing the new Society,
and before the organisation of the Equitable was
completed, applications for £86,600 of assurance
had already been obtained.
“ In July, 1859, the incorporators of the Equit-
able adopted a charter providing for the organisa-
tion of the Society on the Mutual plan, under
which the business was to be conducted solely in
the interests of policy-holders, and under which
all profits were to belong to and be distributed
among the assured.
“At the end of 1859 there were but 268 policies
m force, assuring the modest sum of £238,333.
“In November, 1867, a branch was opened in
Great Britain, and thus, over thirty-one years ago,
the Equitable forged a link in the mighty chain
which now binds the two great branches of the
Anglo-Saxon race so closely together.
“ At the end of 1868 the assurance in force aggre-
gated £23,449,628.
“ At this point the Equitable took a step which
has revolutionised Life Assurance throughout the
world by the introduction of the Tontine or De-
ferred Bonus System, under which the largest
benefits are reaped by those policy-holders who
contribute the most towards the common fund.
Under the old plan of assurance, large profits were
realised by the representatives of those policy-
holders who died early, but who had paid com-
paratively little; whilst the longer-lived paid
more and received less in proportion to their con-
tributions ; also many persons for trifling reasons
allo wed their policies to lapse, whereas the addi-
tional inducements offered under this new system
to those who continue, effectually secure to the
widows and estates of many assurers benefits
which were often lost to them under the old
system.
“ The remarkable impetus which has been given
to the business of the Society since the introduc-
tion of this new form of assurance, and also to the
business of many other Life Insurance Companies
which have, under one name or another adopted
imitations of it, is to be attributed in great
measure to the exceptionally favorable returns to
policy-holders under policies maturing in this
class, and the introduction of this popular and
advantageous form of assurance is an eloquent
testimony to the sagacity and foresight of those
who controlled the destinies of the Equitable in
its early days.
“No one factor has contributed more to the
success of the Equitable than its Surplus. This
Surplus, which is accumulated for the benefit and
security of policy-holders under the wise provi-
sions of the Deferred Bonus System, stands as a
mighty rampart of safety, and places the Society
for ever beyond the reach of the disastrous effects
of financial depressions and fluctuations in values.
“In 1884 the Society opened branches in Aus-
tralasia. Year by year its extensive organisation
has been built up, offering to the people of these
colonies all the advantages of a local office, and, in
addition thereto, the security which is guaranteed
by the immense surplus funds of the Society, and
by the world-wide sphere of its investments and
immunity from the danger of local disturbances
arising from epidemics or financial crises, the risks
of the Equitable being distributed all over the
world.
“ Some idea of the magnitude of the Society’s
operations in Australia may be gathered from the
fact that the assurance in force upon its books in
Australia amounts to nearly £4,003,000, and since
the inauguration of the business of the Society in
Australia, fifteen years ago, the sum of over
£600,000 has been paid to policy-holders or their
representatives.
“The Equitable throughout its forty years’
history has been constant in its endeavors to give
its members the most liberal and flexible contract
consistent with approved ideas of safety. Its
policy has been one of enlightened conservatism.
No rash steps have been taken, and no retrogres-
sions have been made, but ever, as experience has
shown where concessions might be made with
safety, these concessions have been introduced
into the contracts.
“What is the present position of the Equitable ?
Its army of policy-holders the world over out-
numbers 250,000; its policies are written in
upwards of a dozen languages ; and its assurance
in force amounts to over £205,000,000. In the
past forty years it has returned to policy-holders,
or their representatives, over £03,000,000 and now
holds over £51,000,000 of Assets, of which
£12,000,000 is Surplus.”
Its Board of Directors in the various colonies
are composed of prominent and able men. The
Sydney Board of Directors consists of Mr.
William McMillan (Chairman), the Hon. Henry
Mort, M.L.C., and the Hon. Charles K. Mackellar,
M. 8., M.L.C. In the absence of Mr. McMillan,
who is in England, the Hon. Charles K.
Mackellar, who is also Medical Director for the
Society in Australasia, acts as Chairman, while
Mr, J. P. McArthur fills the vacancy on the
Board caused by Mr. McMillan's absence.
Mr. C. Carlisle Taylor, the General Manager
for the Society in Australasia, became associated
with the Equitable in 1889, and occupied for many
years a position on the executive staff at the
Head Office in New York. Although at the head
of an American Institution, he is an Englishman,
his father being a retired army officer, and his
grandparents General Henry G. A. Taylor,
G.C.B , and the Ven. Archdeacon Robert Bentley
Buckle, of Dorsetshire, having served with dis-
tinction in the Army and the Church.
The confidence of the Equitable in the Austra-
lian people and in the future of this country has
been marked by the acquisition of valuable
properties and the erection of magnificent build-
ings in Sydney and Melbourne..
Truly it would appear that in no vain-glorious
spirit, but with a prophetic consciousness that
they were laying broad and deep the foundations
of a mighty edifice, the founders of the Equitable
adopted the proud motto —“Not for a Day, but
for Ail Time.”

“Dear Sir, —Your medicine has cured me of bronchitis
and asthma, from which I suffered for upwards of seven
years, during which period I was scarcely ever free from
coughs, and frequently the difficulty of breathing was so
distressing that for nights in succession I had to sit up.
I write you this acknowledgment from a sense of duty, as
in my ease every other treatment had failed. For a year
previous I had been getting very much worse, and at the
time I obtained your medicine I was confined to bed
suffering from a most violent cough, expectorating blood
and matter, and apparently beyond hope of recovery.
The first dose of the medicine gave me welcome relief,
and I steadily improved as I continued the treatment,
until I became, as I am now, quite well.—Yours sincerely,
H. WALKER, Balmain, Sydney.’

S. Australia, which is very well provided
with philanthropic institutions, has one for the
training of weak-minded children—the only one,
so far as writer knows, in Australia. It is the
late George Ash’s best legacy to a province for
which he promised in his short life to do many
services.- There has now been a year’s practical
work at the home, and the results are altogether
cheering. Two woman experts were imported for
the work—largely at “ Boulder ” Brookman’s
expense.
Encounter Bay (S.A.) election, which
ended in O’Malley’s second defeat by Tucker,
was the liveliest in S.A. for half-a-century. At
one polling-place an O’Malleyitemade a derogatory
remark about the rival candidate, and was ducked
in a horse-trough. Within half-an-hour there
was a free fight, worthy of Dublin’s best days,
the crowning feat being accomplished when the
little village parson took the publican and nearly
drowned him in the ocean. At the last election,
a turbulent mob of O’Malleyites searched for a
high municipal functionary two whole days, and it
subsequently came out that that gentleman had
been hiding—in a most unexpected place.
The Prince of Wales makes himself
“ affable ” enough in public up to the point where
he is paid to do it, and in strict privacy he pro-
bably takes care to avoid all persons and things
likely to ruffle his serenity. But woe to the in-
dividual who asks to be noticed in public by his
Royal Highness when the stout personage has
something else to think about ! _ A worried
“Wales ”is the most merciless of princes. Said
that a lady with whom he had once taken tea,
“ took the liberty ’’ of wearing a frock of purple
and gold (his racing colors) in the royal enclosure
at Ascot. Furthermore, she took the liberty of
curtseying in front of him as he came walking
along with another lady. Result, “Wales” told
somebody to say something to the Master of the
Buckhounds (master of the ceremonies at Ascot)
and the official instructed an underling to inform
the purple-and-gold lady that her enclosure ticket
was—haw !—cancelled. To be snubbed by the
Heir Apparent is to be practically chucked
out, that he may not have the annoyance of
again meeting the individual he has chosen to
humiliate. And a grovelling press records such
society incidents as the above without any ap-
parent sense of the hero’s meanness.

This valuable paper rises to acknowledge
receipt of Wright’s Australian, India, China and
Japan Trade Directory and Gazetteer ; including
Canada, South and Central America, Mexico,
West Indies and South Africa. It is a vast hand-
book of the trades, professions, commerce and
manufactures in the above-mentioned countries.
It is a most useful work, for should a man (or a
woman) meditate a trip round the world he can,
in a few minutes, by reference to this work, dis-
cover exactly where to go and be able to get beer
all the time, also how many soda-water makers
there are in Barberton, in the Transvaal, and the
name of the chemist and druggist in Chilpancingo
de los Bravos, in Mexico. When meditating a
visit to Yokohama, in Japan, the possessor of this
work need not refer to Waek Jaunt to find out
that boots and shoes are sold by Carl Dietrich,
and that the Japan Brewery Co. Ltd., hang out
at 123 Bluff. The work can be obtained in Sydney
from Messrs. T. Cook and Sons, 4 Hunter-street.*
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*

As evidence of how bitterly prejudiced against
boxing some people are, the following slab from Mr.
O’Conor’s speech in N.S.W. Leg. Assembly debate re
police-interference with the Dunn-Conlon boxing-match,
as reported in Hansard, would be amusing if it were not
so utterly ridiculous. Referring to size of gloves “We
know very well how the Boz. are made up. The law
makes no provision as to how the Boz. is to be made up—
whether it is to be made up by the covering of the glove,
or by a leaden band round the wrist. That is where the
Boz. come in, and that is where the 4oz. come in. The
4oz. is nothing more than a piece of kid just over the
knuckles, with a weight put in to make it 4oz. It is
merely a subterfuge, in order to introduce the degrading
sy'stem of prize-fighting.” Surely Mr. O’Conor has got
mixed with the iron cestus of the ancients I

The fight on Saturday night between “Friday ”
O’Neill and “Tot” Higgins, two of the best 10-stoners in
Australia, proved a fiasco owing to O’Neill damaging his
arm in the first round. The loser went out favorite at 6
to 4, and in the half-dozen heavy exchanges of the first
2min. seemed to have the advantage. Then Higgins put
in his humble negative by rushing “Friday" and up-
setting him on his elbow, with the result that one of the
small bones of his arm snapped.

Sydney “ Rocks’ ” last development in the pug.
line, “ Cocker ” Tweedie, has been “up against” it once
more in Yankeedom. At Stockton (Cal.), July 3, he was
doing the butterfly act with black Rufe Turner, and
piling up a heap of points ; but while playing to the
gallery during the eighth round “ Cocker ” stopped a
beauty on the point, and the show was over. On his first
trip to the land of the wooden ham, a precisely similar
occurrence happened, and (if memory serves) in the
eighth round, too. The erstwhile Sydney newsboy will no
doubt be home again shortly as they’ve got no use for
beaten pugs, in the States—particularly foreigners.
• • * •
Erstwhile Australian champion middleweight
pug., sturdy Dan Creedon, and the “ Barbadoes Terror”
(gorilla-like Joe Walcott) fought their third match June
23, at New York. The black won on points after 20
tough rounds. From the sixth Walcott bashed Dan all
over the ring. In the fifteenth the Australian rallied
marvellously, but was too weak to do harm. Creedon
showed better in this than in either of the other fights.
* * * *
Latest re whilom Maitland (N.S.W.) blacksmith
Paddy Slavin is that he reaches London shortly, the
owner of several fine mining properties and with a pile of
Klondyke gold in his kit; not bad after being killed and
resurrected at least half-a-dozen times. Report first had
him buried in the snow of Chilcoot Pass ; then he went
over Yukon Falls in a canoe. Later a drunken miner
drove 12in. cold steel through the Australian’s heart;
then typhoid and other complaints got in their deadly
work. Most of these rumors were so tinged with
genuineness that obituary notices appeared all over the
world. Writing thus calls to mind Slavin’s memorable
10-rounds fight with Peter Jackson in London ; also his
go at Bruges, when Brum, toughs mobbed him and
saved clumsy', muscle-bound Jem Smith ffom slaughter.
Must not be forgotten either that Paddy virtually shook
his fist in Sullivan’s face when John L. was John L. and
then the “Boston Boy” wouldn’t come.
* • * «
The English Diamond Sculls —amounting to the
amateur championship of England—was won for the
second time by American Howells. The Yank is at
Cambridge, and chipped in when the English amateurs
took the mean point of declining the entry of Ten
Eyck, son of the old pro., which they had power to do
without explanation according to Henley' rules. All
real sportsmen must rejoice at the success of the
American.
* ♦ * *
N.S.W. League of Wheelmen promises some-
thing more exciting than record-breaking at its big meet-
ing in Oct. £3OO is to be paid in prizes, and “ pursuit
races ” are to be the novelty. The eight best scratch men
are picked out, and started in heats of four riders each,
the men being started at equal distances from each other.
To prevent loafing, when a man is passed he has to fall
out of the race, and the prize goes to the man doing
fastest time.
• + * *
Cabled last week that arrangements were being
made for an Australian bowling-team to visit the old
country, and that two Australians had already promised
to plank down a cool “ thou.” towards exes. Reps, from
this section (if the best be available, which is doubtful)
should keep their end up all right, but the money pro-
mised must be multiplied by four or five.
* • * *
Bowler Jones’ impressions of London:—“lt’s
all right; I can stand it all the time. Seen the sights?
My' word, I’ve seen everything! No, I’ve not seen
Westminster Abbey or St. Paul’s, but the new American
bar at the Alhambra’s a daisy. Leno’s not so funny as he
was, hut the girls are just as beautiful as ever. Give me
London, and you can have the rest.”
• • * *
A well-behaved crowd, the present Australian
XI., judging from the fact that no accounts to the con-
trary have so far been received, officially or privately.
But how the Britishers must miss the eccentric joker of
a previous XI. who, on a cold day, when fielding near the
boundary, by degrees collected quite a respectable pile
of sticks, and, setting fire to them, squatted down like a
blackfellow for a warm. On another occasion, at a big
dinner, when three cheers were called for somebody or
other, he seized a leg of mutton by the knuckle and,
waving it round his head, sent a shower of caper-sauce
over everybody in his vicinity.
* « • *
Hayward, the Surrey pro., has proved himself
the Australians’ most formidable opponent. It is pro-
bably because he is a professional that he is not so much
advertised as Fry, Ranji, Jackson, and M'Laren, for he
is evidently more to be relied upon than any' of the four
cracks. His scores against the visitors have been—so,
24, 16, 0 (run out), 28,1, 77, 40 (not out), 130, 137; total
499 ; average, 50.
* * * •
In the five tests, Australia made 2475 runs for
80 wickets, average 30'93, and England made 2075 for 69
wickets, average 30‘07, so that there is nothing between
them. As showing how England was on her mettle, how-
ever, no less than 24 players played against Australia’s
13 (Johns didn’t play). Highest aggregate of runs in the
five matches was made by Hayward—4l3 as against
Noble’s 367. Hayward’s average of 68, moreover, beats
Hill’s 60. Jones walks over all the bowlers with his 26
wickets. Where Australia beats England is in all-round
men, Noble being a successful bowder, and Trumble the
same—the latter is perhaps the best all-round cricketer
in the world just now. Of England’s champion batsmen,
neither Hayward, Jackson, Ranji, M'Laren, or Fry are
bowlers worth considering.
• * * •
This year’s test-matches in England clearly
show that the Britishers have never filled Richardson’s
place. As in Australia, England fails in her bowling.
Rhodes and Hearne have each secured 13 wickets, the
highest number against Darling and co. Jones, for Aus-
tralia, captured 26, and Trumble 15. What chance will
England have on Australian wickets with her bowling
bnttery? On our billiard-table wickets Hearne could
i (ict'lung \ ar, and he is not likely to improve as
ti ' As for Rhodes and Young, they have
been ortiv •-*" performers on their own dunghill.
