“Wal.”: Re the idea of sending ’Ome an Aus-
tralian model to meet the Maid of Kent. No use. Mr.
Holmes, who sailed the Irex in one of her races against
the Maid, told me that the tide ran like a mill-race, and
no boat with a grip of the water like our Port Jackson
22-footer would have a show against a skimming-dish of
the Maid order.
* • * *
Ikey Mo hasn’t it all his own way. At a recent
Sydney pony-meet a punter laid odds-on £lOO to £5O, and
went down. When settling arrived, he declined to pay
because, as he put it, Ikey had the pony in the bag.
Stated before Q. Police Commission that
the perpetrators of 21 murders in the last five
years have never been traced I The Land of
Mysterious Assassination!
Seven witnesses wept while giving evidence
in the Norton-Smith criminal libel case ; and five
jurors, four of them apparently as hard as nails,
sobbed at one or other part of the proceedings.
“ The Sydney Bulletin is a cow of a
paper.”— Powerful criticism by the anti-Federal
Brisbane Street.
The action of Portugal (no doubt incited
by Britain) in stopping the transport of war-
material for the Transvaal through the Delagoa
Bay territory looks like the crisis of the Boer
trouble, and prepares one for news of war at any
moment.
Hornsby, where a sewing-machine co.’s
drummer was alleged to have been stuck-up and
robbed by an armed bushranger, the other day,
was originally a grant of land made by Govt, to
one Horn for his prowess in capturing a bush-
ranging desperado of about 40 years ago.
A Melb. militiaman was tried and acquitted
at recent Criminal Sessions for an alleged offence
against a girl. On the day of the illegal act
the girl was 15 years 11 months and 28 days old—
three days only short of the time when the law
allows a woman to become her own mistress.
Those 72 hours were responsible for all the trouble :
had prosecutrix been born in a 28-day month
instead of a 31-day one no trial could have taken
place.
In view of the marriage of Lily Langtry
(aged 47) with Hugo de Bathe (28) son of Sir
Henry de Bathe, baronet, the motto of the
groom’s family is peculiarly interesting. It is
“Nec parvis sisto ” —“Trifles deter me not.” A
man would have to be regardless of many trifles
in marrying the woman who slipped the ice down
Tummy Wales’ back, and was such a dear friend
of Abingdon Baird.
Victoria’s House of 48 Rich Landlords
passed the Bill for the abolition of plural voting at
Assembly elections by 30 to 8. It was decided
that after agreeing to have one-man-one-vote for
both Federal Houses, even the most bigoted 48
Rich Landlords couldn’t pretend to disbelieve in
the principle any longer. Those Queensland anti-
Federalists who yell to the last bitter yelp that
there is no One Man One Vote in the Federal
Constitution might make a note of this episode.
Premier Kingston dished the Tories in
S. A. Assembly last week. He desired to have the
province voting as one district for the Federal
House of Representatives, but the Opposition
sought to split it up into smaller electorates.
Whereupon Kingston, seeing that the amendment
was likely to be carried, accepted the Labor-
party’s suggestion to make each district exactly
equal in respect to population. Now the Conser-
vatives are anxious to get back to the original
scheme again.
The omission of a sentence from the
article “ More Snake ” in last Bulletin makes
one par. unintelligible. The guilty item should
have read—
“ Simple Poser” triumphantly points out that his
hymns and his Scriptures are so colorless as to mean
nothing in particular. That is to say, they are husks.
[And he wonders why on earth the Catholics cannot be
satisfied with husks.] The reason is eminently creditable
to the Catholics, &c.
A Ballarat (Vic.) husband has had his wife
“bound over in the sum of £lO to keep the peace
towards him.” He said he had only seen the lady
twice before marriage ; and the second day after
marriage his eyes were opened to the mistake he
had made—at least one eye was opened, while the
other was blackened and much swollen. The lady
also frequently threatened to poison him, and to
burn down the house, and she threw axes at him.
The bench found the charges proved, in which
case the old man’s safety might have been secured
by binding the lady to a reliable gum-tree. Bind-
ing her over in the sum of .£lO looks an absurdly
weak precaution.
Sydney daily-paper item of last week :
Narrabri, Tuesday.—lntimation was received to-day
that the balloting for 13 settlement leases on Drilldool,
comprising 30,000 acres, announced for September 14, has
again been postponed. Intending lessees feel they are
being hoaxed about these lands, this being the seventh
postponement. The first was announced for December
last, postponed to March, again to April, twice postponed
in April, then announced for June, when the postpone-
ment was published so late that men came hundreds of
miles. There is considerable feeling on the matter, and
some talk of calling a public indignation meeting.
And this is how the Reid-Carruthers Government
“ puts people on the land ! ”
Asked, by London Central News Agency,
whether there would be any criticism of the
Queensland Government’s offer of troops for
South Africa, in the event of war, Agent-General
Tozer said :
Yes. We have 33 per cent, of Irish. We have their
opposition to contend with, as is the case here ; and then
we have Republicans, who, of course, will criticise a step
which tends to the strengthening of the Empire. But
the bulk of the people will be heartily in accord with the
loyal offer. By the way, two of my sons are in these
Queensland troops I’ve been telling you of, and I shouldn’t
be surprised if they are in the contingent which, if the
necessity arose, would fight in South Africa.
An interesting little case has just con-
cluded in Brisbane. A well-known young citizen,
a prominent athlete, was arrested by a constable
rather late at night—or (to speak more correctly)
rather early in the morning. Aforesaid citizen
was more or less “under the influence,” and
lunged about somewhat. Consequence was some-
thing happened, and the citizen lost an eye.
Policeman avers the injured man fell somewhere
and hurt himself, but a friend of the athlete
deposed something very different. A dubious
halting jury discharged the constable, and the un
fortunate citizen goes about minus an eye. And
Brisbane folks are talking queer things about the
“ force " and its methods.
The awful Victorian J.P. on the caper
again ! A cabby, charged at Prahran with
assaulting his fares, started to prove a previous
assault by the Toorak larrikins who had engaged
him, and pleaded self-defence. Solicitor for cabby
said, in opening, that when prosecutors were
asked for their fare they “ dealt it out ” to de-
fendant, knocked him down and kicked him.
Whereupon the amateur J.P. chairman—“No,
they did not kick him.” Defending solicitor: “ I
am going to call a gentleman who will swear that
they did.” Chairman: “Well, I would not
believe him ! ” After which awful exhibition of
impartiality the poor cabby was lucky to get off
with a 20s. fine and costs.
More glory for the Kalgoorlie millionaires.
In chronicling a ball given at the local Palace
Hotel on the eve of the departure to England of
Messrs. Kaufman and C. D. Rose, Kalgoorlie
Miner says:
Mr. Kaufman intends to return to the field in April
and will then spend about three months in Kalgoorlie.
It is possible Mr. Rose will again accompany him, but he
is a very busy man, and is one of the best known finan-
ciers in England. In addition he is a big horse-owner
and breeder, a keen yachtsman, being a member of the
Royal Yacht Club and the Turf Club, and is well known
in club land, being a member of the Carlton, one of the
most exclusive clubs in London. He won the Ascot Gold
Cup just before leaving for Australia, and among those
who saw him off on the occasion of his departure for
Kalgoorlie was the Prince of Wales, with whom he is on
terms of intimacy.
In the memorable words of Kaufman, “Don’t
you see me vink! ”
“ What good can there be in a movement
led by boodlers ? ” queries a prominent Q. anti-
Federal M.L.A. This is probably a slap at the
Anti-National Party, headed by MTlwraith, Bar-
low, Unmack, and Nelson, though the querist
doesn’t seem to have intended it so.
An incident in connection with the wire-
less-telegraph experiments in Adelaide showed up
the weakness of the system so far as it has got.
Todd, chief of the telegraphs, and Prof. Bragg
are making the experiments, one station being at
the Observatory, the other at Henley Beach, six
miles away. At the G.P.0., which is farther
back from beach than the Observatory is, an
electrician, working at a receiver, switched on to
the Henley Beach telephone wire to test his
machine; and immediately he received part of
a wireless message which was being sent from the
Beach to Todd at the Observatory.
A Perth (W.A.) city councillor was lately
informed that some dead dogs had been on the
premises of the local museum for months, and was
implored to castigate the rubbish department in
the local council for not abating the nuisance—
which he duly did. Whereupon the curator of
the museum wrote to the daily press to state that
the only dead dogs on the premises were the
stuffed dingoes in the cases, which had been there
over four months, and that the committee in-
tended to retain them.
Brisbane Telegraph, which has been
preaching up north that N.S.W. dare not federate
without Queensland, therefore there is no need for
Queensland to keep step, may now consider itself a
false prophet and a bowled-out seer. For N.S.W.
has just done it. The last act of the drama was the
adoption of the address to the Queen, which com-
mits the province for good to the national cause,
and the document struggled through the Nominee
Chamber last week by three votes.

Dear Sir, —
If you intend buying a new Binder this year, we ask you to

The material used in the McCormick machine is worth more
money than in any other machine we know of. The workmanship
on the McCormick is superior to any other. In fact, the M‘Cormick
is honestly built throughout.
We would call your attention to one little feature of the Right
Hand Binder that gained the machine many friends the past year.
That is the Spring Roller Twine Tension. You will like it. The
twine runs between rollers and cannot wear a groove and get loose.
It will hold a thread or a clothes-line, or the two mixed, at any
tension you want. Tie a knot in the twine and see how nicely it
slips through the tension. In other kinds of tension it is almost
sure to break the twine. When “time is money,” as it is in the
harvest-field, it is a great satisfaction to have a tension in which the
twine never catches, never breaks, never bunches up, never snarls,
which never causes loss of time or temper.
There are many other features you will notice that are found
only on the McCormick, such as the platform apron tightener—the
only one worthy the name—-and the hinged elevator apron rollers,
which make it easy to take off and put on the elevator aprons.
The steel bottom platform, the long movement of the binder
back and forth, the great reach of the reel forward and backward
and up and down, and the ease of handling it, being helped by the
strong lifting spring, are all winning points which help to make it a
pleasure to run the McCormick.
Trusting that we may hear later of your having placed your
order for a McCormick Machine,

Neighbor Wolff and Others,
Almost every afternoon in pleasant weather I see
my neighbor Wolff sitting in an easy-chair in the
shade in front of his own door. Sometimes he
kills time by reading the paper, but oftener he
does nothing but watch the people and the vehicles
that pass. I frequently stop and talk with him a
few minutes, and he appears to be grateful for the
i trifling attention. He is a man of about fifty,
strongly and sturdily built, and to look at him you
see no reason why he should not be up and about
as actively as the best of us. He is intelligent and
well-informed, has travelled considerably, and is a
man in every way worth knowing. His ideas on
finance and business seem to be clear and practi-
cal, and it strikes a stranger as odd he does not
grapple with his opportunities and pile up a big
fortune. Yet he never makes a motion in that
direction. He lets the chances all slip by, and
just sits there as though the world were no more
to him than the Klondyke goldfields are to the
resident population of a cemetery. Yet my neigh-
boui Wolff knows what money is, and appreciates
what can be done with it. Then why doesn’t he
“get a move on” as the Yankees say, and make
hay while the sun shines ? Take a peep at him
about nine o’clock at night, when he takes his cus-
tomary exercise on the pavement —the way being
mostly clear of pedestrians by then—and you will
need no verbal answer to the question. Stocky and
strong as he appears when in his chair, he makes a
poor show on his legs. Slowly, heavily, leaning
hard on his thick stick, he goes to and fro a few
times between the corner and his house —a dis-
tance of 200 feet may be—and then with a sigh
sinks again into his chair like a man who realises
he is out of the race. A paralytic stroke two
years ago, and the constant fear of another —that
explains it. He is one of the multitude who are
in the world and not of it—as this woman was a
while since:—
“From my girlhood,” she says, “I way always
oppressed and hindered by weakness ; I was
habitually tired, weary, and languid. Like all
such persons, I lived a kind of life to myself. All
pleasures or pursuits which called for energy and
vitality were closed to me. I had a feeble and
fitful appetite, and what I ate gave me great pain
at the chest and back, and a sense of coldness,
deadness, and weight at the stomach. At times
there would be also a gnawing, sinking feeling.
“As I grew older I was much troubled with
palpitation of the heart and loss of sleep. Year
after year I dragged along in this miserable way,
and, being too weak to walk, was drawn from
place to place in a bath chair. Every effort was
made to obtain a cure. I consulted doctor after
doctor, and took many kinds of medicine and
treatment, but got no more than temporary relief.
One doctor said my heart was diseased.
“My sister urged me to try Mother Seigel’s
Syrup. Without any great hopes of its effect
in my case, I nevertheless procured a bottle
from the Stores in High-street, and began to
take it as directed. The result was a decidedly
improved appetite, and I relished and digested
what I ate without any pain or distress following.
As I continued to use this medicine the heart
trouble was less frequent and finally ceased alto-
gether. My nerves soon became stronger, and I
could sleep well. In a few weeks more I was
belter and stronger than ever before in my life , and
have enjoyed good health ever since. My friends
were almost as much surprised and pleased as I
was at this remarkable result of taking Mother
Seigel’s Syrup, and you may make my statement
public if you think it will do good.”—(Signed)
(Mrs.) Mary Ann Welch, 1 Constitution Hill,
Luton, Chatham, February 6, 1895.
My neighbor Wolff may or may not recover
from his paralysis, but all cases like*Mrs. Welch’s
—indigestion, with consequent weakness and ner-
vous debility—should be curable by the remedy
which cured her. It opens and renews the diges-
tion, which is the fountain of life, and puts a
wholly new aspect on the world around us. From
having been laid aside as cumberers of the ground
we become once more workers with others and
sharers of their pleasures.

The Case of
Mrs. ELIZABETH BOLDRA.
(From the “ Newtown Independent .”)
Our reporter received instructions to call
upon Mrs. Elizabeth Boldra, of 61 Prospect-
street, Erskineville, Sydney, to obtain from
her a relation of the facts of the severe
indisposition from which she lately suffered.
That lady invited the newspaper man into
her parlor, and willingly answered the
queries put to her.
“ I was quite a martyr to neuralgia,” she
explained, ‘‘ I never thought I should ever
get rid of it. Sometimes the agony was so
unendurable that I felt I could have
knocked my head against the wall.”
“ What were the pains like ? ”
** Acute darting pains, which would flash
round my head and face and all over my
jaws like lightning. In addition to this I
often had a dull, wearing headache.”
“ I suppose you obtained but little rest.”
“ Indeed, that is so. The night was
worse than the day. I couldn’t stop in bed,
and whole nights together the neuralgia
kept me out of bed pacing the room. It
was dreadful, and I hardly know how I
pulled through it.”
“ Why, how long were you ill ? ”
“ Four months—four months with hardly
an hour free from pain. When I obtained a
little momentary relief I was frightened to
move for fear of starting those horrible
tortures again. Why, any little thing would
start the neuralgia, even so trifling a matter
as a cup of tea.”
“ Did you get weak ? ”
“ Yes, very. My nervous system was
upset, and I was regularly out of sorts all
over. My jaws were so stiff with the in-
cessant pain that I could eat only with
great difficulty; besides, my appetite was
wretched. I didn’t trouble about eating ;
my whole thoughts were centred on getting
rid of the complaint making me weaker and
weaker every day, and was really torturing
me to death. My face was drawn and
pinched, and I lost my generally healthy
appearance altogether.”
“ No wonder, Mrs. Boldra."
“Yes, four months of such suffering as I
passed through leaves its mark on one, can
tell you ~ was not half the woman lam
now.”
“ W effect had the usual remedies on
your complaint ? ”
“None —none at all. At last I came to
believe that I was beyond the reach of
medicines. At last I got relief, which came
about in this manner. Someone said to me,

you would only try it ? ’ ‘ Oh, nonsense !’ I
replied ; but 1 thought over what I had been
told, and bought a bottle, not that I expected

from the hundred and one other things I
had tested.” >,
“In this case, however, you got relief.”
“ I got more—more than mere temporary

and after about a dozen more doses the pain

began to come back. I had a healthy
appetite again. Clements Tonic made me

and I gradually got rid of that low, nervous
feeling which had been the result of my long

often think when I hear of anyone suffering

“I hope so.”

gnawing one/’ explained Mr. Frank Russell,

“ But 1 suppose you couldn’t eat much ?”

Every day when I went to work 1 took
some lunch with me, which I was forced to
eat at about eleven o’clock, otherwise the
peculiar gnawing and sinking pain in my
stomach became ten times worse. A little
food somehow seemed to give a temporary
stay to this pain.”
‘‘ Can you describe the pain ? ”

perhaps, that I may say that when suffering

cease work for an hour till the severest of
the pangs had worn off. And as for my
mouth, the bitter taste in the morning was
more than I could stand, and I frequently
jumped out of bed, obtained a lump of
sugar, and chewed it up ; this, I found, was
a temporary relief.”

that I might come across someone amongst
the many that could give me a bit of ease.”
“ Did you get no benefit ? ”
“ No, not at the time. One after another
I was forced to stop the use of the pills and
other medicines, after giving them each a
decent trial. In every instance I found
these much-advertised cures failed to come
up to the mark, and, more than, that, I
believe that by filling myself up with these
things 1 only upset my system, and made

cumstances I gave up taking physic
altogether.”

COUNTRY TRIAL ORDER RESPECTFULLY SOLICITED.
Granny Pickles.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The rain poured softly on the bark roof of alonely
hut: inside an old woman sat in front of a large
fire. She was as still as though dead; for her
mind was far away, on the Ballarat of ’54.
* * • •
It was night-time. From a canvas gambling-
saloon, not two hundred yards away, the music of
a fiddle floated out of the thudding, shuffling
sound of dancing. All round her was the mur-
muring of many voices, with a word standing out
here and there, meaning nothing to her. Sud-
denly, from the confusion of many sounds, rose
startlingly a shriek of agony. The whole field
heard it, too ; for a sudden silence followed.
Then came the sound of hurrying feet, and a low
muttering, which gradually swelled into a sea of
angry sound that surged from end to end of the
camp. The woman had risen, and was listening,
when she saw a black head, followed by broad
shoulders, appear under the side of the tent. Then
a scared-looking man was gaping at her, 'and
looking each moment more scared as he gaped.
“ What is it, Banks?” she asked, recognising an
acquaintance. “Who yelled like that? What
have you done ? ”
“Killed Joe Darrell!” he replied, curtly, and
looking slightly relieved. “A row over cards.
He cheated, an’ I drew my knife on him ; he slung
his at me but missed, then I stabbed him. The
fellows don’t quite know how it happened yet,
and I want to hide till the fuss blows over.”
The pattering of running feet was heard coming
rapidly nearer. “Quick! can you hide me?”
Banks looked scared again. The woman nodded
towards the low camp-stretcher of saplings, and
the man crept under it. Then she put on a night-
cap, and, sticking out her head with the tent-
flaps close under her chin, as if she were not
dressed, was asked by some of the crowd that
went hurrying by—“ Have you seen Geordie
Banks ? ” “ No ! ” she answered, and stood look-
ing after them and listening till she heard Banks
slip out under the canvas again into the darkness.
Presently she heard the measured tramp of men
carrying something heavy. She guessed it was
the murdered man, and waited for them to pass—
but they didn’t pass ! They stopped opposite her
tent; and a man advanced reluctantly. “ Banks
has killed your man, missus ! ” he said in a low
voice. She stared hard at the man—unrealising ;
then lifted the coat from the dead man’s face.
“ My God ! ” she cried. “ He told me it was Joe
Darrell ” —and she fell swooning across her hus-
band’s body.
The miners wondered at her remark; and as
soon as she recovered they asked the meaning of
it. Her explanation divided the camp into two
parties, of which the suspicious one was the
stronger. Banks was never heard of again ; and
thus a by-wash of accident and suspicion had
swung her out of the main current of life, and
stranded her in this lonely gully—broken hearted.
It was forty years ago. But all the details re-
turned to her with the purring of the rain on the
soft roof.
♦ * * *
Distantly, at; first, came the regular splashes of
footsteps on a watery path. The old woman’s
body vivified. She rose and fastened a piece of
rope across the door, then sat down again. The
gate slammed, and steps came up the path ; weak
steps, as of an old man. “ Who’s there?”
“ Only me, Missus. Can you give us a bite an’
shelter for the night ? ”
“ Wait a minute,” she replied, got some bread
and meat, opened the door as far as the rope
would allow, and handed out the food. “ You
can sleep on the bench under the verandah,” she
said, and the unseen man thanked her.
The old woman was out at daybreak, and found
the man still sleeping. She approached him
cautiously. The damp blanket round him steamed
slightly from the warmth of his body. His hair
was snowy white ; there was a hopeless droop in
the corners of his heavy mouth, and deep corru-
gated lines on his forehead. His withered cheek was
flushed, and he slept restlessly, as if conscious of
being looked at; then he awoke, with a startled
cower, as he saw the old woman’s dim eyes staring
into his face. Then he writhed, and pain drove
every other sensation out of him.
“ What is it ? ’’ she asked.
“Rheumatics,” he replied, and writhed again.
The old woman’s face softened a little. She
went inside and made some tea, and brought it to
him. As the day passed, he still lay on the bench,
sometimes writhing, which Granny noted as she
worked.
At midday she asked him if he were any better.
“ Worse,” he replied ; and, after looking at him
for a little, she went inside. She looked at his
shaking, feverish form, then she made up a bed
for him in the kitchen.
For a week she nursed him ; her dim old eyes
taking on a newer and softer expression as she
felt tiie natural woman awaken within her, after
lying dormant for 40 years. At the end of the
week he could hobble round; at the end of
another he took up a hoe, unasked, and helped her
in the garden. In a month he was fit to go, and —
she was sorry !
“Would you like to stay an’ ’elp ’ere?” she
asked.
“Wouldn’t mind,” he replied, looking slowly
round the well-kept half-acre of fruit-trees and
vegetables.
“Then what’s yer name?” she asked. “ I’m
called Granny Pickles.”
“Well,” he said slowly, “I’ve bin called many
names ; but my real name is George Banks.”
The old woman straightened up suddenly.
“My real name is Mrs. Joe Wilson !”
The man collapsed abjectly. “I’m old —too
old to die — that way ! ” he whimpered.
They stared at each other for a full minute.
“ I’ll go! ” the man said at last.
“You’d better go on hoein’,” the woman an-
swered quietly.
And he did.
W. BRENTA.
“ Dareel ” writes to The Bulletin :
“ S. the W.” is misinformed re N.S.W. police firearms.
The Henry rifle superseded the Terry 15 years ago.
Apropos “skin ’’-cartridges, writer lately had lively experi-
ence of their vitality. Game across a “police” Colt’s
revolver (family relic), which had been loaded for
“’rangers” in the 60’s. Removed the caps (green and
perished), soaked the chambers with kerosene, and,
having vainly tried to push the cartridges out by probing
through the nipple, put the cylinder into a bucket of live
coals to melt the bullets, and, to minimise the danger of
a cartridge exploding, placed bucket down the sink. One
did go off, and the concussion jerked the red hot cylinder
out into a narrow passage, where, as with each successive
discharge it aimed in a fresh direction, we had a lively
object-lesson in being under promiscuous fire. Not bad
for old-time powder and skin covers.
The Makutu.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
There were four of us, and a week straight across
country through Maoriland bush, with nothing
but sodden flour for tucker, makes the prospect of
any other life under the sun agreeable. Men
were scarce in those days, and a vessel would
stay weeks over her time to hunt deserters. What
sense of the beautiful had not been routined out
of us was exercised to its full limit in those fairy
hours of morning, when Maoriland has no peer in
the wide world. As the day proceeds, everything
becomes irritatingly monotonous, and before
night the bush traveller aches for an illimitable
extent of burning, sandy desert, with not a tree
in sight, or a barren, tussocky range of black hills.
It is then indeed a relief to look down into the
broad valley and see a large area of clear coun-
try, and in the midst a Maori village situate near
a lake or lagoon.
Hinde had lived amongst the Maoris, and
although he confessed they were infernally lazy
and dirty, yet he thought much of their hospi-
tality. We were elated, as we felt we could do
with a little recruiting. We were not pressed for
time, so as Hinde promised us, in addition to
other attractions, a temporary wife each, we were
bursting to reach that pah.
Hinde was correct in every particular. We got
everything he promised, including the wahines,
and we could have had two each for the asking.
We lay on our backs for a week and smoked
rau-rau until we were weary. Then we started
exploring, and found the valley to be a
veritable kauri-gum eldorado. You could kick
junks of it out of the loose soil anywhere. The
Maoris, in a desultory fashion, were disposing of
the treasure to advantage. A couple of canoes
were occasionally run down the river to a cutter,
owned by a half-caste, who traded between Waite-
mata and the saw-mill settlements along the
coast.
We were aware that under the kindly treat-
ment of our hosts there were lurking suspicions
that our party was not weighed-down with con-
scientious scruples. Hinde impressed upon us
that, if the natives got to believe that we coveted
their wealth, it would mean, at the least, eject-
ment —probably annihilation. One day, circum-
stances and the bright intellect of Hinde showed
us a way.
The natives were in a very excitable state. Eor
some time a series of misfortunes had befallen the
tribe. The Maoris believe in cause where there is
effect, and stimulated their prophets to research.
When one of the Rangitiras of the tribe died
at the early age of something near eighty, a
clamorous demand was made for retribution upon
something or somebody. The prophets had their
reputation to keep, so Kunui was impeached.
Kunui was about the least important member of
the community, and was pretty well a mokai
(slave). He would long ago have been sacrificed
to religious demands had he been of less value as
a hewer of wood and a drawer of water. The
Tohus (priests) decided that Kunui had bewitched
things in general, and that the withering gaze of
his cross eye was blighting all it looked upon.
Kunui was blindfolded and tied to a stake in
the middle of the pah, where his scanty wearing
apparel had been burnt. Then those who felt
so disposer] took torches and scientifically singed-
off any hair that was visible. This was supposed
to cancel his projected villainies.
The poor devil’s howls sickened us, so we went
for a walk. We were getting tired of Maori-
pakeha-ing, and it struck us that if Kunui’s un-
bewitching didn’t mend matters, their holitiesses
might fix upon us as the authors of evil. Hinde
told us that th? scorching was only a preliminary,
and that the makutu would be bound, thrown into
his whare,; and the place set fire-to that night.
It was while conversing over Kunui’s hard fate
that Hinde conceived his brilliant idea. As we
were tired of rest, we would return to the pah and
say good-bye. We would go a short distance
up the valley; and when night fell we would
return and rescue Kunui, unknown to the Maoris.
We would keep him hidden in the bush for a day
or two, to allow his fate to sink into the minds of
these children of nature, and then, all of a sudden,
in broad daylight spring him upon them. They
would assuredly believe him to be the very devil,
and at once leave the place, damned by his
accursed spirit, as far behind them as they could.
Result : an unlimited field for kauri-gum opera-
tions.
We departed. “ Enoho ! ” (Stay). “ Haere ! ”
(I go) were mournfully repeated respectively by
the Maoris and ourselves. Our abandoned wives
stoically endured the parting, and, to our joy,
showed no desire to accompany us.
We camped about two miles up the river, and
that night Hinde and myself cut our way through
the raup'o and toi-toi lining of the whare, and
bore away the condemned Kunui. We had just
got under cover of the bush when we sa>v the
whare in flames, and heard the hideous shoutings
of the tohus, as they danced around it to give the
spirit a parting blast as it winged its way to te
Reinga (hell).
Everything went splendidly. Kunui joyously
entered into the conspiracy, and could not suffi-
ciently express his gratitude to his saviours.
When all was ready, we accompanied Kunui
down to see the fun, and watch the aboriginals
scatter. We saw the 3inged defendant creep into
the pah. The Maoris were lolling about, and the
only activity noticeable was the passing to and
fro of the wahines, as they prepared the eternal
kai. When Kunui jumped up there was tremen-
dous excitement, and he harangued them impres-
sively. To our surprise they didn’t scatter, but
threw themselves prostrate on the ground before
him. After a while they approached Kunui, and
threw wreaths over him, and worshipped him.
Then Hinde advised us to hurry.
We did go, for eight-and-forty hours straight on
end. It was lucky for us the Maoris had no guns,
but they stoned us for ten miles, and pursued us
with more energy than I ever knew them display
before or since. We had one boot and a pair of
alleged trousers when we at length found succour
at a whaling-station. We didn’t know each other
for weeks, till the swelling went down and we
began to resume our natural shape..
That ungrateful devil, Kunui, had represented
himself as a god, and had attributed the vilest
characters and motives to us. He claimed to
know our game and whereabouts by his divine
powers. He seized the opportunity, and became
the chief tohu of the tribe; so it was not long
before he had his revenge on his judges. His
ingratitude towards us was something incredible,
and I will never rescue a Maori again as long as I
live. And Hinde says ditto.

A Song of Failure.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Here is my hand to you, brother,
You of the ruck who have failed ;
I, too, am only another
Fighter who faltered and quailed !
Here, with my manhood for token,
Here to grim Fate I give lithe;
I, too, am beaten and broken,
Lying, the swarth of the scythe !
We, to the world’s scornful seeming,
Crouch, an incongruous horde—
Fighters enmeshed in their dreaming
Dreamers who girt on the sword,
Weaklings with god-like ambitions,
Heroes who learned to succumb,
Poets a-swoon in their visions,
Singers with ecstacy dumb !
Failed ! So we oast off our burden,
Done with our dreads and our fears
These we have worn for our guerdon,
Pity and tears—woman’s tearß I
You who know never a stumble
Dwell from a woman apart;
Only the bent and the humble
Learn the low door to her heart!
You who have conquered, believing
That you have done with the fray,
Is yours a Heav’n worth achieving ;
Stormed in the space of a day ?
Here is this world we must live in ;
What else is left us to gain ?
More is it worth to have striven
Than in the end to attain 1
Though we have fallen and yielded,
This we have given to you ;
Still stands the work we have build -d,
Plain is the prize to your view !
We have had sight of the glory,
Pointed it clear to the blind;
Yours is the conquerors’ story—
Ours is the vision you find !
You who your fate overmaster,
Take back the scorn that you fling!
Though we be serfs of disaster,
Here in our ranks is a King !
He in defeat shall assail your
Victories, proving them loss ;
He who fought only for failure,
He who achieved—on a cross !
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
A patriarch among pubs. The Black Horse
Hotel at Richmond (N.S.W\) has been licensed
continuously since 1819. Before the railway
days, it was known as The Honeymoon Inn;
newly-married couples drove thither from Sydney.
The visitors’ book, still in existence, contains the
signatures of many early settlers.
The Fall of Captain Davids.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the trading-station, people had ceased to talk
of the inexplicable disappearance of the Agent’s
daughter—they had almost forgotten it. On his
last visit there, Captain Davids, of the schooner
Porpoise, shipped a new hard a young, clean-
faced fellow, who had left a just-departed
schooner.
♦ m ■ * *
The storm had subsided, and the Porpoise was
many a league out of her course. Straight ahead
lay land ; Captain Davids, conning it through his
glasses, knew it as a small island, uninhabited and
out of the way of ships. Forward, the crew were
gathered, gloomily discussing something—all but
Harrison. He lay below in his hammock with a
broken skull—dead. An hour ago he had hesi-
tated at an’order, and the Captain had enforced
his authority with a belaying-pin. And Harrison
had been as much liked by the crew as the Cap-
tain was hated.
The schooner approached the island—being
short of water—and let go an anchor about a
quarter of a mile from the white fringe of sand
which belted the little bay. Beyond towered a
background of scrub and tall trees. The Captain,
having seen the craft anchored, was stepping
towards his cabin, when there Was a hurried
stamping around him, a rush as of sudden wind,
and the crew had a prisoner.
“ What does this mean ? ” he demanded, as soon'
as he could speak.
Mortlock, the boatswain, answered :
“On’y this: We ’ad a meetin’, we’ad ; an’we
decided as ’ow this ’ere belayin’-pin business’as
got to stop. Now, ’Arrison was a mate o’ mine,
an’ I’m a-goin’ to see fair done to him. Ain’t I,
boys?”
A shout of approval from the crew. Black-
bearded. Pearson loudly cried “Ay, ay!” and
Carpenter the cook distorted his wizened face
into a grin. Only Morrel was silent—and he did
not count.
Strengthened by this unanimity, Mortlock
straightened his back :
“ Now, the lads thinks as ’ow I’d be a fust-rate,
captain. I’m as good a seaman as ever you was,
an’ I’m as kind as a kitten. At all events I never
broke a man’s head with a belayin’-pin. So the
on’y thing is, what are we a-goin’ to do with you!”
“ Shoot him, I think,” purred Pearson, and
Carpenter grinned like a cat. The crew laughed
—all but Morrel.
“ Not yet,” laughed Mortlock. “ We’re a-goin’
to make a reg’lar stage-play o’ this ’ere. We’ll go
ashore—all but two, an’ them two ’ll play a game
o’ cards. . If one wins, we shoot him at once ; if
t’other wins, we leave him ’ere to take his chance,
while we ups anchor and away. Let’s see. who’ll
play ? ”
He looked around. Pearson’s black face was
prominent beside Carpenter’s insignificant one.
“Right,” Mortlock went on. “Pearson and
Carpenter. If you win, Pearson, you show a red
lantern through the cabin port; if you win, Car-
penter, show a white one—at midnight neat. You
won’t know what the signals mean, so your con-
sciences can be easy. Besides, you can’t play any
dodge on us. What do you say, boys?”
The crew testified with many oaths their ap-
proval of the scheme—but Morrel edged up olose
to the captain as if in sympathy.
• » • •
The night was ohill and dark and the crew
gathered round the fire in an opening of the scrub.
In the glare the captain’s face was ghastly
—from fear and the pain of his rope-bound limbs.
“ What are you going to do with me ? ” The
voice was quavering.
“ Shoot you, mebbe,” laughed Mortlock. “ If
Pearson shows the red glim, you’re done for ; if
he flashes the white, you can stay ’ere and populate
the island. King o’ the Cannibal Islands, on’y
there ain’t no cannibals.”
“Why should you murder me ! ” The captain’s
face was twitching with fear.
“ Because, blast you ! ” retorted Mortlock, “ you
murdered ’Arrison, an’ you’d murder me if you
got a chance. That’s why ! Because you’re a
our, a drunken blackguard as never spared man or
woman in your life. That’s why.”
It was delicious to hear the scoundrelly boat-
swain rolling forth these charges with the air of
an honest man. The captain would have spoken,
but—-
“ An’ Morrel’s the one to shoot you. He’s been
beside you all night, an’ he’s on’y itchin’ to get at
you. Ain’t you, Morrel?”
But Morrel's smooth face was pale as the Cap-
tain’s. He retreated a step as if the idea fright-
ened him, and said : “ Not I—nor you, Mortlock.
We are not a set of murderers, are we ? As for
shooting—l’d sooner shoot myself, or you.”
(( Mortlock gasped with surprise; then he laughed.
“ You're a coward, Morrel, an’ you like him, even
after he nearly brained you with the boat-hook
t’other day, d’ye remember? But you’re a new
hand, an’ you don’t know him so well as us.” •
“At least,” the captain broke in, “if you kill
me you won’t steal my share of the cargo ! You’ll
give it to someone for me ? ”
“Who?”
“ My wife ?
“ Wife ! ” Mortlock interrupted ; “ which one?
You’ve got half-a-score, to my knowledge. Not
the Agent’s daughter, eh ? She drowned herself,
didn’t she Cap ?—so they said. You was married
then, but she didn't know it. Which one, Cap? ”
But the Captain sulked. No one noticed
Morrel’s agitation at the mention of the Captain’s
wife—no one saw his eyes flash and his frame
tremble as with fierce passion. And no one
noticed that shortly afterwards he slipped from
the crowd and raced to the edge of the scrub over-
looking the bay.
* * ' •
Morrel lay for an hour among the bushes on
the edge of the sand. Then suddenly he sat up
and cursed his folly in delaying. The moon was
rising—already her light was streaming across the
unbroken grey of the beach. The ship was quiet
as if deserted. Morrel —he could see plainly now
in the moonlight—could not discern a single
moving shadow among the still shadows of masts
and rigging. He concluded that the two men
were sitting in the Captain’s cabin. He arose
and strode swiftly across the sand to the
sea’s edge—and again cursed his foolishness.
His wits were wool-gathering to-night ! He
did what he should have done in the shadow ;
stripped himself down to a loose singlet and pair
of cotton drawers, from which he tore the legs
below the knees. In a piece of the spare material
he wrapped his revolver, tore the remainder into
strips and firmly bound the weapon on top of his
head, passing the bandages round under his chin.
Then he strode out into breast-deep water and—
keeping his head dry as possible—swam silently
towards the schooner. J
* • • •
In the cabin sat Pearson and Carpenter, with a
pack of cards between them. Two lighted lamps
stood on the table—a red one and a white. On
the bulkhead a clock showed a quarter to 12.
Suddenly, Carpenter stopped in a shuffle and
stared at the black-bearded face before him.
“ What’s that? ” he said, nervously.
“ Nothing,” said Pearson.
The game went on again.
“ And that! ” Carpenter’s face paled slightly.
“ Cur ! ” growled Pearson. “ The wind Go
on ! ”
It sounded for all the world as if a dog were
trotting across the deck, but Carpenter knew
there was no dog. His hands trembled so that he
could scarcely hold the cards. Suddenly Pearson
swore horribly.
“ You’ve won, Carpenter; the white glim it is
whatever that means.”
“ Did you hear?” gasped Carpenter, white.
The black face opposite was frowning, and it
increased Carpenter’s terror. The dog was out-
side the door now. Carpenter found himself
listening for a scratching at the panels.
“ The wind,” said Pearson.
“Wind be damned ! ” Carpenter leapt to his
feet, wheeled—and sat down in a heap.
The door had opened silently, and a semi-nude
man stood against the outer darkness. The man
was streaming wet but the revolver was
dry. And the revolver covered Pearson’s head.
Carpenter was a mere heap of helpless flesh-
fright had paralysed him.
Pearson sat still, only a twitching of the black
beard telling of inward passion.
“What o’olock is it?” The man at the door
never moved his eye for a second from Pearson’s
face. The latter leisurely looked at the clock.
“ Just four minutes to twelve.”
“Take that lamp, then. No, not that! The
red one—the red one, I tell you. ”
Pearson lifted the lamp. “ Well, what now?”
Carpenter had not stirred : Morrel seemed to
think him unworthy of notice. For a space there
was silence.
“ What time is it ? ”
“ Twelve o’clock.” Pearson laughed softly.
“ Then open the port, and show that light.”
Pearson glanced momentarily at the set face,
smiled, opened the port, and the red rays shot
through the night.
Carpenter, looking surreptitiously from beneath
his brows, saw Morrel step quickly forward, and
he could have sworn that the hand that laid the
revolver upon the table trembled violently. Also
that Morrel’s face was relaxed and white, and that
his hands went forward as if to tear the lamp
from Pearson’s grasp. Surprise undid the work
of fear, and Carpenter became alert: his hand
went out, and quietly gripped the revolver.
In the next second three things happened:
Morrel sprang forward, with a shrill cry, and
wrenohed the lamp from Pearson’s hand; two
pistol-shots—clear and distinct —rang from the
shore, and Carpenter levelled the revolver at
Morrel and fired.
Pearson’s face was calm as ever as he turned
with,.“ Well done, Carpenter ! ” He stooped to
examine the still form at his feet; but his face was
pale as Carpenter’s when he looked up again.
“ Carpenter ” —and he laughed idiotically—“ he
was— a woman / ”
